15.3.1  Different Classes of Cyanotoxins 
and Their Effects
15.3.1.1  Hepatotoxins
Microcystins are cyclic heptapeptides with the structure 
cyclo-(D-Ala1-X2-D-MeAsp3-Z4-Adda5-D-Glu6-Mdha7) 
in which X and Z represent variable L-amino acids. 
D-MeAsp is D-erythro-ß-methylaspartic acid, Mdha is 
N-methyldehydroalanine, and Adda is (2S, 3S, 4E, 8S, 9S)-3-
amino-9-methoxy-2,6,8-trimethyl-10-phenyldeca-4,6-dienoic 
acid.119 Around 256 microcystins of varying toxicity have 
been reported.120 Nodularins are closely related pentapeptides 
with the structure cyclo-(D-MeAsp1-L-Arg2-Adda3-D-Glu4-
Mdhb5), in which Mdhb is 2-(methylamino)-2-dehydrobutyric 
acid.119 Ten nodularin variants have been reported.120 Due 
to their similar structures, microcystins and nodularins are 
believed to have similar mechanisms of toxicity. Microcystins 
are produced by several genera of cyanobacteria, including 
Dolichospermum, Microcystis, Nostoc, Phormidium, and 
Planktothrix, while production of nodularins is limited to 
Nodularia and Iningainema species.
From a public health perspective, microcystins are the 
most problematic freshwater cyanobacterial toxins occurring 
in drinking water and irrigation reservoirs worldwide, while 
nodularins are usually encountered in brackish water bodies 
such as the Baltic Sea. In addition to direct exposure via con-
taminated water, indirect exposure to these hepatotoxins may 
occur via the consumption of contaminated seafood, milk, 
meat, plants, and health-food supplements.
The most famous case of microcystin poisoning occurred in 
Caruaru, Brazil where 100 of 131 patients undergoing hemo-
dialysis developed symptoms of hepatotoxicity after being 
exposed to contaminated dialysis solution. Sixty patients died 
from hepatotoxic effects or indirectly from complications such 
as sepsis, GI bleeding, and cardiovascular disturbances.121
The intraperitoneal LD50 of microcystin-LR (one of the most 
common variants) in mice is ~36–122 µg/kg, the oral LD50 is 
~5 mg/kg, and the inhalation toxicity is ~43 µg/kg.122 The val-
ues are similar for nodularin, with reported intraperitoneal 
LD50 in mice ranging from ~30 to 70 µg/kg.123
Most microcystin variants do not readily cross cell mem-
branes and hence do not enter most tissue types. After inges-
tion, microcystin is transported across the ileum into the blood 
via bile acid transporters. It is actively taken up by hepatocytes 
and becomes concentrated in the liver. Significant amounts 
of the toxin may also be localized to the intestine and kid-
neys.124 Microcystin exerts its toxicity by binding covalently 
to eukaryotic serine/threonine protein phosphatases (primar-
ily PP1 and 2A) inhibiting phosphorylation and causing cyto-
skeletal breakdown in hepatocytes.125 This leads to loss of cell 
morphology and eventual tissue necrosis. The mode of action 
of nodularin is similar; however, it does not irreversibly bind 
protein phosphatases.
Symptoms of acute microcystin poisoning include diarrhea, 
vomiting, piloerection, weakness, and pallor.126 In severe cases, 
haemorrhage occurs in the liver due to the damage of sinusoi-
dal capillaries.119 There is emerging evidence that microcys-
tin is both genotoxic and carcinogenic. Chronic exposure to 
low concentrations is highly detrimental to the liver, causing 
lesions and possibly promoting hepatocellular carcinoma.126 
Most of the toxin is rapidly excreted via the urine within 
12 hours, while the remaining toxin is slowly excreted via the 
fecal route within a few weeks.127 The glutathione pathway 
in the liver plays a major role in the detoxification process.128 
There is no standard method of treatment for microcystin or 
nodularin poisoning. Liver damage is rapid and irreversible. 
However, administration of melatonin may protect against oxi-
dative damage.129
Cylindrospermopsin is a polyketide-derived alkaloid featur-
ing a sulfated tricyclic guanidine moiety bridged to a hydroxy-
methyluracil ring. Five variants have been identified.120 While 
it is often grouped with the hepatotoxins, it also has nephro-
toxic and general cytotoxic effects. Cylindrospermopsin is 
produced by Anabaena, Aphanizomenon, Dolichospermum, 
Lyngbya, Oscillatoria, and Raphidiopsis species. The toxin is 
most problematic in tropical regions.
The first recorded incident of cylindrospermopsin poison-
ing occurred on Palm Island, Queensland, Australia in 1979, 
where 138 people suffered hepato-enteritis with symptoms of 
bloody diarrhea and urine after drinking from the local res-
ervoir. The outbreak was linked to a Raphidiopsis (formerly 
Cylindrospermopsis) bloom, and cylindrospermopsin was 
identified as the causative agent. All patients recovered within 
4–26 days.130
Like microcystins and nodularins, cylindrospermopsins 
enter cells via the bile acid transport system. However, symp-
toms are more systemic and include damage to the liver, kid-
neys, lungs, thymus, spleen, adrenal glands, intestinal tract, 
the immune system, heart, and blood vessels.130 On a molecu-
lar level, they have been shown to inhibit protein synthesis, 
as well as the production of glutathione, progesterone, and 
uridine monophosphate.120 They may also damage DNA and 
initiate tumor development.131 There is evidence that cylindro-
spermopsin is converted into a more cytotoxic and genotoxic 
form by cytochrome P450.132
The intraperitoneal LD50 of cylindrospermopsin in mice 
is ~2.1 mg/kg at 24 hours and ~0.2 mg/kg at 5–6 days.133 The 
oral LD50 of cylindrospermopsin is estimated to be ~4.4–6.9 
mg/kg.134 Most of the toxin is excreted in the urine and feces 
within 12 hours of exposure. Like microcystin, chronic low 
doses may be carcinogenic.131
15.3.1.2  Neurotoxic Alkaloids
Saxitoxin and its analogs, also known as paralytic shellfish 
toxins, are produced by filamentous cyanobacteria of the gen-
era Aphanizomenon, Cylindrospermopsis, Dolichospermum, 
Lyngbya, Planktothrix, and Raphidiopsis, as well as marine 
dinoflagellates (eukaryotic microalgae). Over 57 different 
structural analogs have been described with varying toxic-
ity. All share the same tricyclic 3,4-propinoperhydropurine 
core but differ by way of functional side groups.135 Saxitoxin 
is one of the most potent neurotoxins known and is listed in 
Schedule 1 of the Chemical Weapons Convention. The intra-
peritoneal LD50 for saxitoxin in mice is ~10 µg/kg, while the 
oral LD50 is ~263 µg/kg.136 The estimated lethal oral dose in 
humans is ~1–4 mg.137 The main route of poisoning is via the

770
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
consumption of contaminated shellfish, which feed on toxic 
cyanobacteria and dinoflagellates but are not themselves 
affected by the toxin.
Saxitoxin is a potent inhibitor of voltage-gated sodium 
(NaV) channels in peripheral neurons and skeletal muscle cells. 
Acute poisoning leads to sensory disturbances and paraly-
sis. Symptoms usually present within 30 minutes to 2 hours 
of exposure. Early and mild symptoms include tingling and 
numbness of the lips, nausea, vomiting, and diarrhea. In severe 
cases, these are followed by loss of motor control, drowsiness 
and incoherence, complete flaccid paralysis, and cardiopul-
monary arrest. There is no specific antidote for saxitoxin poi-
soning and treatment is generally supportive. Gastric lavage 
and administration of activated charcoal or dilute bicarbonate 
solution may be useful for decontaminating the GIT follow-
ing a recent meal of contaminated shellfish. In severe cases, 
mechanical ventilation may be necessary. The toxin is cleared 
by the kidney in under 24 hours and patients usually make a 
complete recovery within a few days to a few weeks.138
Anatoxin-a, formerly known as “very fast death factor”, is a 
secondary bicyclic amine (2-acetyl-9-azabicyclo[4.2.1]non-2-en) 
that is typically produced by Aphanizomenon, Cylindrospermum, 
Dolichospermum, Kamptonema (Oscillatoria), Microcystis, 
Nostoc, Phormidium, Planktothrix, and Raphidiopsis strains. 
The toxin and its various analogs (ten in total120) are agonists 
of muscular and neuronal nAChRs; upon binding, they induce 
opening of the receptor channel and depolarization of the cell 
membrane, causing postsynaptic neuromuscular blockade.139 
The main route of poisoning is via the consumption of con-
taminated water. Poisoning leads to muscle overstimulation and 
symptoms include tingling in fingers and toes, abdominal pain, 
vomiting, diarrhea, fatigue and weakness, ataxic gait, wheezing, 
and gasping for breath. In severe cases, this can progress to con-
vulsions, paralysis, and asphyxiation. Death by respiratory arrest 
may occur within a few minutes to a few hours, depending upon 
the dosage.
In mice, anatoxin-a poisoning impairs blood pressure, heart 
rate, and gas exchange, causing hypoxia, respiratory arrest, and 
severe acidosis.140 The intraperitoneal LD50 of anatoxin-a in 
mice is ~260-315 µg/kg, while the oral LD50 is >5,000 µg/kg.119 
There is no specific antidote for anatoxin-a. Artificial respira-
tion may be of benefit along with general supportive care. No 
long-term effects of anatoxin-a have been reported.
Guanitoxin (formerly anatoxin-a(S)) is an unusual reactive 
organophosphate 
((S)-5-((dimethylamino)methyl)-2-imino-
imidazolidin-1-yl methyl hydrogen phosphate) produced by 
certain Dolichospermum and Sphaerospermopsis strains, and 
it is less frequently encountered than most other cyanotox-
ins.141 Guanitoxin has a similar structure and mechanism of 
action to organophosphate and carbamate insecticides. It irre-
versibly inhibits acetylcholinesterase142 leading to a build-up 
of acetylcholine in the peripheral nervous system (PNS) and 
overstimulation of muscles. The main route of poisoning is via 
the consumption of contaminated water. Symptoms include 
diarrhea, fasciculation, convulsions, weakness, incontinence, 
and respiratory failure. Other characteristic symptoms include 
excessive salivation and chromodacryorrhea (bloody tears).143 
The intraperitoneal LD50 of guanitoxin in mice is ~40–60 
µg/kg. Treatment with atropine, a competitive antagonist of 
muscarinic AChRs, can alleviate parasympathomimetic signs 
of toxicity in mice but does not prevent convulsions or respira-
tory arrest.143
15.3.1.3  Dermatoxins
Lyngbyatoxins are dermatoxic alkaloids comprising an indol-
actam ring attached to a linalyl side group.144 Seven analogs 
have been identified.145 They are produced by benthic fila-
mentous marine genera such as Lyngbya (“mermaid’s hair”), 
Oscillatoria, and Schizothrix and are usually encountered in 
estuarine and coastal waters in tropical and subtropical cli-
mate zones. Contact with lyngbyatoxins (e.g., during water 
sports) causes severe dermatitis, with symptoms of erythema 
and inflammation, itching, and burning sensations, followed 
by blistering and deep desquamation within 12 hours of con-
tact and lasting several days. Eye and respiratory irritation may 
also occur. Lyngbyatoxins have been shown to induce protein 
kinase C and promote tumors.146. They are also highly toxic if 
accidentally ingested (e.g., along with edible seaweed) causing 
bleeding of the small intestine. Experiments in rodents suggest 
an intraperitoneal LD100 of ~250 µg/kg.147
Aplysiatoxins are dermatoxic polyketide-derived alkaloids. 
Structurally, they may be described as bislactones of 3,4-dihy-
droxyvaleric acid and 4,6,6,10,12-pentamethyl-3,7,9,11,15-
tetraoxy-15-phenylpentadecanoic acid. They are produced by 
Lyngbya species. Ten analogs have been identified.120 They 
have a similar mode of action and symptoms to lyngbyatoxin. 
Aplysiatoxin and debromo-aplysiatoxin were also found to be 
potent tumor promoters in mouse skin cells.148 The LD50 of 
debromoaplysiatoxin is ~107–117 µg/kg, while aplysiatoxin 
has an LD50 of ~300 µg/kg.149
15.3.1.4  Other Cyanobacteria Toxins
In addition to the major families of cyanotoxins mentioned 
above, several other compounds produced by cyanobacte-
ria (e.g., antillatoxins, jamaicamides, kalkitoxins, majus-
culamides, hectochlorins, curacins, and others) may pose a 
threat to human health. Few toxicological studies have been 
performed on these compounds, and very little is known 
about their acute or chronic effects. Of emerging concern, is 
the non-proteinogenic amino acid, β-methylamino-L-alanine 
(BMAA), a neurotoxin produced by a variety of cyanobacteria, 
which may be a contributing factor in the development of neu-
rodegenerative diseases, including amyotrophic lateral sclero-
sis, Alzheimer disease, and Parkinson-dementia-complex.150 
In addition to agonizing both NMDA and AMPA glutamate 
receptors in motor neurons, BMAA induces oxidative stress 
and depletion of glutathione. As a non-proteinogenic amino 
acid, BMAA may also cause intraneuronal protein misfolding 
associated with neurodegeneration.150
15.3.2  Public Health Significance
Cyanobacteria in natural and controlled water bodies present 
a serious public health issue. Toxic cyanobacteria have been 
detected on every continent, including Antarctica, and they

771
Plant and Animal Toxins
are a frequent occurrence in marine and coastal waters around 
the globe.145 Thousands of human poisonings resulting in mor-
bidity and mortality have been documented since the 1930s, 
and due to the mild and self-limiting nature of many cases, 
it is likely that thousands more have gone unreported. Most 
documented poisonings have occurred in North and Central 
America (39%), followed by Europe (20%), Australia and New 
Zealand (15%), Africa (11%), Asia (8%), and South America 
(8%).149
Direct exposure to cyanotoxin-contaminated drinking water 
or hemodialysis fluids is responsible for approximately half of 
all reported human intoxications, while the remaining cases 
are the result of recreational exposure. Incidents from drink-
ing water have the potential to poison more individuals simul-
taneously and have resulted in several mass poisonings as 
mentioned above. However, due to widespread cyanobacterial 
monitoring regimes by water utilities, such incidents tend to be 
rare. Conversely, recreational exposures typically only affect 
a small number of individuals simultaneously but occur more 
frequently as monitoring of recreational waterbodies tends to 
be less stringent and warnings not to access them are some-
times ignored.
Over the years, cases of human intoxication from drink-
ing water have been reported from the U.S., Zimbabwe, 
the Philippines, Australia, Brazil, and Sweden, while rec-
reational exposures have been reported from the U.S., 
Canada, Argentina, the U.K., the Netherlands, Australia, 
and Finland.149,151 Fatalities, while rare, have been mostly 
associated with microcystins. Saxitoxin fatalities have been 
recorded, but these were associated with marine dinoflagellate 
blooms, not cyanobacteria.
While most patients survive intoxication, long-term mor-
bidity may result due to liver injury or other tissue damage. 
Chronic exposure to low levels can also pose a significant 
threat to public health. Of particular concern are cyanotoxins 
that have been shown to promote tumors, such as microcys-
tins, cylindrospermopsins, lyngbyatoxins, and aplysiatoxins, 
or cause neurodegeneration, such as BMAA.
Children are more vulnerable to cyanotoxins than adults 
because they drink a higher volume of water relative to their 
body weight. Individuals with pre-existing tissue damage from 
injury, exposure to other toxins or diseases (particularly hepa-
titis and liver cirrhosis) may also be at increased risk.119
15.3.3  Prevention and Control
To reduce the incidence, severity, and impact of toxic cyano-
bacterial blooms, it is imperative for government and industry 
bodies to implement a variety of management strategies at a 
national, state, and community level. Minimizing eutrophi-
cation from agricultural, urban, and industrial runoff rich in 
phosphates, nitrates, and other nutrients is particularly impor-
tant for preventing cyanobacterial overgrowth in populated 
areas. However, it is important to remember that blooms are 
a natural phenomenon that also occur in “pristine” waterbod-
ies. Thus, regular monitoring of bloom-prone areas, coupled 
with the temporary closure of reservoirs and fisheries during 
bloom events, is also paramount. As exposure to cyanotoxins 
often occurs during recreational activities, such as swimming, 
or through the consumption of seafood harvested from the 
wild, public awareness campaigns play an important role in 
protecting communities. Finally, medical professionals must 
be appropriately trained to recognize symptoms of cyanotoxin 
poisoning so they may respond quickly and appropriately to 
such incidents.
Paramount in managing cyanobacterial blooms and their 
impact is the identification and monitoring of potentially toxic 
species as well as the toxins produced. Because some toxins 
are produced by multiple genera and most genera contain 
morphologically indistinguishable toxic and nontoxic strains, 
microscopic evaluation of environmental samples is of limited 
diagnostic value. For many years, analytical chemical testing, 
for example, by high-pressure liquid chromatography with 
online mass spectral analysis, has been the “gold-standard” 
for monitoring cyanobacterial toxins in water. However, such 
methods require expensive equipment and specially trained 
technicians to process samples and interpret the results. 
Moreover, many toxins share the same mass and may thus be 
difficult to distinguish. More recently, enzyme-linked immu-
nosorbent assay (ELISA) test kits have become available for 
the major families of toxins (anatoxins, cylindrospermopsin, 
microcystins, and saxitoxins). These tests are rapid and can be 
performed in the field, with little specialist training. The main 
drawback to these is that due to the hundreds of structural 
variants of cyanotoxins that exist in nature, some may not be 
detected as well as others, if at all. Molecular tests that detect 
cyanotoxin biosynthesis genes are increasing in availability 
and popularity due to their sensitivity, rapidity, and potential 
to provide “early warnings” for potentially toxic bloom events. 
However, these tests are not useful for measuring actual toxin 
levels. Further, ELISA and molecular tests do not provide 
structural information about the toxin variants that may be 
present in a given sample, which is a significant shortcom-
ing as different variants have different toxicities. Nonetheless, 
these new technologies are highly sensitive, require minimal 
sample processing, and offer a simple and less expensive alter-
native to existing analytical techniques.
15.4  Mushroom Poisoning
Mushrooms, the ephemeral fruiting bodies of often complex 
fungal organisms, are both commonly encountered and com-
monly eaten, with a long history of wild mushroom foraging 
and consumption. If every mushroom was dangerously poi-
sonous, such historically ingrained human behavior would not 
have developed. However, to some extent, all mushrooms are 
poisonous or at least have the capacity to cause GI upset. It is 
just a matter of dose. Even mushrooms that are not technically 
“poisonous” may cause GI upset if eaten in sufficient quantity, 
or without sufficient preparation, due to the indigestible nature 
of at least some parts of the mushrooms. Poor post-harvest 
storage may also render mushrooms indigestible or toxic.
Identifying mushrooms can be fraught with difficulty. 
Those who forage for, and consume, wild mushrooms can be 
divided into three groups; those who have not yet had detect-
able poisoning, those who have been poisoned but survived, 
and those for whom the poisoning was a terminal event. There

772
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
are a number of mushroom species capable of causing severe 
or lethal poisoning and the mode of death can be both drawn 
out and unpleasant, with no guarantee that modern medical 
care can save the patient. Even some well-known “edible” spe-
cies have caused severe, in some cases lethal, poisoning. For 
these reasons, the best medical advice is “don’t consume wild 
foraged mushrooms”.
Many people will ignore this sage advice, and thus clini-
cians are faced with suspected or confirmed mushroom inges-
tion, sometimes with manifest poisoning. One valid approach 
to managing these patients is to try to identify any proffered 
mushrooms and correlate this with known risks from a given 
mushroom species. Given the problems inherent in mushroom 
identification, a process beyond the scope of this chapter, such 
a management approach may prove problematic. An alterna-
tive is to diagnose based on clinical presentation, which relies 
on knowledge of the various types of mushroom poisoning 
currently documented. This chapter will focus on this latter 
approach, by listing each type of currently recognized mush-
room poisoning, key diagnostic features and management 
approaches, and illustrative causative species.
A new clinical classification of mushroom poisoning was 
recently developed, and this classification (summarized in 
Table 15.2) will be used in this chapter.152 However, it should 
not be assumed that all possible forms of mushroom poison-
ing have now been reported. New poisoning syndromes may 
emerge, and clinicians should be prepared to consider such 
new syndromes when clinical findings fail to match known 
syndromes and seek expert identification of culprit mush-
rooms in such cases, so that reliable reporting may ensue.
15.4.1  Group 1—Cytotoxic Mushroom Poisoning
This group covers those types of poisoning where specific 
organs are primarily affected, either the liver or kidneys.
15.4.1.1  Group 1A—Primary Hepatotoxicity
This type of poisoning classically involves amatoxins and 
related heat-stable cytotoxic/hepatotoxic toxins, found in 
several mushroom genera and species, particularly Amanita 
phalloides, A. virosa, A. verna, A. exitalis, A. fuliginea, 
A. bisporigera, A. brunnescens, A. elliptosperma, A. magniv-
ellaris, A. ocreata, Lepiota helveola, L. brunneoincarnata, L. 
subincarnata, L. brunneolilacea, L. castanea, Macrolepiota 
neomastoidea, Galerina marginata, G. sulciceps, G. autum-
nalis, and Conocybe filaris.152 These cyclopeptide toxins 
inhibit RNA polymerases selectively, disrupting messenger 
and micro RNA synthesis, resulting in failure of multiple cel-
lular systems and cell death, particularly hepatocytes. The 
most hepatotoxic of these cyclopeptides is α-amanitin with an 
estimated median lethal dose of 0.1 mg/kg, equating to a lethal 
dose in adults of about 7 mg, or just one A. phalloides mush-
room.153 Cooking does not deactivate the toxins. Clinically, 
poisoning classically manifests late at >6 hours, and often 
>12 hours, post-ingestion with the onset of severe GI effects, 
including vomiting, abdominal pain, and diarrhea, which can 
lead to dehydration, particularly in children. The GI phase can 
last up to 36 hours, followed by apparent improvement for a 
brief period of up to a day or so, then the development of pro-
gressive hepatotoxicity and secondary bleeding, acute kidney 
injury (AKI), multi-organ failure, terminating in fulminant 
liver failure and death, in severe cases, about 7 days post-inges-
tion. Diagnosis is based on the classic history of late-develop-
ing severe GI effects followed by recovery, then liver failure, 
with abnormal liver function tests (LFTs) and coagulation tests 
(International Normalised Ratio (INR) etc.). Amatoxins can 
be detected as late as 6 days post-ingestion, at least in bile, but 
this is not a standard medical laboratory test.154 It is important 
to obtain early baseline tests, before major poisoning effects 
are evident, particularly covering liver function, renal function 
coagulation, basic hematology, and electrolytes. Treatment is 
controversial, but with a common element of early detection 
and IV rehydration combined with attempted hepato-protec-
tion using silibinin/silymarin (widely advocated and used, 
particularly in Europe; 20–40 mg/kg/day in divided doses), 
or high-dose penicillin G (300,000 to 1 million units/kg/day), 
and possibly N-acetylcysteine and cimetidine155–158. None of 
these treatments have undergone rigorous assessment in large 
randomized control trials. Rifampicin has been advocated in 
Australia based on limited experimental evidence,159,160 but 
without clinical experience or trials of clinical effectiveness in 
amatoxin poisoning, though it is a well-established medication 
for some other diseases. An experimental model using mice 
demonstrated that a combination of polymyxin B plus methyl-
prednisolone provided substantial protection against amatoxin 
hepatic and renal toxicity, but this has not been confirmed in 
human trials.161 Early GIT decontamination has been advo-
cated if <4 hours post-ingestion, but thereafter only activated 
charcoal is advised. Molecular absorbent recirculating system 
has been advocated to remove circulating toxins, but plasma-
pheresis and hemoperfusion have been disappointing. In the 
liver failure stage, IV glucose has been proposed and, if active 
bleeding, fresh frozen plasma (FFP) transfusion. Use of FFP 
before active bleeding becomes apparent has been advocated, 
once the prothrombin time dips below 20%. Infection can 
present major problems and prophylactic antibiotics have been 
advocated. If secondary renal failure develops, hemodialysis 
may be appropriate. In severe cases, only liver transplantation 
can save life but is hazardous and sometimes impossible in 
such severe fulminant liver failure. Some advocate for pre-
emptive liver transplantation before fulminant liver failure 
and hepatic coma develop, to improve outcomes. Such early 
transplantation may utilize partial transplantation only (single 
lobe), thus potentially allowing recovery of damaged liver and 
later removal of the transplant. Deaths from amatoxin poison-
ing remain the most common cause of mushroom ingestion 
fatality, even in countries with advanced health facilities.
15.4.1.2  Group 1B—Early Primary Nephrotoxicity
Caused by mushrooms containing aminohexadienoic acid 
(AHDA) and/or possibly other toxins, including several 
Amanita species (A. smithiana, A. pseudoporphyria, A. 
proxima, possibly A. neoovoidea, and others),152,162,163 and 
presenting clinically with initial “pre-renal” early symptoms 
(30 minutes to 6+ hours post-ingestion; nausea, vomiting,

diarrhea, abdominal pain, headache, weakness, fatigue, myal-

2–5 days post-ingestion. Renal failure, often requiring dialy-
sis, can last from 9 to 180 days, but with the eventual return 
of renal function. There can be secondary evidence of mild 
hepatotoxicity, potentially causing confusion with amatoxin 
poisoning. Treatment consists of supportive and symptom-
atic care as no antidote is available. If presentation is early, 
<6 hours post-ingestion, GIT decontamination might be practi-
cal with oral activated charcoal. Forced diuresis, including the 
use of furosemide, may be associated with poorer outcomes. 
Hemoperfusion is unlikely to be advantageous because of the 
early irreversible fixation of toxins to renal tissue. Once renal 
failure is established, dialysis is required until recovery, with 
few cases requiring renal transplantation.
15.4.1.3  Group 1C—Delayed Primary 
Nephrotoxicity
Associated with mushrooms containing orellanine (princi-
pally Cortinarius orellanus, C. rubellus, C. orellanosus, and 
C. armillatus)152,164 and presenting clinically, after a symptom-
free latent period, with initial “pre-renal” symptoms (12 hours 
to 14 days post-ingestion, mean 3 days; nausea, vomiting, diar-
rhea, anorexia, headaches, chills, drowsiness, sweats, rash, 
dyspnea, and lumbar pain) followed by AKI at 4–15 days post-
ingestion (mean 8.5 days), generally initial polyuria, then oli-
guria or anuria. The more rapid the onset of AKI, the more 
severe the level of renal damage, with onset more than 10 days 
usually associated with only mild AKI, while a brief latent 
period of 2–3 days is associated with severe AKI sometimes 
requiring renal transplantation for survival. Recovery from 
the AKI is often slow and chronic renal failure may ensue. 
Diagnosis can be problematic because of the delay between 
mushroom ingestion and the onset of symptoms. A specific 
test for orellanine is not widely available, so diagnosis relies on 
the history of a mushroom meal, then a symptom-free period 
of days to weeks, followed by the pre-renal symptoms, then 
the onset of AKI for which other possible causes have been 
excluded. Treatment is supportive and symptomatic as no anti-
dote is available. If the patient presents <6 hours post-inges-
tion, a rarity, then oral activated charcoal can be considered; 
otherwise decontamination is not appropriate. Forced diuresis 
and furosemide are associated with worsening renal damage 
and should be avoided. Hemoperfusion is unlikely to be ben-
eficial because the toxin is irreversibly bound to target renal 
tissue. Minimal reports are suggesting that N-acetylcysteine 
and/or selenium might be beneficial. Once renal failure is 
established, dialysis is required, potentially for an extended 
period, but recovery is usual, with few cases requiring renal 
transplantation.
15.4.2	 Group 2—Neurotoxic Mushroom Poisoning
15.4.2.1  Group 2A—Hallucinogenic Mushrooms
Mushrooms causing psychoactive effects, including hallu-
cinations as the primary effect, including several species of 
Psilocybe, Conocybe, Gymnopilus, Panaeolus, Copelandia, 
Pluteus, and possibly Stropharia. Toxins involved include 
psilocybin, psilocin, and gymnopilins. These mushrooms are 
often consumed deliberately to achieve the CNS stimulant 
effects and are sold commercially in a few countries and via 
the internet, either as whole mushrooms or as liquid extracts 
with concentrated toxins. Poisoning presents rapidly, generally 
10–30 minutes post-ingestion, with the onset of various CNS 
effects, not only including illusions, hallucinations, altered 
sense of space/time, euphoria, but also sometimes aggression 
toward self or others. Other features include anxiety, ataxia, 
pupil dilatation, nausea, vomiting, abdominal pain, paresthe-
sia, hyperreflexia, tachycardia, hypertension, cardiac arrhyth-
mias, and myocardial ischemia. After the initial stimulatory 
period subsides, the patient may be depressed, exhausted, and 
have cognitive deficits for up to 36 hours. There is a reported 
case of classic psilocybin intoxication following consumption 
of Psilocybe cubensis in a 15-year-old boy who then devel-
oped AKI not requiring dialysis, with subsequent recovery.165 
Diagnosis is usually based on a history of ingestion of mush-
rooms or their concentrated products, most often as a delib-
erate ingestion with the intention of experiencing sensory 
effects, and a typical story of symptoms consistent with psilo-
cybin-type poisoning. There are no diagnostic laboratory tests, 
but if co-ingestion of other mushrooms cannot be excluded, 
renal, hepatic, and coagulation function should be checked. 
Decontamination is not generally helpful or recommended. 
Treatment is symptomatic and supportive, preferably using a 
darkened room with family/friends in attendance to provide 
support. Anticonvulsive therapy may be required for ongoing 
seizures and diazepam may assist with the control of severe 
hallucinations. Beware rare cases with secondary myocardial 
ischemia, hyperthermia, or rhabdomyolysis and the possibility 
of co-ingestion of other mushrooms or drugs. The latter such 
as lysergic acid diethylamide and phenylcyclohexyl piperidine 
may be unacknowledged additives in purchased psilocybin-
type products.
15.4.2.2  Group 2B—Autonomic 
Toxicity Mushrooms
These mushrooms cause specific autonomic effects associ-
ated with muscarines and related toxins found in a variety of 
species (selected Inocybe spp., Clitocybe spp., Mycena spp., 
Rubinoboletus spp.). Poisoning develops rapidly 15–120 min-
utes post-ingestion with a classic triad of increased sweating, 
salivation, and lachrymation, with other effects, including 
constricted pupils, blurred vision, urgent/painful micturition, 
nasal discharge/congestion, hypotension, bradycardia, bron-
choconstriction, skin flushing, diarrhea, vomiting, and abdom-
inal pain. Severe, even lethal, poisoning is possible. Diagnosis 
is based on clinical findings, particularly the presence of the 
classic triad of increased sweating, lachrymation, and saliva-
tion. There are no specific laboratory tests. Early decontami-
nation of the GIT may be appropriate if the patient has not 
had early sustained vomiting. Treatment is symptomatic and 
supportive, including rehydration as appropriate and atropine 
in patients with moderate to severe poisoning. Glycopyrrolate 
has been suggested as an alternative to atropine.

776
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
15.4.2.3  Group 2C—CNS Neurotoxicity 
Mushrooms
Mushrooms also contain neuroexcitatory toxins, particularly 
muscimol and ibotenic acid (Amanita muscaria, A. panthe-
rina, and A. Ibotengutake). Poisoning develops rapidly, from 
3 minutes to 3 hours post-ingestion, commencing with initial 
drowsiness, then manic excitement with bizarre behavior, 
visual misperceptions, agitation, elation, disorientation, and 
confusion. Other effects include nausea, vomiting, abdominal 
pain, rash, sweating, ataxia, incoordination, dizziness, muscle 
fasciculation/tremors, hyporeflexia, coma, and convulsions. 
These effects may last up to 48 hours, followed by a further 
period of drowsiness/sleep. Post-poisoning amnesia can occur. 
There are apparent differences in poisoning between each 
reported causative species; A. muscaria is more associated 
with confusion and agitation, while A. pantherina is more 
likely to cause coma. Diagnosis is based on a history of inges-
tion and the development of symptoms consistent with this 
type of poisoning. There are no diagnostic laboratory tests. 
Treatment is supportive and directed to ensuring the patient 
neither sustains harm, nor causes harm to others, particularly 
during the manic phase, but also during the somnolent phases, 
the latter being of particular concern in severe poisoning, in 
children, and the infirm. In children, convulsions can occur 
with substantial ingestions. Because the manic phase can last 
hours and be similar to inebriation, the patient is a potential risk 
to themselves and others and may require close and confined 
observation in a controlled environment. This may necessitate 
intensive use of staff resources. Sedatives, atropine, and phy-
sostigmine have shown no benefit and should be avoided. Only 
if convulsions occur, should medication be considered. The 
likelihood of harm during the final somnolent phase appears 
to be minor, but continued observation until final awakening 
is advisable. Amnesia for the period of intoxication is com-
mon and patients may need reassurance about this. In addition, 
some patients can find their thought processes impaired for a 
prolonged period, up to 6 weeks, but longer term or permanent 
impairment is not expected. Prolonged headache, lasting sev-
eral days in some cases, is frequently seen.
15.4.2.4  Group 2D—Morel Neurologic Syndrome
A currently ill-defined syndrome occurring in a subset of peo-
ple consuming large quantities of “edible” morel mushrooms 
(Morchella spp.) with no clearly identified toxin reported. The 
time to onset of effects is unclear, but most patients experience 
GI effects, including nausea, vomiting, diarrhea, abdominal 
pain, plus neurologic effects, particularly dizziness, and ataxia. 
Other reported effects include sweating, hyperthermia/hypo-
thermia, salivation, visual disturbance, headache, paresthesia, 
muscle spasms/trismus, drowsiness, dysarthria, nystagmus, 
agitation, confusion, and convulsions. Despite the severity of 
effects, the syndrome appears to be self-limited, non-lethal, with 
spontaneous recovery. Diagnosis is based on a history of inges-
tion and subsequent symptom development. There are no diag-
nostic laboratory tests. Treatment is not well characterized, with 
few published cases available, and should be supportive, symp-
tomatic, and optimistic with spontaneous recovery expected.
15.4.3  Group 3—Myotoxic Mushroom Poisoning
15.4.3.1  Group 3A—Rapid Onset Myotoxicity
Potentially lethal poisoning can occur following ingestion of 
Russula subnigricans mushrooms, the causative toxin is con-
sidered to be cycloprop-2-ene carboxylic acid166,167. Poisoning 
onset is usually within 2 hours post-ingestion, occasionally lon-
ger, commencing with GI effects (nausea, vomiting, diarrhea) 
which in most cases resolve within 24 hours without developing 
rhabdomyolysis, but in more severe cases systemic rhabdomy-
olysis develops with other effects, including myalgia, hyper-
tension, AKI, hyperkalemia, and cardiovascular collapse. 
Diagnosis is based on the history of ingestion and subsequent 
development of characteristic clinical effects. There is no spe-
cific diagnostic laboratory test, but an otherwise unexplained 
major elevation in creatine kinase (CK) following the early 
GI phase may point to the diagnosis. Laboratory tests should 
include CK, renal function, and standard blood chemistry. 
With so few documented cases, treatment is not yet well estab-
lished. On first principles, initial supportive care is advised, 
which may include IV hydration and attention to electrolyte 
imbalance, depending on the severity of GI symptoms. CK, 
renal function, and electrolytes should be regularly assessed in 
the first 24 hours to exclude developing myolysis and second-
ary renal failure. If myolysis develops, continued supportive 
care is indicated. If secondary renal failure develops, hemodi-
alysis should be considered early, before problematic hyperka-
lemia has developed. In the event of cardiovascular collapse, 
vigorous and optimistic resuscitation should be instituted.
15.4.3.2  Group 3B—Delayed Onset Myotoxicity
This is a controversial type of poisoning as it follows consump-
tion of “edible” species, particularly Tricholoma terreum, T. 
equestre, T. auratum, and some Leccinum spp. and Boletus 
spp.167–169 The toxin(s) involved remain unclear but may be sapo-
naceolides. Poisoning presents late, 1–3 days post-ingestion of 
several meals of these mushrooms, with the onset of fatigue and 
myalgias, then muscle weakness, myoglobinuria, sometimes 
facial flushing and mild nausea, and profuse sweating. CK can 
exceed 200,000 IU/l, though renal function is usually unaffected 
and there is spontaneous resolution over about 15 days in most 
cases. A few patients progress to acute myocarditis, AKI, hyper-
thermia, hyperkalemia, and cardiac failure with a fatal outcome. 
There is no specific diagnostic test available, but a history of 
ingestion of these specific mushrooms, followed days later by 
evidence of rhabdomyolysis, without an earlier GI phase, sug-
gests the diagnosis. Laboratory tests center on CK, renal func-
tion, and general blood chemistry. The treatment of Tricholoma 
equestre–type rhabdomyolysis is evolving. All other possible 
causes of myolysis should be sought and excluded. Supportive 
care is required and recurrent checks on electrolytes, renal func-
tion, and CK are advised. Alkalinization of the urine is not of 
proven benefit. There is no known antidote. The onset of dyspnea 
at rest is a bad prognostic sign and may indicate developing acute 
myocarditis, with the potential for cardiorespiratory failure, 
secondary renal failure and hyperkalemia, and a fatal outcome. 
Successful management of this problem is not yet established.

777
Plant and Animal Toxins
15.4.4  Group 4—Metabolic, Endocrine, and 
Related Toxicity Mushroom Poisoning
15.4.4.1  Group 4A—GABA-Blocking 
Mushroom Poisoning
This form of potentially lethal poisoning causes complex 
metabolic effects secondary to gyrometrins which are 
metabolized to GABA-blocking toxins that inhibit pyri-
doxine synthesis, reducing GABA synthesis, and causing 
glutathione depletion170–172. Implicated mushrooms include 
Gyromitra esculenta, G. ambigua, G. gigas, G. infula, and 
Cudonia circinans. Poisoning presents 5–12+ hours post-
ingestion with GI effects (nausea, vomiting, abdominal pain, 
bloating, bloody diarrhea, and dehydration), followed, in 
more severe cases, with a variety of effects (vertigo, sweat-
ing, diplopia, headache, dysarthria, incoordination, ataxia, 
hemolysis, hypoglycemia, methemoglobinemia, convulsions, 
and coma) and multi-organ damage, including liver dam-
age/failure. An unfavorable prognosis may be indicated by 
encephalopathy, AKI, lactic acidosis, and <8 hours between 
ingestion and development of GI symptoms.173 Diagnosis is 
based on a history of ingestion and subsequent onset of typi-
cal clinical effects. There are no diagnostic laboratory tests 
available; serial assessment of liver & renal function, CK, 
blood chemistry, and hematology are advised. Treatment of 
gyrometrin/mono methyl hydrazine (MMH) poisoning is 
generally supportive, commencing with GI decontamination 
if still applicable (determined by the delay between consump-
tion and presentation) using activated charcoal. IV fluids may 
be required to correct GI fluid loss. Pyridoxine (Vitamin B6) 
can be used as an “antidote” in the treatment of convulsions 
or coma at about 25 mg/kg IV over 15–20 minutes, but if 
repeated doses are used, do not exceed 20 g/day. Other treat-
ments of value include methylene blue for methemoglobin-
emia (except in patients with G6PD deficiency), folinic acid 
for folate inhibition, benzodiazepines for seizure control, and 
vitamin K for coagulopathy (secondary to liver failure). If 
renal failure develops, hemodialysis may be required. For 
severe liver failure from amatoxin poisoning, an orthotopic 
liver transplant is an established lifesaving final therapeutic 
approach, but this has not been documented for gyrometrin/
MMH poisoning.
15.4.4.2  Group 4B—Disulfiram-Like 
Mushroom Poisoning
Some mushrooms, notably selected Coprinus spp., particu-
larly C. atramentarius, contain toxins such as coprines that 
can cause a disulfiram-like reaction on consumption of alco-
hol after consuming the mushrooms.167,174 Other mushrooms 
implicated include Lepiota aspera, C. clavipes, and Boletus 
luridus. This effect can last several days. Within minutes of 
drinking the alcohol, patients develop a blotchy erythematous 
rash, flushed faces, with other effects, including headache, 
dyspnea, sweating, nausea, vomiting, tachycardia, premature 
ventricular contractions, atrial fibrillation, vertigo, confu-
sion, and a metallic taste in the mouth. These effects typi-
cally last around 30 minutes, but occasionally up to 24 hours. 
Diagnosis is based on a history of ingestion of mushrooms, 
then alcohol, with subsequent rapid onset of typical symp-
toms. There is no specific laboratory test, but serum ethanol 
levels may be useful, particularly if alcohol consumption is 
denied. Treatment of coprine poisoning is principally sup-
portive and management of significant complications, which 
occur in a minority of cases. Decontamination is, in general, 
unhelpful, even the use of activated charcoal. An emetic 
such as ipecac is contraindicated because of alcohol content. 
Antihistamines have not proved useful and propranolol is 
potentially risky. There is no specific antidote. Fomepizole 
or similar alcohol dehydrogenase inhibitors, if given early 
enough while ethanol levels are elevated, may reduce the 
severity of poisoning. In severe poisoning, hemodialysis 
might prove useful to reduce both ethanol and acetaldehyde 
levels. IV fluid load may be required for control of hypoten-
sion and dopamine may also be effective. It is important to 
ensure that no medication containing alcohol is given to the 
patient for at least 3–5 days post poisoning.
15.4.4.3  Group 4C—Polyporic 
Mushroom Poisoning
A few mushrooms, notably Hapalopilus rutilans, contain 
polyporic acid and ingestion can cause a multi-system dis-
ease commencing late, about 12 hours post-ingestion, with 
GI effects (abdominal pain, nausea, vomiting, and diarrhea), 
then neurologic effects (diplopia, blurred vision, disordered 
balance, nystagmus, and occasionally hallucinations), plus 
mild AKI, hepatotoxicity, proteinuria, leukocyturia, and the 
key diagnostic feature, purple urine. The illness is gener-
ally self-limited, resolving over 2–7 days. The key diagnostic 
feature is purple urine in a patient with a history of inges-
tion and typical symptoms. Laboratory tests should include 
hematology, blood chemistry, liver and renal function, and 
urine analysis. Treatment is poorly defined, supportive and 
symptomatic.
15.4.4.4  Group 4D—Trichothecene 
Mushroom Poisoning
An often-fatal form of mushroom poisoning characterized 
by bone marrow and multi-organ failure, reported only from 
Japan and Korea and associated with ingestion of Podostroma 
cornu-damae, is considered to be due to trichothecenes. Onset 
time is very variable, from soon after ingestion, to only after 
repeated ingestion over days to weeks. Some patients have ini-
tial GI effects (nausea, vomiting, diarrhea, and dehydration) 
but then develop hypotension, oliguric AKI, altered conscious 
state, lamellar desquamation of palms/soles/face, hair loss, and 
bone marrow failure with pancytopenia. Diagnosis is based on 
a history of ingestion and the development of typical clinical 
features, particularly those indicating bone marrow failure. In 
addition to a standard gamut of laboratory tests, bone marrow 
biopsy may be required. Treatment is not well characterized, 
is supportive and symptomatic, with a poor prognosis expected 
in many cases.

778
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
15.4.4.5  Group 4E—Hypoglycemic 
Mushroom Poisoning
This frequently lethal poisoning, described from Yunnan, 
China, and associated following ingestion of Trogia venenata, 
is thought to be caused by unusual amino acids which block 
β-oxidation of fatty acids and gluconeogenesis resulting in a 
rapid fall in blood glucose. The onset of effects is rapid with 
palpitations, dizziness, chest discomfort, dyspnea, abdominal 
pain, and collapse, death occurring within 2 hours. Diagnosis 
is based on a history of ingestion and often the rapid onset 
of typical symptoms, including collapse, with hypoglycemia 
detected. Laboratory tests center on blood glucose levels and 
other blood chemistry. Treatment is not well characterized but 
clearly should include urgent reversal of hypoglycemia and 
attention to secondary metabolic effects.
15.4.4.6  Group 4F—Hyperprolactinemia 
Mushroom Poisoning
This rarely reported poisoning (seven cases) from France, fol-
lowing ingestion of Boletus satanas, presents with GI effects 
(vomiting and diarrhea) about 2 hours post-ingestion, often with 
a low-grade fever (38.5°C), then, at about 12 hours post-inges-
tion, high levels of procalcitonin and C-reactive protein (CRP) 
are detectable. All symptoms and abnormal laboratory findings 
resolve after about 24 hours. Diagnosis is based on a history of 
ingestion and the development of typical symptoms in associa-
tion with raised levels of procalcitonin and CRP. Treatment is 
not well characterized but should be supportive and symptom-
atic, with an expectation of resolution over 24 hours or so.
15.4.4.7  Group 4G—Pancytopenic 
Mushroom Poisoning
A very rarely reported poisoning (two cases) from Japan, 
developing after several days of sustained consumption of 
Ganoderma neojaponicum mushrooms in herbal treatments, 
presenting with fever and isolated pancytopenia, without other 
organ involvement and no dermatologic manifestations (unlike 
type 4D poisoning). Both cases resolved over 1–2 weeks with 
supportive care only. The causative toxin(s) and mechanism 
of action are unknown, but it is thought that the use of these 
mushrooms was in error, mistaken for the commonly used 
Ganoderma lucidum. Diagnosis is based on a history of 
ingestion and the development of typical symptoms, notably 
unexplained fever and pancytopenia. No specific treatment is 
known; optimistic supportive care is appropriate.
15.4.5  Group 5—GI Irritant Mushroom Poisoning
Arguably the most common form of mushroom poisoning, dis-
tinguished from all the other forms by the absence of effects 
other than GIT disturbance. Because many types of mushroom 
poisoning can cause GIT effects, diagnosis of Group-5 poison-
ing requires patience, exclusion of other types of poisoning, 
particularly potentially lethal types such as Group-1, particu-
larly Group-1A, amatoxin poisoning, or clear identification 
of causative mushrooms as known Type-5 species. It is likely 
that as more case information is published that subgroups of 
Group-5 poisoning will be identified. A wide range of spe-
cies has been implicated, including Chlorophyllum molyb-
dites, Entoloma sinuatum, Tricholoma pardalotum, and 
Omphalotus guepiniformis. It is likely that a variety of toxins 
may be involved. Clinically poisoning usually presents with 
variable GIT effects that may include nausea, vomiting, diar-
rhea, abdominal pain, and secondary dehydration, with onset 
mostly 1–3 hours post-ingestion. The clinical situation can 
be confusing because mushrooms contain components indi-
gestible by humans, so the consumption of large amounts of 
mushrooms can result in GIT effects that are not toxin related. 
A study in Poland estimated that 87% of cases followed con-
sumption of “edible” mushrooms and some or most of these 
cases may represent indigestibility or poor post-harvest stor-
age causes rather than toxin-based GIT effects. The post-col-
lection storage problem may be significant. Diagnosis is based 
on a history of ingestion and subsequent early development of 
GIT effects, without evidence of other systems involvement. 
Beware of co-ingestion of other types of mushroom, particu-
larly amatoxic species, as the normal late presentation of GIT 
effects with these latter mushrooms may be masked by the 
early GIT effects of Group-5 poisoning. Treatment is based on 
supportive and symptomatic care with attention to appropriate 
rehydration, including IV fluid replacement when indicated.
15.4.6  Group 6—Miscellaneous Adverse 
Reactions to Mushrooms
15.4.6.1  Group 6A—Shiitake Mushroom Dermatitis
Some humans can develop acute dermatitis, with flagellate 
linear wheals 1–2 days post-ingestion of either raw or cooked 
Shiitake mushrooms, Lentinula edodes. While the pathophysi-
ology is incompletely understood, it appears to be associated 
with lentinan in these mushrooms. The skin lesions can occur 
anywhere, but particularly on the body and head, and resolve 
over days to several weeks. They can be pruritic but are not 
induced by scratching. A single similar case has been reported 
after the consumption of Auricularia auricular-judae. 
Diagnosis is based on a history of ingestion and subsequent 
development of the classic dermatologic features. Treatment is 
supportive and symptomatic.
15.4.6.2  Group 6B—Erythromelalgic 
Mushroom Poisoning
Consumption of mushrooms, principally Paralepistopsis (ex 
Clitocybe) acromelaga/amoenolens causing an erythrome-
lalgia-like syndrome, presenting several days post-ingestion 
with reddening, swelling, and burning pain in the hands and 
feet, resolving over several weeks and considered caused by 
acromelic acid. There are no specific diagnostic tests, so a his-
tory of ingestion and subsequent development of allodynia is 
key. There is minimal clinical information available on what 
to base treatment advice on, but it appears that poisoning is 
unlikely to be lethal and as pain is the predominant feature, 
symptomatic analgesia should be considered. As the delay 
between ingestion and symptom onset is uncertain, the role

779
Plant and Animal Toxins
of early decontamination is unknown. A single case report 
claimed that nicotinic acid, initially IV, then oral, was associ-
ated with clinical and radiologic improvement.175
15.4.6.3  Group 6C—Paxillus Syndrome
An uncommon to a rare syndrome, potentially fatal, pre-
senting after repeated consumption of mushrooms, notably 
Paxillus involutus, which stimulates an autoimmune reaction 
resulting in initial rapid onset GIT effects (nausea, vomiting, 
abdominal pain, and diarrhea) followed by major systemic 
autoimmune effects which can include intravascular hemoly-
sis, anemia, secondary kidney injury/failure, shock, dissemi-
nated intravascular coagulopathy (DIC), and acute respiratory 
failure.176,177 Diagnosis is based on a history of ingestion and 
the development of typical clinical features. Diagnostic confu-
sion with Group 1A (amatoxin hepatotoxicity) is possible, as 
the hemolysis in Paxillus syndrome can result in high bilirubin 
levels and elevation of transaminases. Treatment is not well 
characterized and is supportive and symptomatic.
15.4.6.4  Group 6D—Encephalopathy Syndrome
Initially described from Japan, in patients with pre-existing 
chronic renal failure, who developed delayed onset enceph-
alopathy after consumption of mushrooms, particularly 
Pleurocybella porrigens, Grifola frondosa, and Pleurotus 
eryngii, containing high levels of HCN.178–180 This type of 
mushroom poisoning requires further characterization. 
Treatment is not well characterized.
There are other types of mushrooms or suspected mush-
room poisoning reported, but with insufficient character-
ization to currently include in a taxonomy of mushroom 
poisoning. However, clinicians should always consider that 
they may encounter hitherto unreported new forms of mush-
room poisoning and in such cases should strive to confirm the 
involvement and identity of mushrooms and document clinical 
presentation, progression, outcome, and treatments used, both 
successful and unsuccessful/harmful.
Sometimes patients will present with clearly identifiable 
mushrooms that will make the establishment of a diagnosis 
and consequent treatment pathway clear, but frequently the 
identity of the mushrooms may be unknown, at least initially. 
Always try and secure samples of implicated mushrooms and 
seek expert identification, especially in cases with major poi-
soning effects. Identifying that a severely poisonous mushroom 
is fruiting in areas where it may be harvested by the public 
is important public health information that should result in 
public warnings to reduce the chance that others will harvest, 
consume, and be poisoned. While most types of mushroom 
poisoning may cause GIT effects, the timing of onset can be 
important diagnostically, with delayed onset >6 hours raising 
a warning flag for severe types of poisoning such as amatoxin 
poisoning. Beware the patient who presents early with GIT 
effects, but who has consumed multiple species of mushrooms 
in a meal as this may mask late developing GI effects. A diag-
nostic algorithm to assist in the diagnosis of the type of mush-
room poisoning and consequent treatment pathway, based on 
clinical presentation rather than identified mushroom species, 
is available (Figure 15.1). This diagnostic algorithm is a guide 
only and cannot cover all known, or unknown, variations on 
documented types of mushroom poisoning, let alone cover 
currently unreported new types of poisoning.
15.5  Animal Toxins
Toxins are represented by an impressively wide spectrum of 
compounds in numerous botanical species; however, nature’s 
evolutionary chemistry is equally impressive in its complex 
synthesis of toxins and their diversity and distribution in the 
world’s animal taxa. From the oceans and continental land-
masses of all geographic regions and climates, toxins have 
been discovered in a diverse array of aquatic and terres-
trial animals. The prominent animal phyla associated with 
toxins are the Annelida, Arthropoda, Chordata, Cnidaria, 
Echinodermata, Platyhelminthes, and Mollusca.181
15.5.1  What is an Animal Toxin?
An animal toxin is produced by living cells or organisms in 
animals and it can be a protein, peptide, or non-proteinaceous 
molecule, although the production of some toxins requires the 
co-dependence of associated microorganisms and the host 
animal. Additionally, an animal’s toxin repertoire can be influ-
enced by geographic location, climatic factors, gender, life 
stage, and, in the case of eusocial insects, their caste. Toxins, 
biologically and chemically, range from quite simple to com-
plex compounds. Certain animals possess only a single toxin 
produced within a specific anatomical site in their body. For 
example, formicine ants produce a single toxin, formic acid, in 
a specialized venom gland. The effect of a single toxin can be 
like a biochemical bullet with a specific molecular target, or it 
can have broader activity like the aforementioned formic acid. 
However, many animals, especially venomous animals, pro-
duce complex mixtures of toxins, and each toxin in the mix-
ture may have a specific molecular target. Moreover, to add 
to the pharmacological complexity of these mixtures, some 
toxins have multiple active sites, each with specific targets; 
for example, phospholipase A2 (PLA2) “neurotoxins” found in 
snake venoms can target both the terminal axon at the neu-
romuscular junction and muscle cells, causing both flaccid 
paralysis and widespread muscle destruction.
When a toxin is introduced into the tissues of another liv-
ing organism, it induces an adverse physiological event. This 
results from a plethora of potential biochemical events, rang-
ing from cell membrane destruction to inhibition or activation 
of specific ion channels, receptors, or enzymes. In certain 
circumstances, once a toxin has been introduced into another 
organism, an immune response is elicited, triggering the 
induction of neutralizing antibodies or antitoxins. Allergy 
can develop to previously encountered toxins and, at least in 
humans, anaphylactic reactions are possible and potentially 
fatal. Exposure to the toxin may be as simple as acciden-
tal inhalation of minute amounts of aerosolized venom in a 
research laboratory, which may result in levels of allergy vary-
ing from minor nuisance-type reactions on recurrent exposure 
through to full anaphylaxis.

780
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology

Plant and Animal Toxins
15.5.2  Venoms and Poisons
Many toxin-producing animals, such as arachnids, insects, 
reptiles, and marine animals, are known to possess a com-
plex mixture of toxins usually associated with, and harbored 
in, highly evolved toxin delivery systems or glands. These 
toxin arsenals are referred to as venoms or poisons, depend-
ing on the mechanism by which they are delivered to target 
organisms. By definition, a venom is a secretion produced in 
a specialized gland and delivered to a target animal through 
the infliction of a wound in order to facilitate feeding or 
defense.182 Examples include the venoms of snakes, spiders, 
and scorpions. In contrast, poisons are not delivered via 
infliction of a wound but rather passively via contact, inha-
lation, or ingestion. Poisonous animals often deliver their 
toxins during attempted consumption, such as when a preda-
tor bites around the head of a toad, causing the nuchal poison 
glands to exude toxins.
Thus, an animal should be described as venomous rather 
than poisonous if it actively uses venom and a specific venom-
delivery apparatus to aid the acquisition of prey, defense 
against predators, or some other function such as deterrence 
of competitors in the case of platypus. The primary function 
of venom likely defines its mechanism of action; prey acqui-
sition may favor venom components that rapidly immobilize 
or kill prey such as neurotoxins and hemostatic-system tox-
ins that promote bleeding or clotting, while defensive toxins 
may favor induction of pain or other non-lethal but distressing 
effects in potential predators.
15.5.3  Toxin Functions
Toxins can act as sole agents or work synergistically with 
other toxins to repel other organisms (e.g., antipreda-
tor, anti-competitor) or more effectively achieve a desired 
adverse end-effect on another organism (e.g., prey acqui-
sition). Commonly, toxin functions are loosely related to 
their effects on a given physiological system, resulting 
in terms such as neurotoxins, myotoxins, cytotoxins, and 
hemorrhagic toxins. Regardless of the physiological system 
affected, animal toxins evolved primarily for two reasons, 
as noted earlier. First, many toxins are useful in subduing 
prey for food, and second, toxins often serve in a role of 
self-defense. Both of these functions provide a competitive 
advantage for the animal in evolutionary terms. However, 
there are circumstances where a toxin is present in an ani-
mal as a result of being passively taken into the body, caus-
ing no apparent adverse effects to the animal, and having 
no apparent function. It is difficult to know if a mollusk 
harbors a toxic alga for protection from predators or for 
predation of prey food versus simply being present due to 
the alga’s proximity in the mollusk’s environment. Thus, 
a toxin can enter the food chain without having a specific 
function for the animal harboring the toxin, such as tetro-
dotoxin (TTX), ciguatoxins, and various shellfish toxins in 
the marine environment, all rendering the animal poten-
tially poisonous when consumed by some predators, such 
as humans and domestic animals.
15.5.4  Bioaccumulation and Bioamplification 
of Animal Toxins
Animals can accumulate toxins as they are transferred up the 
food chain. This can be an active or passive bioamplification 
process as animals ingest, filter in, or absorb certain toxins or 
toxin-producing organisms, with the end result being a pas-
sively acquired toxicity for the host animal. In turn, these ani-
mals may, or may not, become poisonous to larger animals that 
prey on them.183 This particular scenario is prominent in marine 
biology as many species of fish are the top consumers or preda-
tors of various toxic marine organisms. For example, a mollusk 
can filter feed on toxin-producing algae and then itself be eaten 
by a fish that survives the ingestion without complication. A 
human then consumes this fish with its burden of bioaccumu-
lated toxin. Whether the lack of effect on the mollusk or fish is 
the result of tolerance to the algal toxin or natural resistance 
to the toxin’s effects depends on the species. When humans or 
other mammals ingest these toxin-laden fish, severe toxicologi-
cal consequences can occur. Animals may also acquire their 
toxins from a wide variety of toxin-containing plants on which 
they feed as part of their diet. A more thorough discussion of 
toxins in the food chain is discussed in Chapter 14.
15.5.5  Toxin Delivery Systems
Toxin delivery to a specific target can only occur following 
its introduction onto or into another living organism, and in 
this respect, the animals of the natural world have proven to 
be quite accomplished. The introduction of a toxin, venom, or 
poison can occur via absorption onto, or across a barrier, and by 
injection into, or through, a barrier. Usually, this barrier is ana-
tomically dermal in nature and not readily penetrated by most 
noxious external environmental substances. Toxin application 
and penetration processes are achieved by specific mechani-
cal strategies such as superficial mucous secretions, bristles 
and hairs, glandular skin secretions, barbs, spines, spurs, and 
teeth. Pharmacological or toxicological actions of toxins fol-
lowing their topical deposition on body surfaces can cause 
superficial or severe localized reactions. This topical delivery 
process can be enhanced, dependent on the chemical properties 
and pharmacological actions of the toxin, inducing the toxin’s 
transdermal absorption, and result in systemic toxicity. Toxin 
delivery into the systemic processes of another living organism 
can cause effects ranging from limited physiologic responses 
to severe systemic physiological alterations that result in death. 
Airborne toxin delivery is a method employed by some ani-
mals, such as insects and certain snakes (spitting cobras), and 
is achieved by spraying a toxin topically onto dermal or ocular 
surfaces or even spraying in the oral cavity of predators. Some 
marine venomous animals may release venom into the water 
adjacent to their prey, immobilizing or otherwise rendering 
the prey less resistant to subsequent capture and consumption, 
as seen with some species of venomous octopuses and cone 
snails. More advanced and direct methods of toxin delivery are 
achieved by injection via a bite or sting. These may be associ-
ated with an anatomically sophisticated delivery system such 
as that present in venomous snakes and arachnids, or the sting-
ing apparatus of hymenopterans (ants, bees, and wasps).

782
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
15.5.6  Complementation and 
Diversification of Animal Toxins
The sharing and interrelationships of phyla, toxins, toxin 
structures, and toxin functions exist across marine and ter-
restrial environments. Distinct animal species from different 
phyla, from widely separated geographic regions and habitats, 
have developed toxins with a variety of chemical-structural 
and functional relationships. Combinations of animal phyla, 
animal toxins, and the associated toxin structure–function 
relationships have yielded four basic schemes in nature: (1) 
interphylogenetic species producing a common toxin that acts 
on a common target; (2) interphylogenetic species producing 
different toxins with chemically related structures that act on 
a common target; (3) interphylogenetic species producing tox-
ins with unrelated chemical structures that act on a common 
target; and (4) intraphylogenetic species producing toxins with 
similar chemical structures, or toxins with unrelated chemi-
cal structures, that act on the same target, or different targets 
(Table 15.3). The interrelationship of these factors provides 
evidence for divergent evolutionary function in toxin-produc-
ing animals, between phyla or within a phylum, and for con-
vergent evolutionary function with respect to toxin properties 
and function.
κ-Conotoxins from marine cone snails (Conus spp.; phy-
lum Mollusca) are quite different in chemical configuration to 
charybdotoxin from the yellow scorpion, Leiurus quinques-
triatus, phylum Arthropoda, yet both of these toxins target 
voltage-gated potassium (KV) channels.181 An alternate path 
in this relationship is observed between toxins from the same 
phylum when toxins of similar chemical structure act on sepa-
rate targets. For example, neurotoxin alpha from the venom of 
the black-necked cobra Naja nigricollis, and fasciculin 1, from 
the venom of the East African green mamba Dendroaspis 
angusticeps, are structurally similar three-finger toxins, but 
neurotoxin alpha acts on nAChRs, while fasciculin 1 targets 
acetylcholinesterase. ω-Conotoxins from cone snails and 
ω-agatoxins from American funnel-web spiders (Agelenopsis 
spp.; phylum Arthropoda) both have an inhibitor cystine-knot 
fold, and both target voltage-gated calcium (CaV) channels.184 
Thus, toxins from quite evolutionarily distinct animals, or 
evolutionarily similar animals, can exhibit both qualitative 
and quantitative similarities and differences, in their effect. 
However, there are also toxins that are widespread in the ani-
mal kingdom, such as TTX which is found in amphibians, 
fish, blue-ringed octopus, and pitohui birds that have both 
a conserved chemical structure and pharmacological site of 
action.185
15.5.7  Mechanisms of Animal Toxin Actions
The biodiversity of organisms provides a diverse spectrum 
of animal toxins with pharmacological activities that are 
essential for the survival of the host animal, whether they 
are required for food acquisition, defense, competitor deter-
rence, or other functions. Whether the effects of the toxin are 
neurotoxic, myotoxic, cardiotoxic, nephrotoxic, cytotoxic, or 
coagulopathic, these effects are usually due to toxin proteins 
or peptides, acting either singly or collectively, as in the case of 
some venoms, to create a single action or a cascade of reactions 
that result in toxicological consequences to a living organism.
Toxicity resulting from the pharmacologic actions of differ-
ent toxins can reflect both qualitative and quantitative differ-
ences. A toxin’s ability to elicit a toxic effect by acting at a 
single target may be quantitative due to the resultant toxicity
