Plant and Animal Toxins
being dependent on the dose of toxin reaching the intended 
molecular target. Receptors may structurally possess a single 
binding site for a toxin or more than one binding site for the 
toxin. The degree of toxin binding may be limited by confor-
mational characteristics of the binding site but binding to a 
single site or more than a single site may still depend on the 
dose of the toxin. If the dose is low, there may be minimal 
binding at only one site on the receptor, and this may not result 
in any measurable toxicity. If the toxin dose is large, increased 
binding may occur at a single site, or at more binding sites on 
the receptor, resulting in significant toxicity. Thus, with differ-
ent animal toxins and their quest for different receptor targets 
or sub-targets, there potentially exists a resonance of flexible 
stoichiometric relationships. All these factors, in addition to 
the toxin’s own chemical configuration, can affect what bind-
ing site a toxin is specific for. Subtle differences in how these 
factors are related are especially important as slight chemical 
and structural differences in toxins cannot only provide for a 
specific toxin’s binding the same receptor as another toxin but 
also allow for the toxin to bind at a different site on the same 
receptor. For example, the aforementioned TTX binds to the 
pore region of NaV channels and essentially acts like a cork in 
the bottle that sterically prevents ion flow, whereas NaV chan-
nel toxins from arachnid venoms are agonists or antagonists 
that bind to the channel’s voltage-sensor domains (VSDs) and 
allosterically regulate channel activity.
Additionally, there may exist binding sites within a given 
target, such as a cell membrane, which allow the binding of 
different toxins. This multiple toxin–multiple target relation-
ship is commonly observed with snake venoms. These mul-
tiple toxins found in venoms may also function sequentially 
via synchronized pharmacologic actions when injected into 
another organism, with one toxin leading to improved bio-
availability of another more potent toxin.
Although toxin dose is an important component in the devel-
opment of toxicological effects, the inherent affinity of toxins 
for given targets relating to toxin specificity is also a factor that 
influences the potency of a given dose of toxin. Animal toxins 
can compete as agonists or antagonists with endogenous neu-
rotransmitters in synaptosomal clefts and may either act pre-
synaptically or postsynaptically. Many toxin pharmacologies 
are widespread in venomous phyla, including toxins that target 
the acetylcholine receptor and those that target voltage-gated 
sodium, potassium, and calcium channels.
Toxins may also digest or directly inactivate neurotransmit-
ters. PLA2 is an enzymatic protein with wide representation 
in the animal kingdom and known to exist in greater than 100 
isoforms. Not surprisingly, the various forms are capable of 
exhibiting diverse pharmacologic activities and physiological 
properties ranging from the destruction of cell membranes to 
acting as presynaptic neurotoxins.186,187 Other animal toxins 
can act on a variety of cell membranes, altering cellular mem-
brane integrity, and inducing secondary toxic effects from a 
toxin’s cytolytic, myotoxic, and coagulopathic effects.
Target specificity also affects toxin potency. Toxins gen-
erally have specific targets, and these may only be present, 
or accessible, in certain animals, usually the natural prey or 
predator species for a given toxin-producing animal. This 
specificity can lead to an “arms race” between predator and 
prey. Examples include the relationship between the mongoose 
and cobras; the cobra venom contains postsynaptic paralytic 
neurotoxins that target acetylcholine receptors at the neuro-
muscular junction. A small variation in the structure of this 
receptor can reduce or eliminate the binding of these neuro-
toxins, allowing potential predators of the cobra, such as the 
mongoose, to avoid life-threatening effects of being bitten and 
envenomed. Equally, a venom that has a highly specific prey 
target may, coincidently, also affect animals that are manifestly 
not a prey target. An example is the Australian funnel-web spi-
der whose venom targets arthropod prey. Most mammals are 
essentially immune to this venom, but humans are not; with-
out effective treatment (in this case a specific antivenom) a 
funnel-web spider bite that would be harmless for a cat could 
potentially kill an adult human. Clearly this susceptibility of 
humans to the venom has no clear evolutionary advantage for 
the spider; rather, it is an accidental consequence of this toxin 
being developed as a defense against vertebrate predators.188
15.5.8  Marine and Freshwater 
Ecosystem Animal Toxins
Greater than two-thirds of the earth is covered by ocean salt 
waters that is home to diverse and highly integrated marine 
communities dominated by animals rather than plants. The 
remaining one-third of the planet’s landmass is dissected by 
rivers and scattered with lakes, comprising freshwater aquatic 
ecosystems that also contain integrated animal communities 
of great diversity. All marine animals are aquatic, but not all 
aquatic animals are marine. In organisms from marine envi-
ronments alone, the chemical structures of greater than 5,000 
natural products, as single chemical classes or mixed biosyn-
thetic compounds, have been elucidated.189–191 This number is 
probably far less for compounds derived from freshwater spe-
cies because of the considerably smaller freshwater habitat on 
earth. These organism-taxonomic and toxin-chemistry rela-
tionships are complex, and the biogenetic diversity of animal 
toxins in these habitats is enormous.
15.5.8.1  Invertebrate Marine and Freshwater 
Animal Toxins and Venoms
Multiple genera representing 11 different classes, derived 
from five different phyla, comprise the phylogenetic roots for 
thousands of invertebrate species in marine ecosystems (Table 
15.4). Although marine invertebrates are rather simplistic in 
form, ranging from simple hydrozoan corals to more com-
plex forms such as gastropod cone snails, many possess some 
form of toxin or venom as a means for acquiring a meal, or 
as a deterrent against predators. However, in addition to toxin 
effects on other organisms, a remarkable number of marine 
invertebrate toxins are also harmful, or even lethal, to humans.
15.5.8.1.1  Annelida
Several genera of bristle worms and fire worms, class 
Polychaeta, have been reported to possess toxins (Table 15.4).192 
Known by a variety of common names, they share the com-
mon feature of having parapodia, leg-like appendages on each 
of their body segments, to which hollow monofilament-like

784
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
bristles called setae are attached. In many cases, the bristles 
may have a feathery appearance. Despite anatomical similari-
ties, different species display a range of appearances reflected 
in their common names such as palolo worms, featherduster 
worms, sea mouse, or lug worms. Some species such as Eunice 
gigantea have been reported to grow up to 3 m in length.192
The orange fireworm Eurythoe complanata, a species indig-
enous to the tropical Pacific Ocean and the Gulf of Mexico, has 
spiny, barbed bristles that it promptly flares when threatened. 
If bristle contact occurs by a predatory animal or human touch, 
the barbed glassy spines are readily released into the tissues of 
the offender. This action results in a mechanical or physical 
insult causing a localized dermal inflammatory response and 
paresthesia that may last for weeks but is not believed to be 
associated with the delivery of a toxin. Embedded bristles may 
be partially removed by the application of adhesive tape over 
the affected area. Topical use of a steroid cream and oral or 
topical antihistamines may be a beneficial symptomatic treat-
ment, and severe cases of secondary infection or even gan-
grene are possible.193,194
Importantly, certain species such as Eunice and Glyceria 
species have jaws associated with a defined venom gland and 
a fang-like proboscis that delivers a large 320-kDa presynap-
tic neurotoxin, known as α-glycerotoxin,195 that stimulates the 
activity of CaV2.2 channels.196 Clinical symptoms from the bite 
include pain with blanching around the punctures, followed 
by several days with persistent itching. However, uneventful 
recovery generally follows.
The medicinal leech Hirudo medicinalis, Class Hirudinea, 
is a freshwater annelid that has been used by the lay public 
for self-remedies, and by physicians since 200 BC for a vari-
ety of medical purposes, including as a postoperative aid in 
microvascular surgery.197,198 These creatures have two suckers 
that lie at the anterior and posterior ends of the head, with the 
mouth being in the anterior sucker where three jaws of teeth are 
located. Once attached to the often-unknowing victim, blood 
is sucked out as the leech uses rhythmic contractions of its 
pharynx to deliver the blood to its crop for storage until diges-
tion takes place. The leech can ingest a blood volume nearly 
ten times its weight (up to 15 mL). The anticoagulant effect of 
leech venom stems from the toxin hirudin, an antithrombin 
substance, but the venom also contains hyaluronidase, pro-
teolytic enzymes such as bdellins and elgins, fibrinase, col-
lagenase, and salivary apyrases.199,200 Notably, recombinant 
hirudin is now used clinically as an anticoagulant.201
The most common effects of leech bites are the depletion 
of blood volume in small animals from which the leech draws 
its nutrition and continued oozing of blood from the bite site 
following detachment. Oozing can continue for up to 24 hours 
after leech removal.202 Leeches have caused hematuria due 
to urinary tract invasion, and hemoptysis from upper airway 
attachment.203
Treatment of leech bite is not standardized and a myriad 
of means for leech removal have been proposed. Cocaine has 
been used to paralyze leeches, resulting in their release from 
their victim, but would certainly not be recommended in cases 
of a leech-lodged airway. The use of forceps to hold the leech 
until it releases has been effective, and one individual required 
anesthesia with direct visualization for leech retrieval.204 
Regardless of the leech removal method, once removed, it is 
recommended that the wound be irrigated, evaluated for infec-
tion, and pressure applied and gelatin sponge used if bleeding 
persists.202
15.5.8.1.2  Cnidaria
The phylum Cnidaria includes sea anemones, corals, jelly-
fish, and hydrozoans (Table 15.4). This phylum is unique for 
being the only entirely venomous phylum in the entire ani-
mal kingdom, as well as the oldest class of venomous animals. 
The creatures in this phylum often appear to be quite differ-
ent visually, yet they share a common toxin-delivery mecha-
nism that involves stinging organelles known as nematocysts, 
sometimes referred to as cnidocysts.205,206 These microscopic 
structures are organelles contained within specialized stinging

Plant and Animal Toxins
cells, or cnidocytes, which, in the resting stage, appear like a 
well-inflated balloon with a small spike protruding. The spike 
continues to the interior as a barbed luminous thread that 
spirals like a spring within a venom capsule. If a significant 
osmolar or pressure change occurs, the nematocyst explosively 
discharges its inverted, barbed filament into the tissue of its vic-
tim with simultaneous injection of venom.207 The discharged 
nematocyst then appears, grossly, as a slightly deflated balloon 
attached to a string.192 Nematocysts are primarily located on 
tentacles or along folding anatomical ridges, with thousands to 
millions of these organelles present, allowing for the delivery 
of considerable quantities of venom. Interestingly, some crea-
tures have extended the use of nematocysts and their toxins 
via an interesting phenomenon in which non-cnidarian marine 
creatures such as the octopus, Tremoctopus violaceus, attach 
fragments of Portuguese man o’ war (Physalia physalis) ten-
tacles to their own tentacles as both offensive and defensive 
armaments.208
Toxins that have been identified in cnidarians stem from 
a variety of peptides and proteins that possess neurotoxic, 
hemolytic, or cytolytic properties.209 Although the number 
of cnidarian species has been estimated at 10,000 or greater, 
the number of species potentially dangerous to humans is not 
known but is believed to be relatively low.210 Regardless of the 
unknown number of species dangerous to human health, the 
medical problems associated with cnidarian envenoming are 
global.211
15.5.8.1.3  Anthozoa
Sea anemones are one of Nature’s most beautiful and grace-
ful sea life forms, yet these mostly sedentary cnidarians can 
readily deploy their paralyzing toxins from nematocysts into 
potential predators that brush against their tentacles, or into 
the unknowing prey animal that seeks shelter among their 
tentacles. However, not all sea anemone toxins have been 
confirmed to be located in tentacle nematocysts, as the con-
tracting, intact body of some anemones will secrete cytolytic 
toxins with mechanical stimulation.212,213 Anemones can also 
retract their tentacles under stress conditions, resulting in the 
secretion of large quantities of toxins in mucus, rather than 
by nematocyst release, that can induce osmotic cell lysis via 
pore-forming ion channels in membranes. Interestingly, these 
toxins that serve to acquire prey may also aid in the self-pres-
ervation of an individual within an anemone colony by allow-
ing intraspecific spatial competition.214
An additional complexity concerning this marine life 
form is the interrelationship between some anemone genera 
(Entacmaea and Heteractis) and fish in the genera Amphiprion 
and Premnas that have a symbiotic relationship in which 
anemone toxins provide protection to fish hiding among the 
nematocyst-rich anemone tentacles.215 The exact reason that 
fish survive in this symbiotic relationship has not been con-
firmed to be universal, but some fish species are protected 
by a mucus coating of their skin, while others appear to have 
acquired immunity to certain sea anemone toxins.216
Sea anemones produce a diverse array of at least 17 dis-
tinct families of proteinaceous toxins, as well as less well-
studied non-proteinaceous toxins.217 Pore-forming cytolytic 
toxins known as actinoporins are common, and they have 
been characterized from more than 20 species.218 However, 
sea anemones also produce a diverse array of neurotoxic pep-
tides that modulate ion channel activity and are critical for 
rapid immobilization of prey.217A cardiostimulatory protein 
that lacks cytolytic activity has been isolated from Urticina 
piscivore, and some sea anemone toxins such as tealiatoxin 
produced by Tealia felina exhibit both hemolytic and histami-
nolytic activities.219
Although a multitude of toxins has been identified, the 
medical consequences of most anemone stings to humans 
are fairly similar. The common symptom is a painful, sting-
ing cutaneous lesion, which is localized to the area of tentacle 
contact.192 Severity is proportional to the number of discharged 
nematocysts in the skin. Nematocysts can be inactivated by 
fixing with the application of 5% acetic acid (vinegar), or a 
70% isopropyl alcohol solution. Removal of remnant tentacles 
and nematocysts can be accomplished by the unidirectional 
scraping of the affected area with a knife blade or the edge of 
a plastic card. The lesion should then be washed with antibac-
terial soap and generously rinsed with water. Topical steroid 
cream may be useful in reducing local inflammation.220 A spe-
cies of Condylactis is believed to have been responsible for the 
death of a young male due to rapid hepatocellular failure.221
15.5.8.1.4  Cubozoa
The Australian box jellyfish Chironex fleckeri (Order 
Chirodropida) is probably the most well-known of the jelly-
fishes because of its unique anatomy and potentially severe 
toxicity, with their stings being intensely painful and poten-
tially fatal. However, it is just one of numerous species of 
Cubozoan “box jellyfish”. Although closely related, their 
distinct anatomy separates them from Scyphozoan “true” jel-
lyfish. As their name suggests, box jellyfish are four-sided, 
cowbell shaped, translucent, gelatinous creatures that have 
muscular pedalia at each corner, where tentacles that are ten 
or more times the height of the bell and can be in excess of 
60 m for some species, stream in the underwater currents.222 
They inhabit mostly subtropical and tropical waters, and the 
coasts of northern Australia are notoriously famous for the 
presence of dangerous jellyfish. Carybdea species have been 
occasionally found in temperate waters.223 Cubozoans pos-
sess four photosensory structures known as rhopalia, which 
each containing four simple eyes and two complex eyes. This 
visual system makes cubozoans exceptionally phototactic as 
evidenced by their light-sensitive behavior of descending to 
deeper water during bright sunlight and moving toward the 
surface in the early morning, late afternoon, and evening.
In contrast to the majority of jellyfish, box jellies do not drift 
with the underwater currents and are not planktonic. They are 
strong swimmers and some species, such as Chironex fleckeri, 
have been reported to move at 3–6 m/min.224 Cuboidal jel-
lyfish from Australian waters are associated with interesting 
and unique local nomenclature such as the Irukandji (Carukia 
barnesi), the jimble (Carybdea rastoni), and the Morbakka 
(Tamoya spp.).225–227
Cubozoan tentacles are laden with nematocysts of several 
different types, which collectively form a complement of 
nematocysts referred to as a cnidome.222 Differing severity 
of toxicity between different genera may correlate with the

786
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
nematocyst concentration present in the tentacles of a given 
species, which is also related to its size.228 However, antibod-
ies against box jellyfish venom do not cross-react with other 
jellyfish venoms, suggesting that the observed differences in 
toxicity are due to different toxins present in the venoms of 
different genera.229 The venom of Chironex fleckeri is protein-
aceous and possesses dermonecrotic, hemolytic, and myotoxic 
toxins that may work synergistically to cause lethal cardio-
respiratory effects.230,231 Myotoxins of 150 and 600 kDa have 
been isolated from C. fleckeri and shown to be lethal in animal 
studies, but the characterization of other venom components 
has proven elusive.232 Commonly, envenomation results in 
hypercatecholemia, but this does not hold for all species, as the 
effects of venom from Chiropsalmus spp., a species respon-
sible for many fatalities in the Philippines, does not respond 
to receptor antagonists, or antibodies, that are effective in 
reversing C. fleckeri venom-induced symptoms.233,234 In cases 
of severe envenomation, death has occurred within minutes, 
and there have been at least 67 documented deaths attributed 
to C. fleckeri.231
In general, cubozoan envenomations do not result in severe 
complications, but those inflicted by Chironex fleckeri can 
cause death as evidenced by the numerous fatalities reported 
in Australian waters.235 Treatment of cubozoan envenom-
ations, without severe symptoms, involves the use of acetic 
acid to retard nematocyst firing, and symptomatic support, but 
there are increasing questions about the effectiveness of ace-
tic acid and it may be, in the future, that recommended treat-
ment, including first aid, will involve use of hot water (up to 
45°C) rather than vinegar. This would be in line with recom-
mendations for managing non-tropical jellyfish stings. More 
severe envenomations have required support with mechanical 
ventilation, which has still been ineffective in cases of pro-
found cardiotoxicity.236 The cause of death from C. fleckeri 
stings is rapid onset cardiac collapse and arrest, but this car-
diotoxicity may be short lived, so aggressive persistent resus-
citation is recommended. These severe stings are associated 
with massive tentacle contact, usually greater than half of one 
limb, whereby many millions of nematocysts may fire into 
the victim, with a substantial portion of venom being injected 
directly into intradermal capillaries, hence the rapid onset of 
systemic and cardiotoxic envenoming. Antivenom for C. fleck-
eri envenomation treatment has been developed, but there is 
only limited documentation as to its effectiveness in humans 
with severe cardiorespiratory symptoms,237 perhaps due to the 
rapidity of development of life-threatening envenoming.
Irukandji syndrome is a severe form of toxicity result-
ing from the sting of the small Carybdea barnesi jellyfish 
(Order Carybdeida) and several related species. It is a spe-
cies of undefined geographic origin that has primarily been 
reported off the coasts in the northern half of Australia, yet 
an Irukandji-like syndrome has been reported in Hawaii.238 
The clinical picture is distinct from C. fleckeri envenoming. 
The latter causes immediate severe pain along tentacle contact 
points which may be extensive and associated with blistering. 
In contrast, Irukandji-type stings, from tentacles or just the 
bell, may cause minimal pain, show minimal local effects, and 
the tentacle/bell contact area may be minute. Instead, a vari-
able period later, typically 20–30 minutes, the victim develops 
potentially severe systemic effects, including back and mus-
cle pain, sweating, hypertension, vomiting, rash, pulmonary 
edema, and other features of a catecholamine storm.239 At 
least one fatality has been reported, but the role of pre-existing 
medical problems may have contributed to the fatal outcome. 
Treatment is generally supportive, requiring analgesics, anti-
hypertensive drugs, ventilation, and pressor drugs, with recov-
ery in typically 3–4 days.231
15.5.8.1.5  Hydrozoa
Hydrozoa are primarily colonial benthic cnidarians, except 
for floating Physalia, which are simple marine life forms that, 
like all other members of Cnidaria, possess nematocysts. Four 
different morphological forms of nematocysts are known to 
be present in Hydrozoa: atrichous isorhiza, microbasic eury-
teles, microbasic mastigophores, and stenoles.210 They differ 
in the arrangement of spines on the shaft, which may result 
in varying degrees of anchoring to tissue and venom delivery 
into the victim.240 Thus, when contact with these organisms 
occurs, there is mechanical injury in addition to toxicological 
injury. Although the number of species in the class has been 
estimated at greater than 10,000, the number of species known 
to be potentially dangerous to humans is small.231
The fire corals (Millepora sp.) are hydrocorals rather than 
true corals as, despite their appearance, they are more closely 
related to other hydrozoans than true corals. They are familiar 
to many reef divers as they are frequently encountered among 
colonies of true corals. Antler-like in appearance, the velvety 
tines are covered with nematocyst-containing hydranths that 
look like small, segmented hairs with umbrellas at the tip. 
The venom contains thermolabile, proteinaceous components 
possessing both dermonecrotic and hemolytic properties. The 
venom is lethal to rodents.241 When contact with fire coral 
occurs, there is an immediate burning and stinging sensation 
that progresses rapidly to painful pruritus. Wheals develop and 
the area of contact becomes edematous and erythematous. Pain 
generally resolves within an hour or two, while dermatologi-
cal symptoms may persist for up to a week, with pigmentation 
lesions persisting for 1–2 months.242 Amelioration of symp-
toms usually requires only the application of a skin cream, but, 
in more severe cases, may require deactivation and removal 
of nematocysts, followed by the application of hydrocortisone. 
It has been reported that papain-containing meat tenderizers 
may be useful in recovering ingrained nematocysts.220
The Portuguese man o’ war, Physalia physalis, is a non-div-
ing, oceanic, jellyfish-like hydrozoan found primarily in the 
Atlantic Ocean. Their gas-filled bladders, which are actually 
modified medusae, allow them to drift with wind and surface 
water currents. Their numerous tentacles may be long, trail-
ing 13 m or more into the depths, and they can deliver potent 
venom from their associated nematocysts.243 A small, mor-
phological form of this genus, Physalia utriculus, commonly 
called the bluebottle or Pacific man o’ war, is widespread in the 
Indian and Pacific oceans, but lesions resulting from its stings 
are less extensive than those of P. physalis.192 Venom from 
Physalia has not been fully characterized, but a glycoprotein 
toxin, physaliatoxin, has been isolated and reported to possess 
cytolytic and hemolytic properties.244 Additionally, enzymatic 
proteins with cardiotoxic effects have been isolated, and whole

787
Plant and Animal Toxins
venom can elicit rapid histamine release from mast cells.245,246 
Complications to humans from the stings of Physalia can 
range from acute pain in tentacle contact areas to migratory 
joint pain, and in rare circumstances death from rapid respira-
tory failure followed by cardiovascular collapse.247 It may be 
that these rare major systemic reactions are allergic in nature, 
a form of anaphylaxis, rather than a primary toxin cardiotoxic 
effect. In contrast with the inhibitory effect of acetic acid on the 
firing of Chironex nematocysts, this therapy has been reported 
to induce further nematocyst discharge in cases of Physalia 
sting.248 Cold packs may reduce the superficial inflammatory 
and pain response of stung tissues, and papain solution has 
been useful for inactivating nematocysts.249 Topical lidocaine 
may also be beneficial in reducing localized inflammation 
and pain.250 However, recent research has demonstrated that 
hot water (up to 45°C), either through immersion, or as a hot 
shower, is more effective than cold packs and has now become 
the standard first aid for not only Physalia stings, but all non-
tropical jellyfish stings in Australia and elsewhere. Antivenom 
for Chironex stings provides no paraspecific coverage or 
protection to victims of Physalia envenomation, as it is not 
cross-reactive with Physalia venom.251 Thus, there is no defini-
tive treatment in cases of life-threatening envenomation, and 
basic cardiopulmonary resuscitation and support measures are 
essential if recovery is possible.
Feather hydroids in the family Aglaopheniidae are elegant 
colonial hydroids with a feathery, fern-like appearance. These 
benthic hydrozoans are found in shallow Atlantic, Indo-
Pacific, and Australian waters. Despite their harmless appear-
ance, their nematocysts can deliver painful stings, associated 
with erythematous and edematous wheeling when contact 
occurs.211 The best known genera for delivering venomous 
stings are Aglaophenia and Lytocarpus, but other genera 
may also cause dermal contact-related problems (Table 15.4). 
Little is known of feather hydroid venoms, but in recent years 
new venomous species have been discovered by accident as 
case reports of stings find their way into the literature. Sea 
fan envenomations are not life-threatening, but they cause dis-
comfort; superficial symptoms respond to the deactivation of 
nematocysts still in contact with the skin, and the application 
of anti-inflammatory creams.220
15.5.8.1.6  Schyphozoa
Scyphozoa is the class representing the “true” jellyfish, which 
are probably the jellyfish most frequently encountered by 
beachgoers. They tend to be radially symmetrical with their 
gelatinous medusal forms swimming in varying currents from 
the surface waters down to the abyss of the oceans, depend-
ing on the species.192 They have a variety of interesting and 
descriptive names: upside-down jellyfish (Cassiopea xamach-
ama), sea nettle (Chrysaora quinquecirrha), hair jelly (Cyanea 
sp.), described by Halstead as mop hiding under a dinner 
plate, and mauve stinger (Pelagia noctiluca).185,192 Although 
Schyphozoan nematocysts are capable of delivering irritat-
ing stings that may rarely cause muscle cramps and uncon-
sciousness, their venoms are not as dangerous as those of their 
Cubozoan relatives, as no fatalities have been reported.252
C. quinquecirrha crude venom (CQV) exhibits hemolytic 
activity253 and contains hyaluronidase, an enzyme thought to 
aid in the spread of venom, which is found in the venom of 
many diverse animal phyla.182 Animal studies have predomi-
nantly supported that fatal reactions to jellyfish envenomation 
have been the result of cardiac, respiratory, or renal toxicity, 
but CQV studies also suggest that the venom is capable of 
inducing hepatic lesions, subsequent to lethal cytotoxicity, 
which may explain fatal reactions as a result of delayed hepatic 
failure.254
C. xamachama, from Puerto Rican coasts, can cause pain-
ful stings, and their venom contains a low-molecular weight 
(<10 kDa) fraction that binds to muscarinic acetylcholine 
receptors and has strong hemolytic activity in an animal 
model. Additionally, a PLA2 fraction contributed significantly 
to its paralytic and lethal activity.255 The degree to which 
Schyphozoans share similar toxins remains to be elucidated, 
but toxin effects appear to cause a similar clinical profile in 
humans. The severity of envenomation depends on the extent 
and duration of the tentacle contact.192 The treatment of 
Schyphozoan envenomation is similar to that recommended 
for cubozoan stings, and with good supportive care, a favor-
able outcome is expected.
15.5.8.1.7  Echinodermata
The phylum Echinodermata is likely familiar to most people 
because of the amazing starfish (Class Asteroidea). For many 
years, these creatures have been taken from the world’s oceans, 
dried and sold as curios, to be placed on desktops, library 
shelves, and used for school show and tells. Sea urchins, Class 
Echinoidea, are also widely recognized due to the fear of a 
painful encounter with one of their spines. Many children 
and amateur snorkelers have paid the price for that tempting 
moment of curiosity, when the extended finger has tried to gen-
tly touch one of these creatures and been rapidly withdrawn 
in profound pain, or the unwary wader plants a foot on the 
needle-like spines. Unlike these better known echinoderms, 
the sea cucumbers (Class Holothuroidea) are more fragile and 
sluggish, and they lack the mechanical injury-inducing abili-
ties of other echinoderms. Although there are about 7,000 spe-
cies of extant echinoderms, only about 1% are believed to be 
venomous.256
Sea urchins, Class Echinoidea, represent the majority of 
venomous echinoderms and are painfully familiar to most 
people who have spent any time swimming, beach walking, 
surfing, or diving and snorkeling in subtropical or tropical 
coastal waters. They have a pincushion appearance with stiff, 
bristle-like spines that are structurally a calcium carbonate 
matrix, which may be hollow or solid, blunt or sharp, and may 
possess a venom-producing gland at the spinal apex.257 They 
also may introduce venom via pedicellariae, which are jaw-
like structures used to grasp unsuspecting prey. Urchin ven-
oms comprise cholinergic substances, hemolysins, proteases, 
serotonin, and sterol glycosides.258
Most urchins are incapable of effectively injecting venom 
into humans and stings result primarily in mechanical injury. 
However, there are two Indo-Pacific species, the needle-spined 
sea urchin (Diadema setosum) and the flower sea urchin 
(Toxopneustes pileolus), that can inflict serious envenom-
ations.243 Symptom severity depends on the number of pen-
etrating spines, and the associated pain is greater than that

788
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
of mechanical injury alone. In rare cases of severe human 
envenoming, symptoms of bronchospasm, cranial nerve dys-
function, hepatitis, hypotension, palpitations, and syncope 
have occurred.257,258 Toxopneustes envenomation has even 
been reported to have caused deaths in Japan.259 Treatment of 
urchin envenomation symptoms is ill-defined and complicated 
by the fragile nature of the spines that are frequently broken 
off in the wound. Methods developed to extricate spines are 
relatively ineffective, and appropriate wound care may involve 
warm water immersion, analgesic drugs, and the breaking of 
large embedded spines into smaller fragments so they may be 
more readily absorbed or extruded.194 Thus, the mainstay of 
treatment is symptomatic and supportive as needed.
Though most species of starfish, Class Asteroidea, are harm-
less, one species is responsible for envenomations to humans, 
the crown-of-thorns starfish (COTS) (Acanthaster planci) of 
the Indo-Pacific region.192 COTS, as their name suggests, are 
disc-shaped and replete with spines that can be as longer as 
6 cm. COTS are large, typically growing to 25–35 cm in diam-
eter, and they possess up to 23 arms. They are found among 
coral reefs where their feeding on coral polyps can destroy the 
fragile coral reef structure.260 Like urchins, it is the spines of 
this starfish that inflict a painful wound.192 Venom is believed 
to lie in the epidermal cell layer of the spines where acidophilic 
glandular cells produce venom, which has been shown to have 
hemolytic and lethal activities in a rodent model.261 Crude 
venom has also been shown to exhibit edema-forming and cap-
illary permeability inducing properties.262 More recent studies 
have revealed that the venom contains a 15.5-kDa PLA2 that 
can induce skeletal muscle myonecrosis in animals.263 Their 
venom has also been reported to contain a basic glycoprotein 
of 20–25-kDa molecular weight, with lethal properties when 
injected into mice, and hepatic and hypotensive effects have 
also been observed in animal experiments.264,265 COTS are 
also rich in saponins known as asterosaponins that are ich-
thyotoxic and hemolytic, and which are used in prey capture.266
COTS stings are similar to those observed from urchins, but 
the pain subsequent to venom release from embedded spines 
has been described as extremely intense with throbbing and 
burning sensations, which may last for a month. Treatment 
is also similar to that for urchins, with hot water submersion 
being of benefit in relieving pain initially, with prudent follow-
up wound care, observation for secondary infection, and use 
of steroid creams and antibiotics as indicated by symptoms.194
Sea cucumbers, Class Holothuroidea, in stark contrast to their 
spiny-bodied urchin and starfish relatives, are secretive, soft-bod-
ied, blob-like creatures with an amazing array of color patterns. 
They have no true appendages other than small tentacles around 
their mouth, and internally harbor organs referred to as Organs 
of Cuvier, also known as Cuvierian tubules.243 Occasionally, div-
ers and beachgoers will handle sea cucumbers causing them to 
release a glycoside known as holothurin. This toxin is a saponin 
produced in the Cuvierian tubules that is readily deployed from 
the cloaca when contact is made with an offending organism; it 
has a glue-like consistency and can be inflammatory to skin and 
cause conjunctival complications to the eye. To remedy symp-
toms, victims should decontaminate the areas of contact with 
gentle and voluminous rinsing and washing with mild detergent, 
while ocular exposure requires saline irrigation.
15.5.8.1.8  Mollusca
The cone snails, Class Gastropoda, superfamily Conidae, are 
dangerously beautiful, toxin-producing representatives of the 
phylum Mollusca. Conus is the largest genus of living marine 
invertebrates, with greater than 500 species that display an 
array of brilliant color patterns.267 Their venom is dominated 
by small, disulfide-rich peptides that are collectively known as 
conotoxins.268 The number of different conotoxins is believed 
to be greater than 50,000, and this number includes a pleth-
ora of peptides with diverse structures, and pharmacological 
activities on a wide range of ion channels and receptors.269,270 
Not surprisingly, given the pharmacological complexity of 
Conus venoms, they have been widely studied as a potential 
source of human therapeutics. Ziconotide, a synthetic version 
of ω-conotoxin MVIIA found in the venom of Conus magus, 
was approved by the FDA in 2004 for the treatment of intrac-
table chronic pain.201
Cone snails are predatory mollusks that inhabit tropical 
waters. They possess a hollow, harpoon-like radular tooth 
within an extensible rostrum that can be rapidly deployed for 
envenomating prey.256 They can be divided into three groups 
depending on their preferred prey: molluscivorous (feeding on 
gastropods), piscivorous (feeding on fish), and vermivorous 
(feeding on polychaete worms). They have also been known to 
prey on bivalve mollusks, octopus, and hemichordate worms.271
There are two major groups of toxins—those with mul-
tiple disulfide bonds (“conotoxins”), and the much less 
prevalent Conus peptides that possess only a single, or 
no, disulfide linkages. The disulfide-rich toxins are gener-
ally classified into superfamilies on the basis of their cys-
teine framework, which is the distribution and number of 
cysteines in their primary structure.272 Some of the most well-
studied conotoxin superfamilies include the A superfamily 
(α-conotoxins, αA-conotoxins, and κ conotoxins), M super-
family (μ-conotoxins and ψ-conotoxins), and O superfamily 
(ω-conotoxins, δ-conotoxins, and μO-conotoxins). Those in 
the A-superfamily primarily act as nAChR antagonists or KV 
channel blockers, while the M-superfamily toxins modulate 
the activity of NaV channels or act as nAChR antagonists. The 
O-superfamily conotoxins possess the greatest range of phar-
macological actions with activity on NaV, CaV, and KV channels. 
The non-disulfide Conus peptides such as conantokins, contu-
lakins, and conopressins, exhibit a diverse range of activities, 
including NMDA receptor antagonism, neurotensin-like activ-
ity, and vasopressin antagonism, respectively, while the phar-
macological role of contryphans remains to be determined.270
Cone-snail–related fatality to humans was first reported 
in 1705, and the fatality rate in more recent times has been 
reported to be 70%.273 The Geography Cone (Conus geogra-
phus) is the most frequent cause of human envenomations, and 
it is responsible for most fatalities. However, C. aulicus, C. glo-
riamaris, C. marmoreus, C. omaria, C. striatus, and C. tulipa 
have been reported to cause death in humans.273,274 Victims 
of C. geographus and C. obscurus envenomation experience 
a radiating numbness, while, contrastingly, victims of C. 
aulicus, C. textile, and C. tulipa report intense pain. Acute 
envenomation symptoms include muscular paralysis, respira-
tory distress, loss of gag reflex and the ability to swallow, and

789
Plant and Animal Toxins
blurred or double vision. Timely treatment involving cardio-
pulmonary support is essential as there is no antivenom.270
The blue-ringed octopus, Class Cephalopoda, is a small 
octopus with several species native to shallow coastal and 
intertidal waters of Australia and adjacent seas. Two similar-
appearing species, Hapalochlaena maculosa and H. lunulata, 
as their common name implies, have yellowish-gold bodies 
with dark mottled bands and blue rings, with the eponymous 
blue rings becoming much more intense when the animal is 
disturbed or frightened.243 They have eight arms, are carnivo-
rous, and use their two beak-like jaws to introduce venom via 
the salivary duct with the inflicted bite. They have also been 
observed to subdue prey by releasing their saliva into the water 
surrounding the prey.275
Two main toxins, TTX and hapalotoxin, have been identi-
fied from the venom of Hapalochlaena sp. Additional venom 
components such as histamine, serotonin, and tyramine have 
been found in the salivary glands.276 The bite of the blue-
ringed octopus may not elicit significant pain, and it may go 
unnoticed until symptoms of paresthesia around the face and 
neck are felt. Envenomation to humans has proven fatal, and 
death has been reported to occur within 30 minutes of hav-
ing been bitten. Since no antivenom is available, the rapid use 
of standard life support measures, with emphasis on respira-
tor-supported breathing being maintained until spontaneous 
breathing returns.277
Interestingly, saxitoxin has been reported to be present in 
Abdopus sp. octopus from the northern coastline of Western 
Australia. The source of this neurotoxin, normally produced 
by dinoflagellates, is uncertain, but the tentacle toxin concen-
tration is believed to be capable of causing profound paralytic 
shellfish poisoning.278
15.5.8.1.9  Porifera
Sponges are the simplest multicellular marine life forms, 
having no defined organ systems. They are sessile creatures 
with amorphous and radially symmetrical shapes that grow 
attached to nearly any substrate, and they are found from deep 
ocean bottoms to shallow coastal waters. There are four classes 
of Porifera, with Class Demospongiae being the most studied. 
At least 7,000 species have been reported in the literature (far 
fewer are freshwater species), and it has been speculated that 
the total number of species may be greater than 15,000.
Some sponges have sharp spicules, but these are not believed 
to deliver toxins, and it may be that potential toxins are asso-
ciated with the solid body rather than spicules.279 Although 
knowledge of sponge chemistry and pharmacology is in its 
infancy, the number of pharmacologically active compounds 
being discovered, such as the antitumor macrolides (spon-
gistatins), is increasing.280 Toxins specific to sponges have not 
been identified, but it is clear that adverse dermal reactions can 
result from dermal contact.
Skin contact with the Southern Australian toxic sponge, 
Neofibularia mordens, can cause severe dermatitis that may 
last for months, and it does not respond well to symptomatic 
medication. Tedania sp. and Lissodendoryx sp. have also been 
reported to cause an irritating dermatitis, but of shorter dura-
tion.243 Ice packs and cooling solutions, with camphor and 
menthol, have been reported to ameliorate the irritated skin, 
and hot water and alcohol are to be avoided as they intensify 
the agitation.194
Freshwater sponges such as the pipera-coora (Ephydatia sp.) 
from Australia and Spongilla lacustris from Europe may 
cause irritation during handling, and undefined substances 
from both were found to cause diarrhea, respiratory distress, 
and death in animals.243
15.5.8.2  Vertebrate Marine and Freshwater 
Animal Toxins and Venoms
Toxins are present in some of the marine creatures most famil-
iar to humans (Table 15.5). Whether it is a stingray in sandy 
beach waters, a catfish caught on the fishing line, or a sea snake 
swimming in a coral reef, these are all descriptions for which 
we can easily conjure up images. However, these conjured 
images are linked with the fact that stingrays, venomous fish, 
and sea snakes are the marine vertebrates most responsible for 
significant human envenomations.
Marine animals that are considered poisonous generally 
possess an acquired toxicity as a result of ingesting other 
toxin-producing organisms, while venomous animals manu-
facture their venoms in a distinctive venom gland primarily 
from endogenously produced toxins and deliver their venom 
via barbs, fangs, spines, or teeth that cause trauma in addition 
to the toxicity associated with venom. Toxins from vertebrate 
venomous animals tend to be heat labile, high-molecular–
mass proteins. Poisons, in contrast, are primarily low-molec-
ular-mass, heat-stable substances, and the poisonous animals 
involved lack a toxin delivery system.
15.5.8.3  Chordata, Marine and Freshwater 
Venomous Vertebrates
15.5.8.3.1  Chondrichthyes
Sharks from only two genera—Heterodontus and Squalus—
are known to be venomous (Table 15.5), and they are 
sometimes described as shark-like fish of the subclass 
Elasmobranchii.281 Although it is likely that envenomations 
by these sharks are more frequent than reported, literature 
that documents the consequences of envenomation by either 
genus is lacking. Envenoming by these sharks usually occurs 
to fishermen as a result of a dermal puncture by their venom-
ous spines. They have two sharp spines with luminal venom 
ducts, and no associated integumentary sheath enclosure, that 
contain a whitish mass with venom-producing vacuolated 
cells. A single spine is located immediately in front of the 
anterior dorsal fin, and immediately in front of the posterior 
dorsal fin.222 Spines of the Port Jackson Shark (Heterodontus 
portusjacksoni), which is native to coastal regions of south-
ern Australia, inflict rough-edged lesions with venom delivery 
resulting in muscle weakness of the envenomated append-
age.282 Envenomations by spiny dogfishes, including the short-
nose spurdog Squalus megalops found primarily off the coasts 
of Southern Australia and South Africa and Squalus cubensis 
from the western Atlantic (known in Brazil as cacao-bagre), 
cause immediate and intense pain at the site of spine puncture, 
followed by erythema, edema, and localized keratosis that can

790
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
persist for 2 weeks.283 Shark spine injuries have the obvious 
trauma component that accompanies venom effects and have 
been treated with wound irrigation, hot water submersion, and 
antibiotics.192,281
Stingrays are elasmobranchs that are represented by at least 
one genus in nearly all oceans and seas, and the fresh waters of 
Central and South America. Their dorsoventrally compressed 
bodies are the perfect design for their cryptic cover while 
resting in shallow water. The tail extends from the flattened, 
tetrahedral-shaped body, making them appear like a kite with 
an attached balancing tail. The tail may possess a single spine 
or multiple spines with highly serrated edges. Their body size 
may range from centimeters to greater than 3 m, and their 
barb-like spines have highly serrated edges.281 Spine morphol-
ogy varies considerably between different genera and species. 
The venom is contained within specialized secretory cells 
inside an integumental sheath that surrounds the caudal spine.
There is little known about the specific chemical make-up 
of freshwater or marine stingray venoms, but they are ther-
molabile.284 Stingray venoms of the genera Potamotrygon and 
Urobatis have been reported to cause cardiocirculatory toxicity 
as a result of direct effects on the myocardium,284,285 and neuro-
toxic effects have also been reported.286 Recent studies of fresh-
water stingray venom from Potamotrygon falkneri revealed 18 
different venom components, with molecular weights ranging 
from 12 to 130 kDa, and the venom was shown to exhibit casei-
nolytic, gelanolinolytic, and hyaluronidase activities.287
Envenomation occurs via the tail whipping the spine for-
ward and upward into a predator or other unfortunate victim, 
when the stingray is alarmed, frightened, or stepped on. This 
causes the simultaneous rupturing of the sheath and secre-
tory cells to release the venom. There may be considerable 
mechanical tissue trauma at the site of laceration or puncture 
resulting in severe bleeding, and the venom can add further 
insult by intensifying severe localized pain, erythema, edema, 
skin necrosis, and ulceration. Systemic symptoms of dizzi-
ness, fever, migraine, myalgia, and vomiting have occurred 
from freshwater stingray envenomations.287 Victims of marine 
stingray envenomation have died of cardiovascular failure,288 
and in one case, the reported cause of death was the result of 
cardiac tamponade and myocardial necrosis, following punc-
ture of the thoracic cavity by the stingray spine.289
Stingray envenomation is typically inflicted on the lower 
extremities of humans, and treatment may include an immedi-
ate attempt in pain reduction by immersion of the affected area 
in hot water (up to 45°C). The wound area may also be anes-
thetized with 1% lidocaine and irrigated with povidone-iodine, 
followed by prophylaxis for infection and tetanus. The wound 
should be carefully examined to confirm that no residual spine 
fragments or other debris remain, as the removal of these in a 
timely fashion reduces pain and secondary complications.287 
Significant wounds should not be primarily closed but allowed 
to heal through secondary intention. Severe cases of envenom-
ation, usually those involving puncture of the upper torso, may 
require careful radiographic evaluation of the puncture site, 
and advanced life-support measures.289
15.5.8.3.2  Osteichthyes
There are 22,000 extant species of fish, accounting for half of 
the world’s vertebrate population,290 but only a few hundred 
species are venomous.291 It has been speculated that the evolu-
tion of a venom apparatus in a large number of these fish is a 
result of their non-migratory, slow moving habits, and their 
liking of sheltered shallow-water environments.292Venomous 
bony fishes are primarily found in tropical oceans but also exist 
in some temperate oceans and seas. The venoms of bony fishes 
are produced, maintained, and delivered by anatomical struc-
tures that are similar across all species. This involves venom

production by specific secretory glands within an integumen-

in conjunction with fins of the anal, caudal, dorsal, opercular,

a spine pierces tissue, the integumental sheath’s integrity is 
disrupted and the venom is delivered into the wound.293 The 
inflicted spine injury from many species may be more trau-
matic and damaging than the effects of the venom.
Fish venoms are used almost exclusively for self-defense, 
and as such, there is a degree of similarity in their chemical 
and pharmacological properties.294 Like the venom of their 
cartilaginous relatives, bony fish venoms also exhibit labile 
sensitivities, their stability, and activity being easily abolished 
by fluctuations in temperature, heat, and pH.295Venom toxins 
identified for piscine fish range from 15 kDa296 to 800 kDa.295 
The pharmacological effects of piscine venoms, collectively, 
are represented by three major toxic effects in vivo: (1) seri-
ous cardiovascular toxicity, (2) neuromuscular activity, and 
(3) cytolytic activity. Mechanistically, it is thought that both 
the cardiovascular and neuromuscular effects are the result 
of the potent cytolytic property and that a single toxin in the 
venom is responsible for all these activities.294However, it is 
important to note that most venom research does not test for 
effects directly applicable to human envenoming and that the 
finding of any given activity in a venom does not mean that 
the observed effect will occur or be significant in envenomed 
humans. Thus, the presence of cardiotoxins does not imply 
envenomed humans will necessarily suffer cardiotoxicity, and, 
similarly, the presence of “neurotoxins” does not imply that 
neurotoxicity is likely.
Not surprisingly, given the pharmacological and toxicity 
similarities for most piscine fish venoms, the treatment of their 
envenomations is also similar. Early initiation of treatment is 
important and involves the immediate irrigation of the wound, 
removal of any easily retrieved material, and immersion of the 
wound in hot water (not so hot as to cause burns, up to 450C). 
Analgesic drugs and the use of local anesthetic at the wound 
site should be considered. If specific antivenom is available, 
it should be administered, but this is only available for the 
Australian stonefish, though it may be effective for envenom-
ations by related species. Timely follow-up with wound care is 
important to rule out the possibility of retained solid materials 
in the wound and evaluate for secondary infection or necro-
sis. Marine wound infections, when they occur, are commonly 
polymicrobial, and appropriate antibiotic administration 
should be initiated if symptoms are warranting.297
A few of the more well-known groups and genera of bony 
piscine fish will now be discussed in more detail.
Catfish—Catfish are an easily recognizable whiskered fish, 
and approximately 1,000 different species inhabit fresh and salt 
waters worldwide. They have dorsal and pectoral fin spines, 
which may or may not be edged with sharp serrations, that 
are associated with a venom-containing integumental sheath. 
These spinal fins are usually kept flush with their body; how-
ever, when distressed, the spines become locked in an extended 
position to inflict their wound.222 Although there are multiple 
genera associated with toxic reactions (Table 15.4), the main 
adverse effect of their inflicted stings is related to the trauma 
associated with the puncture of tissue and localized pain.
In North America, catfish of the genera Ictalurus and 
Noturus are responsible for most stings.298 Venom of the white 
catfish, Ictalurus catus, has been reported to contain from two 
to eight lethal factors (in an animal model), and two dermone-
crotic factors that are moderately heat stable.299
In the Indo-Pacific region, the Arabian Gulf catfish (Arius 
thalassinus) and the striped catfish (Plotosus lineatus) are 
notorious for their ability to inflict extremely painful wounds 
and deliver several proteinaceous toxins, known as crino-
toxins, in the stinging process. Both the species also contain 
these toxins in their skin secretions. A. thalassinus venom 
contains components that can induce vasoconstriction in 
vascular smooth muscle300 and may be related to muscarinic 
receptor activity.301 The skin toxin of P. lineatus has yielded 
a hemolysin, two edema-forming factors, and two lethal fac-
tors.302 Venoms of two Indian catfish species, Heteropneustes 
fossilis and Plotosus canius, contain or cause the release of 
prostaglandins that induce smooth muscle contractions in 
experimental models303 and have caused severe neurotoxic 
symptoms when injected into mice.304 A lethal component 
of C. canius venom, toxin-PC, acts presynaptically, blocking 
neurotransmitter release resulting in neuromuscular block-
ade.305 Given the array of catfish venom toxinological effects, 
it is unfortunate that there is little reporting of envenomation 
cases that would provide a basis to develop optimal clinical 
management.
Catfish stings and envenomation most often occur to fisher-
men who are actively pursuing the capture of fish. Symptoms 
of bleeding, stinging, throbbing sensation, intense pain, and 
even peripheral neuropathy may result.306 Treatment com-
prises judicious wound care and removal of any residual spine 
fragments in the wound, and management of pain, which may 
include pain reduction by immersion of the affected area in 
hot water (up to 45°C). Secondary infections are a frequent 
complication with catfish injuries, and antibiotic therapy may 
be necessary.307,308
Scorpionfishes—Scorpionfish is a somewhat vague name 
that is frequently used to encompass a broad general taxonom-
ical classification when referring to any one of a number of 
genera of anatomically similar fish (Table 15.4). They are com-
monly known throughout their wide range, as they are pres-
ent in all oceans of the earth, but venomous species are most 
prevalent in tropical waters. Out of this diverse group, approxi-
mately 80 species have been reported to possess venom toxins 
or have caused envenomation to humans.309 They are easily 
recognized due to their unique and often ornate appearance, 
an attribute that also makes them desirable to collectors. Some 
species, such as the lionfish (Pterois volitans) and zebrafish 
(Dendrochirus zebra) of the Indo-West Pacific oceans and 
the Red Sea, are highly sought after by aquarists.310 This has 
resulted in a major extension of geographic distribution, with 
the lionfish in particular having established feral populations 
in a number of regions, such as the Mediterranean Sea, where 
it is displacing native species and posing a new risk to fisher-
man and others using this sea.
Scorpionfish venom glands are associated with spines repre-
sented in the configuration of three anal spines, 10–15 dorsal 
spines, and two pelvic spines. Venom extracts from lionfish (P. 
volitans) and soldier fish (Gymnapistes marmoratus) increase

792
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
intracellular calcium in cultured neurons, possibly as a result 
of pore formation in cell membranes. This increase in intra-
cellular calcium is associated with increased cell death due 
to cellular swelling and neurite loss.311 In support of the role 
of calcium in toxicity is the observation of both adrenergic 
and cholinergic cardiotoxic effects from P. volitans312 and 
G. marmoratus313 crude venoms in animal tissue and cell 
culture models. Cardiovascular effects of Scorpaena guttata 
venom in rat hearts are biphasic with early reduction in chro-
notropic and inotropic effects, later followed by an increase in 
these effects. Thus, the venom acts on muscarinic and adreno-
receptors, respectively, and this may be the result of endog-
enous autocoid release.314The venom of the bullrout (Notesthes 
robusta), a species native to eastern Australia, contains a 
169.8–174.5 kDa-protein (nocitoxin) that is capable of induc-
ing pain in humans via polymodal nociceptor stimulation.315 
Collectively, the cardiotoxic and neurotoxic effects suggest 
that scorpionfish venoms act both pre- and postsynaptically 
to induce cell membrane depolarization and that this action is 
dependent on extracellular divalent cations.294
Envenomation by scorpionfishes nearly always results in pro-
found pain, a possibility of significant swelling, and systemic 
effects such as nausea, vomiting, diaphoresis, dyspnea, hypo-
tension, weakness, syncope, and collapse have been reported. 
Systemic complications are possibly related to the intensity of 
pain rather than the toxic effects of the venom.316,317 Treatment 
of envenomation is supportive as described earlier; however, 
stonefish antivenom has been implicated as a potential therapy 
for lionfish and soldierfish envenomations.318
Stonefish—Synanceia spp. (sometimes referred to using the 
misspelling Synanceja spp.) are one of the most researched 
venomous fish genera, and their venoms have yielded unique 
toxins with a variety of pharmacological activities.319 The dif-
ferent Synanceia species are native to waters throughout the 
Indo-Pacific oceans and are known by a variety of local names, 
such as dornorn or wary-phoul in Australia, devilfish in Java, 
gofu in Polynesia, and sherova in East Africa; however, the 
name “stonefish” is almost universal.281 These slightly gro-
tesque but interesting looking fish are morphologically remi-
niscent of an algae-, moss-, or barnacle-covered rock, and as 
such, their looks complement their cryptic behavior. Thus, 
they lay on the bottom waiting to ambush unsuspecting prey 
and may also be accidentally stepped on by an unobservant 
human wading in the shallow waters they frequent.
Stonefish venom glands are closely associated with the 13 
dorsal spines, which appear to be covered with rough, thick 
skin. Two lateral grooves run the length of the spine and the 
venom glands reside in the mid-third of the spine shaft. The 
glands are sheathed in a fibrous, multilayered capsule that 
is vascularized and has a nerve network. Stonefish release 
a milky white dermal secretion, independent of their spine 
venom, when handled or agitated. Crude, stonefish venom is 
heat-labile.320
The venoms of three species, S. horrida, S. trachynis, and 
S. verrucosa, contain catecholamines,321 exhibit various enzy-
matic activities,322 and increase vascular permeability.320A 
lethal toxin known as stonustoxin (SNTX) has been isolated 
from the venom of the Indian stonefish, S. horrida.323 SNTX 
is a member of the perforin superfamily of pore-forming 
cytolytic proteins, and it is comprised of two large subunits 
(stonustoxin-α and stonustoxin-β), each with a molecular 
mass of ~79 kDa. SNTX induces cardiovascular hypotensive 
responses in rats, which are thought to be nitric oxide medi-
ated,324 and it inhibits muscle cell contraction by acting directly 
on myocytes.325 Note that an identical toxin was isolated from 
the venom of the estuarine stonefish, S. trachynis, and named 
trachynilysin,326 but this species was subsequently reclassified 
as a synonym of S. horrida. Homologous toxins (verrucotoxin 
and neoverrucotoxin) have been isolated from the venom of the 
reef stonefish S. verrucosa. These perforin-like toxins are the 
most bioactive components of their respective crude venoms, 
account for all of the lethal activity, and suggest that stonefish 
venoms comprise a relatively small number of toxins.312 Their 
pharmacological and toxicological effects in various cell and 
animal models may be useful in biomedical research, but it is 
difficult to clearly define specific functions that impact human 
health.
Clinical features of stonefish envenoming in humans involve 
local tissue trauma with gradually intensifying pain at the 
spine penetration site. Swelling and erythema may progress 
for up to 24 hours. Systemically, the key serious complications 
are cardiovascular related, with hypotension, bradycardia, 
pulmonary edema, arrhythmia, and cardiovascular collapse 
associated with neurologic symptoms of muscle weakness, 
paralysis, and convulsions, potentially leading to death,327 but 
these reported specific systemic effects are exceptionally rare. 
Although death has been reported,328 there may be inaccura-
cies with the documentation, and stonefish envenoming should 
not be a life-threatening event. It is the local effect, particu-
larly the excruciating pain and, uncommonly, necrosis, that 
dominates the clinical picture.
Treatment of stonefish envenomated victims is support-
ive and symptomatic, and specific antivenom produced in 
Australia by CSL Limited is effective in reversing many of 
the severe effects of venom toxicity.329 This antivenom might 
also provide paraspecific coverage against the toxic effects 
of G. marmoratus and P. volitans venoms based on animal 
studies, but this has not been tested in humans.318 Anecdotally, 
this antivenom may also be effective against a variety of other 
scorpaenid fish stings, including the bullrout, but all such use 
would be “off label”. Immersion in hot water, up to 45°C, 
remains the recommended first aid for all venomous stinging 
fish injuries, including stonefish, and is also used in a hospital 
setting as a first step in managing the severe local pain caused 
by these stings.
Toadfish—Thalassophryne spp. are similar to stonefish in 
behavior and appearance, inhabiting the bottoms of coastal 
waters worldwide, and some species can use their swim blad-
ders to sing their watery trill. They have two dorsal and two 
opercular spines connected to venom glands, and their venom 
is delivered via rupture of the integumentary spinal sheath 
upon stinging281 Venom is composed of heat-labile, 18–97 kDa 
bioactive toxins that alter arteriolar and venous microvascu-
lar hemodynamics and cause myonecrosis.330 Envenomation 
to humans results in severe pain, dizziness, and edema with 
a rapidly ensuing tissue necrosis.331 Treatment consists of 
immersion of the wound in hot water, analgesics or local anes-
thetics, and supportive wound care.332

793
Plant and Animal Toxins
Weeverfish—there are only nine extant species of weeverfish 
in two genera, Echiichthys (one species) and Trachinus (eight 
species). They predominantly inhabit the flat sandy bottoms of 
European coastal waters, the Black and Mediterranean seas, 
Chile’s Pacific coast, and South African waters.222 Although 
multiple species have been identified, two European species 
are well known for their toxic stings, the greater weeverfish 
(Trachinus draco; formerly Echiichthys draco) and the lesser 
weeverfish (Echiichthys vipera).222 As implied by their name, 
greater weeverfish are large and can reach a half-meter in 
length. Weeverfish venom delivery is accomplished with any 
of the five dorsal, or two opercular, spines that have a thin-
walled integumentary sheath near their base that covers the 
venom gland.281
Weeverfish toxins are heat labile, but a few intact toxins have 
been isolated, including a 105-kDa protein with hemolytic 
activity (dracotoxin) from T. draco,333 and a 324-kDa protein 
composed of four subunits (trachinine) from T. vipera.334 The 
crude venom of T. draco contains histamine, catecholamines, 
exhibits cholinesterase activity, and produces hypertension 
with subsequently sustained hypotension in cats, demonstrat-
ing a biphasic effect.333
Weeverfish stings vary widely in their severity from the usual 
symptoms of intense pain with local inflammation to extremely 
rare cases of secondary infections associated with fatality.335 
Generally, symptoms resolve in about a week, and treatment is 
the same as that used for other Osteichthyes envenomations.297
Fangblennies—Fangblennies (Tribe Nemophini, genus 
Meiacanthus) are the only fish with an oral venom-delivery 
system. Their paired venom glands are located in the lower 
jaw, and they are connected to grooved canine teeth through 
which venom is delivered to deter predatory fish that attempt 
to eat the fangblenny. The venom is potently hypotensive to 
rats, but bites to humans are generally painless and inconse-
quential.336 The venom appears to be relatively simple, with 
PLA2, neuropeptide Y, and enkephalins that target δ-opioid 
receptors being major venom components.336
Rabbitfish—Siganus spp. are native to the Indo-Pacific 
oceans and seas. They have 13 venom-delivering dorsal spines, 
and the associated venom glands are located within anterolat-
eral grooves that run the length of the spine. The four pelvic 
spines are similar in structural anatomy. This venom-delivery 
system is similar to that of the lionfish. Like the sabre-tooth 
blenny, little is known about their venom, but stings are said to 
be comparable to those of other similar scorpionfish.281
Stargazer—fish in the family Uranoscopidae are known as 
“stargazers” as their small eyes are located on the flat dorsal 
aspect of their cuboidal-shaped head, making them appear to 
be gazing upward toward the water’s surface. The family com-
prises about 50 species across eight genera. Situated behind the 
opercles and immediately above the pectoral fins on each side 
of their body is a short but sharp stubby spine, with a poorly 
defined venom gland that surrounds its base. Knowledge con-
cerning the chemical composition and pharmacological activ-
ity of the venom is lacking. Crude extracts from stargazer 
venom glands were not toxic when injected into laboratory 
animals, perhaps due to instability of the venom toxins when 
removed from the venom gland. Treatment of stargazer enven-
omation should follow that of other piscine envenomations.337
Boxfish 
and 
Trunkfish—Lactophrys 
(trunkfish) 
and 
Ostracion (boxfish) produce toxins in mucous skin secretions 
rather than deliver them via a specialized apparatus. Thus, 
they are poisonous rather than venomous. They are native to 
the Indo-Pacific and their toxins are used as a defense against 
predators such as sharks and other fish.281 The toxins are only 
dangerous when ingested, so there is no harm to humans from 
handling the fish aside from mild dermal irritation.185 The pri-
mary toxin produced by both genera is pahutoxin, a choline 
chloride ester of 3-acetoxy palmitic acid that disrupts phos-
pholipid membranes and has surfactant properties.338
15.5.8.3.3  Reptilia
Sea snakes navigate coral reefs and oceans in the world’s sub-
tropical and tropical regions but have been observed thousands 
of miles distant to these regions, even in the absence of oce-
anic currents or foul weather. Thus, they are the most widely 
dispersed venomous reptiles on Earth.339
Sea snakes are members of the snake family Elapidae (cobra 
family) and they are thought to have evolved from terrestrial 
ancestors in the Australian region that adopted an aquatic, then 
marine, lifestyle. Some subsequently re-entered the terrestrial 
environment and evolved into part of the current Australian 
terrestrial Elapid venomous snake fauna. Their morphological 
appearance and physical body structure, although similar to 
terrestrial snakes, are usually distinguished by the presence of 
laterally compressed paddle-like tails. Their physiology allows 
them to dive to depths of 100 m, and stay submerged for greater 
than 3 hours, but they rely on atmospheric air to survive. Up to 
17 genera have been recorded, but recent taxonomic research 
suggests that many of these genera should be subsumed into 
genus Hydrophis (Table 15.5), though the sea kraits, genus 
Laticauda, remain distinct, reflecting their different biology. 
Sea snakes average about 1 m in length, although some spe-
cies (Laticauda colubrina) approach 3–4 m. Sea snakes usu-
ally have short (<4 mm), conical, fixed-position fangs, and the 
fang length varies between species, possibly reflecting the type 
of prey eaten. There are also numerous other teeth that can be 
similar in size to fangs. The fangs are luminal and interface 
with ducts that connect the fangs to elongated venom glands 
lying in the upper jaw on each side of the head. Venom glands 
are fibrous-encapsulated and attach to adductor muscles that 
contract to express venom through the venom duct network 
and into prey.340
Sea snake venoms contain an array of protein toxin types, 
and they are extremely toxic, being amongst the most potent 
of any venomous vertebrate. The venom yield delivered from 
most sea snake species ranges from 1 to 10 mg, but species 
such as Hydrophis (Astrotia) stokesii can deliver greater 
than 20 mg, with as little as 0.04 mg/kg being fatal to mice. 
Venom dose-response studies suggest that the beaked sea 
snake (Hydrophis schistosa) can deliver enough venom to 
kill more than 50 humans.340 Sea snake venom contains a 
variety of disulfide-stabilized peptide toxins as well an array 
of enzymes, including acetylcholinesterase, hyaluronidase, 
leucine aminopeptidase, 5′-nucleotidase, phosphomonoes-
terase, phosphodiesterase, and PLA.341 Purified toxins have 
been demonstrated to be heat stable, and both acid and base 
tolerant.342

794
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
Envenomation of humans by sea snakes is a serious medi-
cal concern. Sea snake fangs, being small, do not make deep 
punctures, and fang marks may not be easily visualized. 
Additionally, the bite is frequently asymptomatic, not even 
felt by the victim, and local symptoms are usually absent. 
Envenomation is manifested by one of several different clini-
cal presentations; (1) predominantly descending flaccid paral-
ysis, mediated by postsynaptic neurotoxins; (2) predominantly 
myotoxicity with widespread destruction of skeletal muscle 
and consequent myoglobinuria, sometimes secondary AKI 
with the potential for hyperkalemic cardiotoxicity; and (3) 
a variable combination of both descending flaccid paralysis 
and systemic myolysis. The descending flaccid paralysis first 
manifests as bilateral ptosis, diplopia, partial then complete 
ophthalmoplegia, dysarthria, dysphagia, loss of protection 
of the upper airway, then muscle fasciculations, muscle pain, 
shock, trismus, and full respiratory paralysis.343 The myoly-
sis manifests as muscle pain, secondary muscle weakness 
and tenderness, myoglobinuria, and secondary complications, 
potentially including AKI and hyperkalemic cardiotoxic-
ity.344,345 Neurotoxic symptoms may be deferred for up to 
3 hours, often causing the victim to delay seeking medical 
attention. Fatalities usually result from paralysis of the dia-
phragm progressing to respiratory arrest, and lack of timely 
treatment, or from secondary effects of systemic myolysis, 
particularly renal failure and hyperkalemic cardiotoxicity. A 
pressure immobilization method of first aid has been found to 
be beneficial and may be of major importance when a victim is 
remote to a medical facility.343 Cardiopulmonary support is an 
effective therapy, and sea snake antivenom (Seqirus Limited, 
Parkville, VIC, Australia) is a definitive therapy. Importantly, 
the antivenom appears to be effective for envenomation by all 
sea snake species, indicative of similarities in the venoms of 
different sea snake species.346
15.5.9  Terrestrial Animal Toxins
The land-dwelling animals of the world are associated with 
all continental landmasses, although the diversity and num-
bers of different species are the greatest in tropical regions. 
As the latitudinal distance from the equator becomes greater, 
the diversity and number of animal species decrease. Thus, an 
inverse relationship exists between the number of species and 
distance from tropical climates, a relationship that is paralleled 
with respect to the diversity and number of venomous and poi-
sonous terrestrial animals. However, as humans have increas-
ingly traveled the earth, there has been increased dispersal of 
species to regions beyond their natural range. Additionally, the 
expansion of the world’s population into natural environments 
has led to an ever-increasing interface between these animals 
and humans, and thus an increasing frequency of human 
envenomations. The frequency of encounters with these toxin-
producing animals is probably greatest in rural areas of tropi-
cal third world countries, where the availability of modern 
medical treatment may be non-existent or limited.
15.5.9.1  Terrestrial Invertebrate Animal 
Toxins and Venoms
The terrestrial invertebrates capable of delivering toxins 
via bites, stings, or bristle and hair contact are members of 
Arthropoda, the largest phylum in the animal kingdom. These 
relatively small animals are responsible for more human 
envenomations than the sum of envenomations from all other 
animal phyla, though with a far lower fatality rate and abso-
lute numbers of fatal cases compared to venomous snakes.291 
Arthropods all have legs and walk, run, or fly, and there are 
three main classes of toxin- or venom-producing members 
(Table 15.6).
15.5.9.2  Arthropoda, Terrestrial 
Venomous Invertebrates
15.5.9.2.1  Arachnida
Arachnids have eight legs compared to the six possessed by 
insects and the much larger number on centipedes (15–177 
pairs). They do not have antennae or wings but inhabit a wide 
variety of ecosystems. Their venoms represent amazing phar-
macological arsenals that are capable of subduing prey and 
deterring predators. Only a minority of arachnids pose a sig-
nificant danger to human health, the latter including selected 
species of scorpions, spiders, and ticks.
