15.5.9.2.2  Scorpions
Scorpions are the oldest terrestrial venomous animal, having 
evolved more than 400 million years ago.347 They are repre-
sented by more than 2,000 extant species (Table 15.6) that 
inhabit both arid and wet ecosystems. Scorpions appear like 
small lobsters carrying a thin tail with an inverted hook arched 
over their back. Their body comprises three distinct sections, 
but the head and thorax are joined to form a cephalothorax, 
which has pincers, or pedipalps, used for restraining prey and 
feeding themselves. The pincer-like claws are a modification 
of the first set of legs, and the remaining three pairs of legs are 
used for walking. The terminal distal segment known as the tel-
son houses a pair of venom glands that are each connected via 
a duct to separate orifices in the stinger. Scorpions sting with 
this apparatus and deliver their venom into a variety of prey and 
predators, including other arthropods. Scorpions are fossorial 
and reclusive in nature, tending to be nocturnally active.
Scorpion venoms are a complex cocktail of typically 50 to 
several hundred low-molecular weight (<10 kDa) peptide tox-
ins, often rich in disulfide bonds. It has been estimated that 
there are at least 100,000 unique peptides present in scorpion 
venoms.201 These peptides have a wide array of pharmacologi-
cal and physiological actions, and some are highly specific for 
crustaceans, insects, or mammals. Some scorpion-venom tox-
ins with selective insecticidal activity have been investigated 
as potential bioinsecticides.348
Scorpion venoms are rich in peptide toxins that modulate 
the activity of ion channels. In particular, many scorpion 
toxins have been isolated that act on NaV channels349 or KV 
channels.350 Short-chain scorpion toxins contain 23–47 amino 
acid residues and primarily target KV channels.351 whereas the 
long-chain toxins contain 58–76 residues and primarily tar-
get NaV channels.352 The scorpion potassium channel toxins 
have been divided into α-, β-, and γ-subfamilies based on their 
amino sequence and phylogenetic relatedness.351
Scorpion NaV channel toxins are designated as α- or β-toxins 
depending on their binding site and mode of action.352 While 
the short-chain KV channel toxins are pore blocking inhibitors 
that sterically prevent K+ entry through the channel, the long-
chain NaV channel toxins are gating modifiers that bind to one 
or more of the channel’s four VSDs (VSDI–VSDIV) to allo-
sterically modulate channel activity. β-Toxins bind to VSDII 
and activate the channel by shifting the voltage-dependence 
of channel activation to more hyperpolarized potentials, while 
the α-toxins bind to VSDIV and impede channel inactiva-
tion, which results in a persistent current. Some of these NaV 
channel toxins have selective toxicity toward crickets, crabs, 
squids, and triatomid bugs, with no apparent toxicity to mam-
mals, whereas others are potentially lethal to humans.
Scorpion envenomation is a serious public health problem in 
India, Mexico, Africa, the Middle East, and South America, 
but relatively few species are of medical concern. However, 
in countries like Tunisia where up to 40,000 scorpion stings 
occur annually, resulting in 20–40 deaths, or Mexico with 
>300,000 cases/year, medical treatment of scorpion envenom-
ation is important and fairly common.353 Scorpions will sting 
when laid on, sat on, stepped on, or agitated by squeeze-con-
tact with clothing and shoes, and they can deliver a painful 
sting with their telson. Stings frequently occur in the evening 
hours when people walk around in bare feet. Although adult 
humans may suffer severe complications with scorpion stings, 
it is children that suffer the greatest mortality.354,355
Scorpion stings of medical importance can be divided 
into two groups: (1) neuroexcitatory envenoming, often akin 
to a catecholamine storm and by far the most common form 
of scorpion envenoming caused by many Buthid species; (2) 
cytolytic envenoming, largely restricted to the Iranian scor-
pion, Hemiscorpius lepturus. For neuroexcitatory envenom-
ing, given the pharmacological properties of scorpion venom, 
it is not surprising that stings, depending on the genus and spe-
cies involved, can cause local pain, autonomic and CNS com-
plications, seizures, and cardiopulmonary dysfunction,354 with 
shock and pulmonary edema being responsible for most fatali-
ties.356 For cytolytic envenoming by H. lepturus, the clinical 
features are quite different. The sting may be minor, some-
times not even felt. Pain develops later, in some cases only as 
the stung area develops progressive necrosis, a process that 
can take days to evolve. There is a concomitant systemic ill-
ness which, in severe cases, can include intravascular hemoly-
sis, DIC, and multi-organ failure leading to death, particularly 
in children.
Medical management strategies, which vary with different 
global geographic regions, have revolved around treatment 
being performed in medical intensive care facilities, the use of 
various pharmacotherapies, and the administration of specific 
antivenom.354 The use of antivenoms and their effectiveness 
have been historically controversial, with treatment in some 
regions involving large antivenom doses, with resultant ana-
phylaxis and delayed serum sickness.356 However, in regions 
such as Mexico, the southern USA, and large parts of South 
America, antivenom has been the mainstay of clinical treat-
ment for decades, with generally good results and a notable 
reduction in mortality, with a similar experience now emerg-
ing in India as scorpion antivenom becomes available there. 
Because scorpion neuroexcitatory envenoming develops 
rapidly after a sting, antivenom should be given as soon as 
possible, with any delay reducing effectiveness. For cytolytic 
scorpion envenoming the role of antivenom is less clear—and 
early use is unlikely because of the delay in symptom pre-
sentation—but in Iran antivenom is considered the optimal 
treatment.
15.5.9.2.3  Spiders
Except for hymenopterans, spiders are the most speciose Order 
of venomous animals. More than 49,650 species have been 
described across 4,226 genera (for an up-to-date species count, 
see https://wsc.nmbe.ch/statistics/), but the number of extant 
species is thought to be >150,000.357 Spiders inhabit nearly all 
ecosystems, ranging from arid deserts to rainforests, and from 
the slopes of Mount Everest to life underwater.358 Spiders are 
masters of deception; they exist in a wide variety of shapes 
and colors and exhibit equally diverse behaviors. They have 
been documented to mimic different vegetation forms, insects, 
bird dung, twigs, and gastropods, and some like the crab spi-
ders can even change color to camouflage with their preferred 
flower hideouts.359 Their size ranges from the size of a pin 
head (Patu marplesi—Samoan moss spider) up to the 25 cm 
leg span of the goliath tarantula (Theraphosa blondi).358 Like

796
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
scorpions, their head and thorax are combined to form a ceph-
alothorax, but their abdomen (also known as the opisthosoma) 
is differentiated from this anatomical structure by a narrow 
restriction, or pedicel, making the abdomen pronounced. Most 
spiders have eight simple eyes (ocelli), but some have fewer, 
and some cave-dwelling species are blind. Eyesight is poor in 
most species, but some hunting spiders such as crab spiders 
(family Thomisidae), jumping spiders (family Salticidae), and 
net-casting spiders (family Deinopidae) have excellent vision 
and hunt by sight. The net-casting spiders are commonly 
known as ogre-faced ones due to their very large posterior 
median eyes, which provide excellent night vision.
Taxonomically, spiders are divided into two suborders, 
Mesothelae and Opisthothelae.358Mesothelae are comprised of 
less than 130 extant species; they are the sister group to all 
other living spiders and retain ancestral characters such as a 
segmented abdomen. The vast majority of extant spiders are 
found in the suborder Opisthothelae, which is in turn divided 
into the infraorders Mygalomorphae and Araneomorphae. Like 
mesothelids, mygalomorphs have paraxial fangs that move in 
an up-and-down fashion, they do not build elaborate webs, and 
they are mostly ambush predators that live in tube retreats or 
burrows. This group comprises only ~7% of all living spiders, 
but it contains some of the world’s most well-known spiders, 
including the tarantulas (family Theraphosidae), trapdoor spi-
ders (various families), and the potentially lethal Australian 
funnel-web spiders (Atracidae). However, most extant spiders 
are araneomorphs; they have smaller diaxial fangs that move 
laterally in a side-to-side fashion, and they often use silk in 
ingenious ways to capture prey, including the construction 
of sheets, orbs, domes, and nets. This group includes several 
well-known araneid spiders that build spectacular orb webs 
(e.g., the genus Nephila). While some mygalomorphs can live 
longer than 25 years, the lifespan of most araneomorphs is 
only 1–2 years.358
All spiders except the hackled orbweavers (family 
Uloboridae) are venomous and produce toxins in specialized 
venom glands. Spiders do not swallow prey following para-
lytic envenomation but rather extract prey contents using their 
siphoning stomachs. The digestive enzymes used in prey liq-
uefaction are not venom components but come from intestinal 
juices that are regurgitated onto the prey (a process known as 
“extra-oral digestion”). Most spider venoms are exceedingly 
complex, typically comprising a heterogeneous mixture of 
several hundred to several thousand compounds, including 
salts, small molecules such as amino acids, neurotransmit-
ters and acylpolyamines, linear cytolytic peptides, disulfide-
rich neurotoxic peptides (<10 kDa), and proteins and enzymes 
larger than 10 kDa.360–362 The venoms of the lethal widow 
spiders (Latrodectus species) are atypical in containing very 
large (110–140 kDa) presynaptic neurotoxins.363
Although spider and scorpion venoms evolved indepen-
dently, they share many pharmacological similarities. Both 
venoms are dominated by disulfide-rich peptides that primar-
ily target neuronal ion channels, especially presynaptic NaV, 
KV, and CaV channels. However, in an excellent example of 
functional convergence, they achieve this pharmacology via 
different molecular scaffolds. The venoms of spiders are 
dominated by peptides that adopt an inhibitor cystine knot, 
or knottin fold,364,365 whereas scorpions achieve many of the 
same pharmacologies using peptides that adopt a cystine-sta-
bilized α/β (CSαβ) fold.366
Although peptides are the dominant components of spider 
venoms, they also contain a variety of enzymes, including 
hyaluronidase, collagenase, PLA2, various proteases, and, in 
the case of sicariid spiders, sphingomyelinase D (SMaseD). It 
is thought that these enzymes facilitate the spread of peptide 
neurotoxins by degrading the extracellular matrix (hyaluroni-
dase, collagenase, proteases) and the underlying cell mem-
brane (PLA2, SMaseD).362 Many spider venoms also contain 
acylpolyamines that target postsynaptic ionotropic glutamate 
receptors and nAChRs.360,361
Spider venoms evolved primarily for prey capture, and 
therefore they are optimized to kill other arthropods and less 
frequently small invertebrates. Thus, despite their fearsome 
reputation, the vast majority of spiders (>99%) pose no risk to 
humans.367 Despite their generally smaller size, araneomorphs 
are responsible for most bites to humans and this group 
includes most of the potentially dangerous spider genera. In 
contrast to many insect bites and stings, spider envenomations 
do not induce allergic reactions.368
The spiders of greatest medical importance, all of which are 
capable of causing significant morbidity and mortality, are the 
Australian funnel-web spiders (genera Atrax, Hadronyche, and 
Illawarra), Brazilian wandering spiders (genus Phoneutria), 
recluse spiders (genus Loxosceles), and widow spiders (genus 
Latrodectus).369 All but the Australian funnel-web spiders 
(family Atracidae) are araneomorphs.
Australian funnel-web spiders (not to be confused with 
American funnel-web spiders, family Agelenidae) are believed 
to be the most dangerous spiders on Earth. Although bites are 
uncommon and severe envenomation is infrequent, enven-
omations can be life-threatening.370–372 Symptoms associ-
ated with severe envenomation include parasympathetic and 
sympathetic effects on both neuromuscular and cardiopulmo-
nary systems, causing a neuro-excitatory clinical picture with 
increased sweating, piloerection, hypertension, muscle fas-
ciculation, and pulmonary edema in severe cases, with later 
development of hypotension and terminal cardiac collapse in 
cases with life-threatening envenoming who survive the ear-
lier stage.371,373 These symptoms are due to a single peptide 
neurotoxin known as δ-hexatoxin (synonyms: δ-atracotoxin; 
robustoxin; versutoxin) that inhibits inactivation of presynap-
tic NaV channels and causes a modest hyperpolarizing shift in 
the voltage-dependence of channel activation, thereby induc-
ing spontaneous action potential generation and repetitive 
firing.374 Antivenom has profoundly altered the clinical pic-
ture and outcome; patients now receive antivenom early and 
mostly avoid more severe envenoming, with no fatalities in 
antivenom-treated cases since the introduction of a specific 
antivenom in 1981.369
Brazilian wandering spiders, also known as armed spiders 
(Aranha armadeiras in Brazilian Portuguese) or banana spi-
ders, are a significant problem in South America, especially in 
Brazil where ~4,000 bites are reported each year.375 Phoneutria 
venoms are rich in peptide neurotoxins, but those responsible 
for the symptoms of envenomation remain to be definitively 
identified.375 Envenomation can result in serious systemic

797
Plant and Animal Toxins
manifestations potentially leading to acute pulmonary edema, 
with at least one pediatric death reported.376 However, only 
0.5% of bites cause severe complications, and the most com-
mon symptoms of envenomation are intense local pain, edema, 
erythema, and paresthesia.375 Priapism is a particular sign of 
envenoming by these spiders in boys. Local anesthetics and 
oral analgesics are commonly used to treat pain, whereas anti-
venom is typically used only in severe envenomations where 
systemic manifestations are evident, mainly in children.375 
The Instituto Butantan in Brazil produces a polyvalent anti-
arachnid antivenom for use against bites by Phoneutria spp. 
and Loxosceles spp. spiders, as well as Tityus spp. scorpions.375
Recluse spiders (Loxosceles spp.) are capable of inflicting 
severe ulcerating and tissue-necrotizing bites (loxoscelism), 
and they are considered a serious hazard in Central and South 
America, as well as southeastern parts of the U.S. They have 
also appeared in Europe, Asia, South Africa, and Australia, 
most likely having been transported into some of these coun-
tries (notably Australia), and little is known of their bites in 
these settings.369 However, there is emerging evidence from 
southern Europe that loxoscelism is occurring in some areas 
as a previously unreported problem, though it is unclear if this 
is caused by endemic Loxosceles rufescens, or possibly other 
invading Loxosceles spp. Their venoms are unique among ven-
omous animals for harboring several isoforms of an enzyme 
known as SMaseD, the venom component responsible for its 
toxic effects.377 SMAseD (sometimes called phospholipase D) 
performs an unusual transphosphatidylation reaction in which 
the choline headgroup is cleaved from lysophosphatidylcho-
line and sphingomyelin in mammalian cell membranes, and 
the lipid is transformed into an unusual cyclic phospholipid.378
Although many spider bites are attributed to Loxosceles, 
they are rarely confirmed to be recluse bites, and misdiagno-
sis follows, particularly in North America.379 Bites frequently 
go unnoticed, as they are relatively painless, and victims often 
delay seeking medical attention. Envenomation by Loxosceles 
is dichotomous, causing either severe dermonecrosis or appear-
ing as a viscerocutaneous form.369 A necrotic lesion may take 
up to a week to present, and the resulting ulceration can be 
insidious and persist for months. Fever and fatigue may also 
be present. The viscerocutaneous symptoms of Loxosceles 
envenomation may be fulminant, with hemolysis, coagulopa-
thy, shock, renal failure, and multiple systems failure leading to 
death if untreated.372 The persistent nature of recluse spider bite 
lesions has led to considerable controversy as to the most effec-
tive treatment but, in general, thorough wound care following 
an early diagnosis leads to a favorable outcome.379 Therapies 
ranging from hyperbaric oxygen to nitroglycerin patches have 
been used with varying effectiveness.380 Antivenom appears to 
be effective in reducing mortality and clinical complications in 
cases of severe systemic envenoming in Brazil.381
Widow spiders (Latrodectus spp.) are found throughout the 
world, with as many as 40 extant species.369 Envenomation 
(latrodectism) can cause significant morbidity and has 
resulted in death, though this is very rare.382,383 Latrodectus 
venoms, and the clinical features following envenomation, 
are similar throughout all geographic regions.369 The venoms 
of widow spiders and their close relatives the false widow 
spiders (Steatoda spp.) are unique in containing a class of 
high-molecular mass (110–140 kDa) neurotoxins known as 
latrotoxins. The human envenomation syndrome is caused 
by α-latrotoxin, which stimulates massive neurotransmitter 
release from a variety of nerve terminals.360 The tissue around 
a bite does not react to envenomation, but local pain may be 
present. Severe skeletal muscle pain in the abdomen, back and 
chest, diaphoresis, hypertension, and cramping are symptoms 
requiring medical treatment.383 Envenomation by Steatoda 
spp. causes similar but less severe symptoms. Treatment may 
include parenteral benzodiazepines or opioids, and antivenoms 
are available in Australia, the U.S., and some other areas/coun-
tries. Antivenom, given intravenously, is beneficial in cases of 
severe envenomation.369,383 Antivenom to the Australian red-
back spider (Latrodectus hasselti) has also been used in cases 
of severe Steatoda envenomation.368 Recent claims in Australia 
that antivenom is ineffective for latrodectism are not consistent 
with global or Australian clinical experience and research, and 
antivenom remains the global standard of care for significant 
envenoming.
A few other spider genera are worthy of mention. Black house 
spiders (Badumna sp.), wolf spiders (Lycosa spp.), and yellow 
sac spiders Cheiracanthium mildei (sac spider) can cause pain 
and erythema, but these symptoms generally resolve without 
long-term consequences.369 The notion that Australian white-
tailed spiders (Lampona spp.) cause necrotic lesions384 has 
been thoroughly debunked, and the supposed toxicity of North 
American hobo spiders (Tegenaria spp.) is questionable.385
Tarantulas, due to their size and reputation, deserve a final 
word with respect to spiders. These spiders, which are more 
correctly known as theraphosids, consist of >1,000 species 
across 151 genera, and they are widely dispersed in semi-­
tropical, semi-temperate, and tropical regions around the world. 
Due to their notoriety, long lifespan, and ease of rearing, they 
have become a significant portion of the world’s pet trade, and 
multiple species have been afforded legal protection under the 
Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of 
Wild Fauna and Flora. Their large fangs deliver pharmacologi-
cally complex venoms that can induce rapid paralysis, even to 
vertebrates.386 However, despite their size, notoriety, and rapid-
acting venoms, they are generally harmless to humans. Bites to 
humans typically cause mild-to-severe pain, burning sensation, 
edema, erythema, itching, and joint stiffness. Severe envenom-
ations are well documented only for Poecilotheria spp., arbo-
real tarantulas native to Sri Lanka and India; almost 60% of all 
bites recorded for these spiders cause intense pain and muscle 
cramps lasting up to a week.367 Tarantula bite treatment has 
not been specifically defined, but symptomatic treatment with 
analgesics, antihistamines, topical steroids, and supportive care 
as needed have been used with success, even in cases of severe 
envenomation by Poecilotheria spp.387
It should be noted that the first line of defense for most New 
Word tarantulas is not venom, but urticating hairs or bristles 
that decorate the opisthosoma. When provoked, these tarantu-
las rub their hind legs against the opisthosoma to throw urti-
cating hairs in the direction of the attacker. The bristles can 
cause physical and chemical irritation if they embed in the 
attacker’s skin or eyes.388,389 The degree of irritation can range 
from mild in most cases to severe urticaria in the case of the 
Goliath tarantula (T. blondi).

798
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
15.5.9.2.4  Chilopoda
Centipedes evolved >400 million years ago and they are now 
globally distributed, being native to every continent except 
Antarctica.390 There are 3,500 described species across five 
extant 
Orders 
(Lithobiomorpha, 
Craterostigmomorpha, 
Geophilomorpha, Scutigeromorpha, and Scolopendromorpha). 
Centipedes are nocturnally active, fast-moving, have seg-
mented bodies with one pair of legs per segment, compound 
eyes, and range in length from 3 to 250 mm.391 They contain 
15–177 pairs of legs, and the first pair has been modified into 
venomous appendages known as poison claws, toxicognaths, 
maxillipeds, or, more correctly, forcipules.390 Prey are diverse 
and include arthropods, crustaceans, snails, amphibians, rep-
tiles, and even small mammals.390
Centipede venoms are complex mixtures of small molecules 
(e.g., serotonin and histamine), peptides, and larger proteins 
and enzymes. While centipede venoms contain an incredible 
diversity of disulfide-rich peptides, they are not as abundant 
as in spider, scorpion, and cone snail venoms.390 The diver-
gence of centipede venoms from other invertebrate venoms 
was apparent from early studies demonstrating the presence 
of high-molecular mass cardiotoxic392,393 and neurotoxic394 
components.
Envenomation by scolopendrid centipedes (Scolopen 
dromorpha: Scolopendridae) frequently results in intense 
pain and swelling but generally does not cause serious 
complications.395Most fatalities attributed to centipede stings 
are dubious, with a lack of supporting evidence, but there 
appear to be three substantiated deaths.390 A study of con-
firmed centipede envenomations in Australia showed that 
bites from Cormocephalus, Ethmostigmus, and Scolopendra 
species could cause significant pain, erythema, swelling, and 
itching, with Ethmostigmus and Scolopendra causing the most 
severe symptoms.396 Treatment with ice packs, analgesics, and 
hot water submersion have been effective interventions.396 The 
abundance of large pore-forming toxins and metalloprote-
ases in centipede venoms might explain the effectiveness of 
hot water immersion in alleviating pain and swelling, as these 
proteins appear to be very labile.390
15.5.9.2.5  Insecta
Insects are the most ubiquitous of all terrestrial animals, with 
>5 million extant species, and they inhabit every ecological 
niche on the planet.397 They have three hinged body parts 
(head, thorax, abdomen) covered with a chitinous exoskeleton, 
with six legs emerging from the thorax. Many possess wings, 
and larval forms such as caterpillars often have prolegs emerg-
ing from the abdomen in addition to the six true legs.
Venom use has evolved independently on at least 14 occa-
sions within Insecta,397 and these independent acquisitions 
have resulted in a wide diversity of venom-delivery devices, 
including posterior stingers (hymenopterans), dorsal sting-
ing hairs (caterpillars), and anterior needle-like proboscises 
(assassin bugs and robber flies) or mandibles (antlions). 
Although a plethora of insect species are vectors for a variety 
of infectious diseases, few have venoms that are of concern to 
humans. However, the venoms of many hymenopteran insects, 
including fire ants, honeybees, and vespid wasps, are highly 
immunogenic, and the allergic reactions resulting from enven-
omations by these insects are more clinically important than 
any complications associated with direct venom toxicity.398
15.5.9.2.5.1  Ants  Ants, bees, sawflies, and wasps are 
related members of the Order Hymenoptera, and most spe-
cies are venomous. However, only a few ant species are clini-
cally significant. While many people associate ant venoms 
with the production of formic acid that is sprayed for defense, 
this strategy is used only by formicine ants (subfamily 
Formicinae) that spray an acid “venom” via a modified sting 
known as an acidopore. Formic acid is of little consequence to 
humans, causing only mild dermal or ocular irritation. Ants 
of 13 of the other 15 subfamilies (71% of species) are venom-
ous,399 and the major functional components in these venoms 
are peptides.400,401
Fire ants comprise about 20 South American Solenopsis spe-
cies that have been accidentally introduced to remote regions, 
including the U.S., Australia, China, and Vietnam. Fire ant 
venom is not only rich in an alkaloid known as solenopsin, 
but it also contains peptides, enzymes (e.g., metalloproteinases 
and PLA2), and a variety of allergenic proteins, with the latter 
group capable of inducing serious IgE-mediated anaphylactic 
reactions in envenomated humans.402 Fire ants have an aggres-
sive stinging behavior with envenomation resulting in a wide 
range of effects, including dermal necrosis, edema, sterile pus-
tules (occurring several weeks post envenomation), urticaria, 
and in rare cases anaphylactic shock and fatality.403 The quan-
tity of venom delivered by fire ants ranges from 10 to 100 ng,404 
and the severity of envenomation appears to be dose-depen-
dent.405 Management of fire ant stings is not consistent, but 
antihistamines, topical corticosteroids, cold compresses, and 
antibacterials have been used.406
Only a few ant venoms have been studied in detail, and 
one of these is the venom of the Australian giant red bull 
ant Myrmecia gulosa.401 The venom is a relatively simple 
mixture of small peptides ranging in size from 1.5 to 9.2 kDa 
and a much lower abundance of enzymes such as hyal-
uronidase, esterase, phosphatases, and dipeptidyl peptidase 
IV.401,407 The peptide fraction includes both linear and disul-
fide-rich peptides as well as several glycosylated peptides, 
which are rare in animal venoms.401 The major venom pep-
tide in the venom of Myrmecia gulosa has both a defensive 
and predatory role—it induces nocifensive behavior (pain) 
in mice, and it incapacitates insect prey.401 The venom of the 
closely related jack jumper ant venom, Myrmecia pilosula, 
is strongly allergenic; it is responsible for more than 90% of 
ant venom anaphylaxis cases in Australia, as well as deaths 
in Tasmania.408 The allergic problems associated with re-
stings in victims became so serious that a major effort was 
made, and accomplished, for effective immunotherapy in 
preventing sting anaphylaxis.408 Other members of this ant 
genus also cause allergy problems but are generally less 
troublesome than M. pilosula.
15.5.9.2.5.2  Caterpillars  Caterpillars are the larval form of 
butterflies and moths (Order Lepidoptera). Familiar, captivat-
ing, and often beautiful, caterpillars are not all innocent. In 
most countries, they are a major economic agricultural pest as

799
Plant and Animal Toxins
a result of their defoliating appetites. Though most species are 
harmless, there are a few venom-producing species that should 
be treated with caution. The caterpillars of concern have either 
hairs (setae) or spines formed from evaginations of the cuticle 
on their slug-like or tubular-shaped bodies that are connected to 
venom-producing cells. The setae or spines are sometimes so pro-
fuse that they appear furry or brush-like. The caterpillars most 
likely to result in adverse envenomations are found in the families 
Saturniidae, Limacodidae, and Megalopygidae.397 Many noctu-
oid species, especially in the families Thaumetopoea, Erebidae, 
and Lymantriidae, produce urticating setae that introduce tox-
ins such as kallikrein-like serine proteases409 to cause pain and 
inflammation. Others, including those in the families Arctiinae 
and Zygaenidae, produce toxins or sequester them from food 
plants, which renders them poisonous.410–412
Lonomia obliqua (Saturniidae Moth) is a saturniid moth 
from southern Brazil and adjacent regions, including parts of 
Amazonian Peru, and it is the only moth for which the lar-
val caterpillar form is known to have caused human fatalities. 
In contrast, the adult moth is harmless. In addition to local 
symptoms that result from contact with the urticating bristles, 
Lonomia envenomation causes profound burning pain and can 
induce major hematological complications, notably coagulop-
athy. Severe envenomation from bristle contact with Lonomia 
results in a major hemorrhagic syndrome413 with a decline in 
prothrombin and accelerated thrombin generation, manifested 
clinically by ecchymosis, hematuria, with bleeding of the 
nose, skin, GIT, lungs, vagina, and pulmonary and intracere-
bral hemorrhage leading to death.414,415 This syndrome usually 
occurs from 6 to 72 hours post-envenomation. Other saturniids 
with venomous larvae include the Io moth (Automeris io) and 
buck moth (Hemileuca maia).
Many limacodid species feature stinging groups of spines 
that are paired and mounted on body protuberances attached to 
venom-producing cells.243 In some species (e.g., Doratifera spp.), 
these spines are retracted at rest and everted when the caterpillar 
is disturbed. Other examples of limacodid caterpillars include 
the saddleback caterpillar (Sibine stimulea) and rose caterpil-
lar (Parasa indetermina). Limacodid venom is rich in peptides, 
some of which have been shown to disrupt biological membranes 
similarly to venom toxins produced by hymenopteran insects.416
Envenomation by the American puss caterpillars Megalopyge 
crispata and Megalopyge opercularis (Megalopygidae; known 
colloquially as the “toxic toupees”) causes local and often 
intense burning pain, followed by localized erythema and 
rash.417 Systemic manifestations, including fever, headache, 
nausea, vomiting, shock, seizures, and anaphylactic reactions, 
are rare. Despite attempts to elucidate the venom composition 
of these species, the compounds responsible for the human 
envenomation syndrome remain unknown. However, non-GI 
body extracts revealed a high tannin content derived from 
their diet of oak leaves.418
Those species that produce toxin-bearing, urticating setae 
include many medically important species, including the pro-
cessionary thaumetopoein caterpillars responsible for induc-
ing equine amnionitis and fetal loss,419 and the pararama 
(Premolis semirufa), an arctiine caterpillar responsible for 
inducing the chronic inflammatory condition known as para-
phimosis in humans.420
Caterpillar sting treatment from most species is symptom-
atic and involves the removal of hairs using tape, washing of 
the skin, ice packs, and antihistamines, typically leading to 
the resolution of symptoms within hours.412 Lonomia-specific 
antivenom has been developed421 and is the standard treatment 
for Lonomia envenomations in Brazil.
15.5.9.2.5.3  Bees and wasps  Bees use their venom purely 
for self-defense or the defense of their colony, whereas wasps 
also use their venom to acquire prey.422 Most bees and wasps 
deliver a painful sting, consistent with a primary role in 
defense. The primary complication from envenomation by 
these hymenopterans stems from a combination of venom 
toxicity and allergenic toxins.423 However, most species live 
solitary lives and launch solo attacks when threatened, and 
consequently, they do not pose a serious threat to humans. 
A greater concern is posed by a small number of social bees 
and wasps such as Africanized honeybees (an Apis mellifera 
subspecies), hornets, and yellowjackets that aggressively 
swarm victims.424 While the venom of the Africanized hon-
eybee is no different from that of the European honeybee, 
the massive number of stings received by a victim during a 
swarm attack can cause severe toxicity and even death.425 
Thus, victims may die whether or not they are allergic to 
the venom. Death can occur with 20–200 wasp stings and 
150–1,000 bee stings.424
The venom-delivery system for all stinging hymenopterans 
is an anatomical adaptation of the ovipositor in females, with 
the venom gland connected to a channel that runs through the 
barbed stinger; thus, only females can sting. Following a sting, 
a wasp can usually remove its stinger and re-use it. In contrast, 
the honeybee stinger has a barbed anatomy and it remains 
embedded in the victim’s flesh, along with the attached venom 
gland reservoir, which continues to pulse venom into the vic-
tim until it is depleted or removed. Thus, sting use leads to the 
death of the bee.
The venom of bumblebees and honeybees is cross-reac-
tive, indicating similarities in venom composition.426 The 
injected dose of venom for a single bee is about 50 μg.398 The 
major component of bee venom, constituting 40%–60% of 
venom dry weight, is a small cytolytic and algogenic (pain-
inducing) peptide known as melittin. Additional venom 
components include acid phosphatase, hyaluronidase, pro-
teases, PLA2, apamin (an 18-residue peptide that inhibits 
calcium-activated potassium (KCa) channels), and mast-cell 
degranulating peptide.427,428 The allergenic effects of bee 
venom, including IgE-mediated anaphylaxis, are largely 
due to PLA2, hyaluronidase, and, to a lesser degree, melit-
tin.424,429 Wasp venoms are more variable in composition, 
but the major allergenic components in most wasp venoms 
appear to be “venom antigen 5”, PLA1, and hyaluronidase.429 
Envenomation to humans from either bees or wasps can 
trigger rapid acute immune reactions leading to vomiting, 
diarrhea, hypotension, coma, and in mass stings, systemic 
envenoming causing intravascular hemolysis, hemoglobin-
uria, and myoglobinuria progressing to secondary renal fail-
ure and rhabdomyolysis.430,431 However, moderate reactions 
to venom are much more common and involve pain, dermal 
rashes, urticaria, and edema.432

800
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
Envenomations by wasps in the family Mutillidae (~8,000 
species worldwide) are not life-threatening but they possess 
one of the most painful stings of all venomous animals. These 
wasps are nectar feeders that only use venom for self-defense. 
For one species (Dasymutilla occidentalis), the pain from a 
sting is so severe that they have been dubbed cow killers,433 
although in reality the sting is not strong enough to kill a cow! 
These solitary wasps are commonly referred to as velvet ants 
as the wingless females morphologically resemble an ant and 
they are covered in dense, brightly colored hair that gives them 
a velvety appearance.
The treatment of bee and wasp envenomations is largely 
symptomatic and supportive and requires a timely response in 
the case of anaphylaxis, with epinephrine, antihistamine, ste-
roids, and life support. Effective antivenoms are not available, 
but this therapeutic strategy has been investigated.423
15.5.9.3  Terrestrial Vertebrate Animal 
Toxins and Venoms
The terrestrial venomous and poisonous vertebrates have 
caused the human mind to conjure up the most dark-sided 
images of nature. In some cultures, snakes, lizards, frogs, sal-
amanders, toads, and newts have all been associated with the 
evil that exists in the natural world, while in other cultures, 
snakes, in particular, have been revered. The fact that many 
of these animals possess potent toxins has resulted in their 
biological and chemical traits being historically associated 
with mystical powers. Equally intriguing are terrestrial crea-
tures such as certain species of birds and mammals that are 
not usually thought of as sources of toxins. Modern analyti-
cal and technological capabilities allow a more comprehensive 
analysis of animal venoms and individual toxins and a better 
understanding of animal systematics. As a result of the inter-
face between these disciplines, our toxinological knowledge, 
and its value in biological science, conservation, and medicine, 
continues to grow.
15.5.9.4  Chordata
15.5.9.4.1  Reptilia
The ancient Greek historian Diodorus Siculus thought that 
the sands of the Theban desert in Egypt could spontaneously 
generate serpents.434 For millennia since ancient times, terres-
trial venomous snakes, such as cobras and rattlesnakes, have 
consistently maintained a prominent place in the hierarchy of 
many cultures, religions, and the realm of fascination in the 
human mind, even by those who fear them most. Less well 
known, from a global perspective, are venomous lizards such 
as the Beaded Lizard and Gila Monster, as they are only found 
in a small geographic region of the southwestern U.S., Mexico, 
and Guatemala.435 While arachnids account for a large num-
ber of human envenomations, and a considerable number of 
marine animals inflict envenomations as well, the majority of 
serious consequences from human envenomations are associ-
ated with snakes.
Historical studies of mortality from snake venom enven-
oming are limited, and the only global comprehensive review 
revealed an average of 50,000 deaths annually, based on 
hospital records from 1945 to 1949.436 The current number 
of venomous snake bite envenomations has been estimated 
to be 2.5 million, with an annual mortality of 125,000.437 
However, a community survey study in India indicated at 
least 45,000 snakebite-related deaths annually in that country 
alone, suggesting that global mortality from snakebite might 
exceed 200,000. Even so, most snakebite victims will sur-
vive, but many will suffer long-term morbidity, with an esti-
mated 400,000 amputations required annually. In addition, 
in the rural tropics where snakebite is most prevalent, it is an 
occupational disease of rural workers and a poverty trap; the 
costs of medical care and time away from work can amount 
to more than a full years’ income for families; with several 
families affected each year in a village, the entire community 
becomes impoverished over time. Thus, venomous snake bite 
is a medical concern of appreciable significance. The World 
Health Organization recently listed snakebite as a high-prior-
ity Neglected Tropical Disease, and it is working to mobilize 
global resources to tackle this major socioeconomic problem. 
This will require improvements in the quality and availability 
of antivenom, combined with improved training of healthcare 
workers and resourcing of health systems, particularly at the 
local or village level, where the risk is most severe.
Although the problem with snakebites is important, it 
should not overshadow the role that venomous snakes play in 
their natural ecosystems. Venomous snakes, using their venom 
for its primary biological function, also consume millions of 
pests that invade human food supplies in many countries of 
the world. Nevertheless, venomous snakes have the potential 
to become pests in their own right, threatening natural envi-
ronments into which they have been accidently introduced. A 
prime example is the introduction of the colubrid brown tree 
snake, Boiga irregularis, onto the Pacific island of Guam dur-
ing WWII, where it has since decimated the native fauna and 
is impacting human habitation.
15.5.9.4.2  Snakes
According to the Reptile Database (https://reptile-database.
reptarium.cz/), there are approximately ~3,900 species of 
extant snakes in 520 genera. Venomous snakes are found in 
arid, subtropical, tropical, and temperate regions, ranging up 
to polar ecosystems on six of the Earth’s continents, exclud-
ing Antarctica.438 They inhabit arboreal, aquatic, desert, 
mountain, subterranean, and diverse terrestrial microhabitats. 
Snakes are the most common predator of other vertebrates. 
Most venomous snake species feed on other reptiles, amphib-
ians, birds, and mammals, and their venom is often selective 
for their preferred prey.439,440
The classification of venomous snakes is a constantly evolv-
ing taxonomical process. They are generally divided into 
four main groups, namely the superfamilies Colubroidea, 
Elapoidea, Homalopsoidea, and Viperoidea. Within these 
groups are numerous families and subfamilies, only some of 
which contain venomous species and only a portion of which 
can inflict medically significant envenoming. The superfam-
ily Colubroidea contains the medically significant families 
Colubridae (which includes several groups of medically impor-
tant non-front-fanged venomous snakes such as the genera

801
Plant and Animal Toxins
Dispholidus, Thelotornis, and Boiga in subfamily Colubrinae, 
and Philodryas, Clelia, and Hydrodynastes in subfamily 
Xenodontinae) and Natricidae (which includes the medically 
important Rhabdophis spp.). Superfamily Elapoidea con-
tains many medically important snakes, notably in the fami-
lies Elapidae (the front-fanged cobra family, which includes 
cobras, kraits, mambas, coral snakes, Australian elapids, and 
sea snakes) and Lamprophiidae (which includes the side-
fanged vipers of Africa and the Middle East in the subfamily 
Atractaspidinae). Superfamily Homalopsoidea includes several 
non-front-fanged venomous species, but none of major medi-
cal significance. Superfamily Viperoidea contains the family 
Viperidae (the folding front-fanged vipers, which account for 
many medically important species), subdivided into subfamily 
Viperinae, which includes the classic vipers, adders and asps, 
and subfamily Crotalinae (pit vipers) (Table 15.7).
Venomous snakes have evolved several anatomical fea-
tures with a variety of functions important to their survival. 
Biomanufacturing of venom is primarily accomplished via 
ophidian oral glands of two types, although a variety of subtle 
modifications exist. A salivary system modification, frequently 
referred to as Duvernoy’s gland, is the toxin-secreting (venom) 
structure of many non-front-fanged snakes and is a seromu-
cous cell, a secretory structure with a weakly defined lumen-
like duct, that in some species produces toxic proteinaceous 
substances441 which are not expelled under pressure. A super-
ficially similar structure is found in elapid snakes,439 but with 
a lumen and surrounding musculature allowing the venom 
to be ejected under pressure, clearly defined as a true venom 
gland. Asian coral snakes (Elapidae) have an elongated, com-
pressor muscle-enclosed venom gland,434 and some burrowing 
and night adders (Viperidae) have a venom gland approaching 
half their body length.439 Venom glands in viperid snakes are 
slightly more sophisticated as the musculature, derived from 
the jaws, encompasses a well-defined duct that extends to the 
base of the accessory gland that interfaces with the fang sheath 
and terminates at the proximal fang-tissue juncture.442
Fangs of venomous snakes generally are one of four basic 
dentition types: (1) Aglyphous (no defined fangs, typical 
of some colubrids and other non-front-fanged species); (2) 
Opisthoglyphous (enlarged teeth with a groove in the back, 
commonly in colubrid non-front-fanged (“rear fanged”) snakes
