and atractaspis); (3) Proteroglyphous (large anterior, fixed-
position fangs with deep frontal grooves that may be enclosed, 
such as cobras); and (4) Solenoglyphous (large tubular, cana-
liculated, fangs with a hinge-like action due to their attach-
ment to the highly flexible maxillary bone, such as observed 
in Gaboon vipers).439 Both the production of toxic saliva, or 
venom, and the delivery systems for these different dentitions 
are key to the acquisition of prey and defense against preda-
tors. A further variation is the development of fangs for spit-
ting, seen in some African and Asian cobras, where the venom 
exit point near the tip of the fang is anteriorly facing and the 
orifice evolved to allow a tight stream of venom to be expelled 
over a distance of 2–3 m, generally aimed at the eyes of the 
target and thought to have evolved as a defense against preda-
tors, possibly early hominids in Africa.
The venoms of terrestrial snakes (Table 15.7) possess 
activities that affect several physiologic systems in animals, 
including the nervous system, blood coagulation system, car-
diovascular system, musculoskeletal system, and renal sys-
tem.438 The net toxic effect, or effects, may be a result of direct 
activity on a specific molecular target within a single sys-
tem, but more usually results from the combination of effects 
caused by different toxins acting directly, or secondarily.
15.5.9.4.2.1  Atractaspidinae  Mole vipers, or burrowing 
adders, sometimes referred to as stiletto snakes, comprise 
14 genera in the fossorial- and subterranean-inhabiting sub-
family, Atractaspidinae. These snakes, which are not actu-
ally vipers, are small, dark, glossy scaled, with virtually no 
distinction between their head and neck, and they have small 
bead-like eyes.439 Native to Africa, the Arabian Peninsula, 
and Israel, they appear nonthreatening. However, their local 
African names, such as father of blackness and shroud bearer, 
reflect their potential danger to humans.439 When scared, 
the snake will form a buried-head-coil and release like an 
unwinding spring to deliver a slashing bite. Strangely, each of 
the four different dentition patterns is represented by at least 
one genus in this subfamily. Most species in the family are 
“rear-fanged”, but a notable exception is the front-side-fanged 
assembly of those in the genus Atractaspis. Fangs are usually 
grooved, connecting to an elongated venom gland, and biting 
is accomplished with little opening of the mouth, as a single 
fang will extend laterally outward from the side of the mouth 
to deliver venom by making a slashing-stab motion. The abil-
ity of these snakes to side-stab their fangs poses a unique risk 
to snake handlers as the usual practice of restraining a ven-
omous snake by firmly holding the neck immediately behind 
the head, applicable to other venomous snakes, is not safe 
with Atractaspididae snakes. The most significant toxins iso-
lated from the venom of these snakes are the sarafotoxins,443 
a group of cysteine-rich vasoconstrictor peptides that mimic 
mammalian endothelins and are capable of rapidly inducing 
cardiotoxic effects via vasoconstriction of the coronary vascu-
lature.444 As little as 2.5 μg of sarafotoxin from A. engaddensis 
venom is lethal to mice.443
Envenomation by Atractaspis snakes can be a serious event, 
and deaths, although not common, have occurred from bites by 
A. irregularis and A. microlepidota445 and probably other spe-
cies.446 Symptoms of envenomation usually involve pain and 
swelling, with vomiting, abdominal pain, dyspnea, profuse 
salivation, and necrosis,447 but more severe effects can include 
local necrosis, hypertension, cardiac arrhythmias, respiratory 
compromise, anaphylactic-like reactions, shock, heart failure, 
and cardiac arrest.446 First aid is the immobilization of the bit-
ten limb, keeping the patient as still as possible, and support-
ive care.446 There is no specific antivenom or antivenom that 
is known to provide paraspecific protection from the effects of 
envenomation; indeed, the use of antivenom is contraindicated as 
it will provide no benefit, yet cause potential harm.446 Treatment 
is supportive443 and for those with more severe envenoming, may 
require advanced wound care, surgical debridement, possibly 
amputation, and intensive care management of hypertension, 
cardiac and respiratory compromise and, as with all snakebites, 
ensuring tetanus immunization status is current.446
15.5.9.4.2.2 “Colubridae”—the Non-Front-Fanged Colubrid 
(NFFC) Snakes  Snakes previously contained in the vast 
family Colubridae, now split into multiple families and sub-
families, are highly diverse, comprising about 290 genera 
representing approximately 1,700 snake species. Those spe-
cies in this assemblage that produce some form of venom or 
toxic secretion account for greater than 50% of all colubroid 
and related species.439 The bites of a large number of NFFC 
snakes can cause varying degrees of toxicity, yet documenta-
tion of NFFC envenomation in humans has only been reported 
for about 50 species representing 30 genera448,449; however, 
depending on how toxicity is defined, there may be many 
more.435,449,450 However, both colubroid snakes that lack a spe-
cific fang structure (Aglyphous), and those with “rear-fanged” 
structures (Opisthoglyphous), have been documented to cause 
severe envenomation; even human fatalities have resulted 
from the bites of the NFFC South African snake Dispholidus 
typus (boomslang),451 Thelotornis capensis (twig snake),452 
the East Asian Rhabdophis tigrinus (yamakagashi),453 and the 
Argentine racer (Philodryas sp.).454 Bites by this large group of 
snakes has been extensively reviewed.449,455
Given the effects of these NFFC venoms, it is unfortunate 
that comparatively little is known about their venom composi-
tion. Early studies of salivary secretions, or venoms, from “col-
ubrids”, with or without enlarged postmaxillary teeth, revealed 
the presence of 7–10 proteins,456 while more recent electro-
phoretic analyses revealed 10–20 constituents, of 4–200-kDa 
molecular weight, with species-specific profiles, suggesting 
that the oral secretions of some colubrids are equally as com-
plex as many front-fanged venomous snakes.449,457 Enzymatic 
properties had not been associated with colubrid venom prop-
erties until recent investigations characterizing the venoms 
and oral secretions of several “colubrids”, including the keeled 
water snake (Amphiesma stolata), brown tree snake (B. irregu-
laris), and false water cobra (Hydrodynastes gigas), revealed 
the presence of proteolytic activity. Phosphodiesterase activ-
ity, although slight, is present in the venom of the western hog-
nose snake (Heterodon nasicus nasicus) and wandering garter 
snake (Thamnophis elegans vagrans), acetylcholinesterase is 
present in the venom of the brown tree snake (B. irregularis), 
and moderate-to-high levels of PLA2 are present in mangrove 
snakes (Boiga dendrophila) and lyre snakes (Trimorphodon 
biscutatus lambda), respectively.457

803
Plant and Animal Toxins
A variety of NFFC venoms, including those from the dog-
toothed cat snake (Boiga cynodon), mangrove snake, and 
the Egyptian cat snake (Telescopus dhara), demonstrated 
postsynaptic neuromuscular toxicity in avian skeletal-nerve 
muscle preparations.458 Studies of Rufous-beaked snake 
(Rhamphiophis oxyrhynchus) venom, a relatively small 
South African Lamprophiidae of the subfamily Psammophis, 
revealed a high protein fraction with PLA2 activity and post-
synaptic neurotoxicity in animal nerve-muscle preparations. 
The venom of this species induced hypotension followed by 
cardiovascular collapse in rodents, which would be effective in 
subduing prey such as the naked mole rat.459 The composition 
of NFFC snake venoms confirms their similarity in make-up 
to more venomous non-colubrid species, which undoubtedly 
relates to their effectiveness in the acquisition of preferred 
prey and raises concerns about their potential for causing 
severe toxicity in humans.
The bites of colubrid snakes demonstrate the extremes of 
toxic effects—most bites result in signs and symptoms con-
fined to the bitten area, whereas a few species cause pro-
nounced, life-threatening, coagulopathic effects.460 Brief 
details of envenomation to humans by many species of NFFC 
snakes are frequently all that is available, and it is impos-
sible to know if the symptoms observed represent a severe 
bite or those associated with a small amount of venom inef-
fectively delivered.435,449 However, a note of caution is evalu-
ating published information on alleged “envenoming” by 
“colubrid” and NFFC snakes; a significant number of reports 
of harmful effects do not meet rigorous standards for clini-
cal reporting and are thus difficult to evaluate as to accuracy 
and applicability, with the identity of culprit snakes and the 
actual nature of clinical effects uncertain.449 Indeed, it may 
be that some reported “venomous” effects are just examples 
of local inflammation secondary to injection of a foreign 
substance, as might occur, for instance, with a domestic ani-
mal or human bite. Thus, while some “colubrid” species may 
potentially harbor quite toxic venoms or oral secretions, in 
cases of envenomation, as with viper and elapid bites, the 
degree of envenomation is not always fully expressed, and 
the incidence of bites by some species so rare, that the true 
significance of “colubrid” and NFFC snake venom toxicity in 
humans is unknown.
Envenomations by the boomslang, twig snake, and red-
necked keelback can be severe and are known to have caused 
severe coagulopathic complications resulting in a delayed 
onset of extensive and prolonged superficial hemorrhaging 
that progressed to death.446,460 Their bites do not induce major 
local symptoms of envenomation, fang punctures do not bleed 
at the time of the bite, and their venoms are defibrinating, and 
activate prothrombin, making them procoagulant in effect, 
with anticoagulated blood resulting due to consumption.461–463 
Unfortunately, a species-specific antivenom only exists for 
treating boomslang envenomations, and despite similarities 
in blood coagulopathy effects observed with envenomation 
by other similar species such as Thelotornis capensis, there is 
no antivenom cross-reactivity.462 As such, supportive care and 
replacement of blood components may be the only potentially 
effective therapies available.463 There was a specific antivenom 
produced in Japan for Rhabdophis envenoming (currently not 
readily available, even in Japan), but it is likely these snakes 
cause medically significant coagulopathic envenoming in 
a number of East and South East Asian countries, currently 
poorly documented and likely ineffectively treated.
Other NFFC snakes known to be capable of causing adverse 
effects in humans, in some cases potentially severe and ascrib-
able to true envenoming, include selected members of the tree 
snake genus Boiga, notably the brown tree snake B. irregularis, 
some of the South American racers of the genus Philodryas, 
notably P. olfersii and P. patagoniensis, and the Central and 
South American racers of the genus Alsophis, specifically A. 
portoricensis.449
Not as potentially deadly as the genera previously men-
tioned, but of interest because of its wide range across the 
North American continent and frequent exposure to humans, 
is the genus Thamnophis, the aglyphic colubrid snakes com-
monly known as the garter snakes.464 In addition to possessing 
enlarged, grooved, post-maxillary teeth, the salivary secre-
tions of the common garter snake (Thamnophis sirtalis) are 
elaborated from mandibular glands and serve as evidence 
of a venom-delivery system.465 Several cases of speculative 
“envenomation” have resulted from bites by the garter snake 
species T. sirtalis466 and T. elegans vagrans.467 The victims 
of these bites suffered local swelling, edema, hemorrhagic 
vesicles, and ecchymosis, to the extent that they required hos-
pital admission.466,467 Systemic symptoms did not occur, but 
the clinical presentation was likened to that observed with 
pit viper envenoming.467 It remains uncertain if these effects 
were ascribable to envenoming.449 Thus, even colubrid species 
considered to be totally harmless are capable of causing some 
degree of at least local effects.
15.5.9.4.2.3  Elapidae  Elapid snakes are found on four con-
tinents (Table 15.7). This family is represented by about 63 
genera that contain approximately 272 species. The cobra, 
coral snake, mamba, and taipan are familiar and notorious 
members of this snake family.439 Ranging in size from a few 
cm to meters in length, elapids present an abundance of bril-
liant colors and color patterns, such as the coral snakes of the 
Micrurus and Micruroides genera, and they exhibit functional 
threatening anatomical adaptations, such as hooding in cobras 
(Naja spp.). All elapids are venomous and possess front-fanged 
proteroglyphous dentition.435
The elapid venom-delivery system consists of tubular or 
grooved front teeth that are usually located on the anterior of 
the maxilla, followed by conical solid teeth. In general, their 
fangs are shorter than vipers and the associated venom glands 
have a lesser storage capacity. Elapid venoms have been con-
sidered to be primarily neurotoxic in effect as their venoms 
contain nonenzymatic proteins, some enzymatic proteins, and 
cytotoxic components.468 However, this is an overly simplistic 
view as some elapid venoms contain cytolytic toxins that can 
cause extensive local tissue injury around the bite site (nota-
bly some cobras, particularly spitting species), while others 
contain potent myotoxins (some kraits and Australian ela-
pids) and even potent coagulopathic toxins, procoagulants or 
anticoagulants (Australian elapids), such that the patterns of 
envenoming caused by elapids overlap, in some regions, with 
the typical effects of viper envenoming.469–476 The presynaptic

804
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
neurotoxins are usually PLA2 isoforms that either inhibit or 
induce transmitter release from presynaptic neuromuscu-
lar junctions.477 The postsynaptic neurotoxins, which are 
classed as either short-chain (60–62 residues) or long-chain 
(66–74 residues) peptides, with a tertiary structure stabilized 
by 4–5 disulfide bridges, competitively block acetylcholine 
from binding to the acetylcholine receptor and cause a non-
depolarizing block.181 Elapid PLA2 toxins may induce phar-
macological effects independent of their enzymatic activity, 
and independent of other venom components such as is 
the case with notexin from Notechis scutatus (tiger snake) 
venom. Some PLA2s have to be complexed with other pro-
tein factors, forming subunits, to express their pharmacologic 
actions, as in the case of taipoxin from Oxyuranus sp. (tai-
pan) venom.477 The tiger snake presynaptic PLA2 neurotoxins 
such as notexin are examples of toxins evolved from enzyme 
precursors, which now show multiple activities and targets 
independent of the original enzymatic activity; in this case 
presynaptic neurotoxicity and, separately, myotoxicity.475,476 
The PLA2 enzymes exist in a wide variety of forms, and each 
enzyme may be highly specific in its action, as is the case 
with β-bungarotoxin from the venom of Bungarus multicinc-
tus (many banded Krait) that acts via a presynaptic effect on 
nAChR transmission, but not on adrenergic transmission.478 
Adding to the complexity of PLA2 enzyme functions is the 
fact that some PLA2 isoforms may express identical pharma-
cological effects, but via different mechanisms. Thus, gener-
alizing PLA2 venom activities for all elapid species would be 
erroneous.187
In contrast to neurotoxins, some elapid venoms induce 
hemotoxic effects by possessing procoagulant properties 
that enhance the conversion of prothrombin to thrombin. 
Australian elapid venoms contain toxins with this pharmaco-
logic action. For example, notecarin from the tiger snake (N. 
scutatus), oscutarin from the taipan (Oxyuranus scutellatus), 
and pseutarin from the brown snake (Pseudonaja textilis) are 
all potent procoagulant toxins.463,475 These varied venom phar-
macological properties make snakes of the family Elapidae 
some of the most dangerous in the world. Indeed, many of the 
most potent snake venoms (lowest LD50) are found amongst 
elapids, particularly Australian elapids.479,480
However, in assessing the potential danger of any venomous 
animal, including snakes, other factors beyond venom toxic-
ity, as typically measured by the lethal dose in rodents, need 
to be considered. Technically, the most dangerous snake on 
Earth based on its LD50 in rodents is the Australian inland tai-
pan, Oxyuranus microlepidotus; however, it is found in remote 
inland regions of Australia, has bitten few humans, and no 
fatalities have so far been recorded, in part because there is a 
readily available and effective antivenom and excellent aerial 
medical retrieval services. In contrast, snakes such as the saw 
scaled vipers, genus Echis, which range from West Africa to 
South Asia, and have a far less potent venom, are estimated 
to kill many thousands of humans annually, in part because 
most victims live in areas without rapid access to high-quality 
healthcare and appropriate antivenom. So, in practical terms, 
which of these two types of venomous snake is more danger-
ous? With this caveat in mind, we now discuss the more prom-
inent elapid genera.
Cobras (genera Naja, Hemachatus, and Pseudohaje) are ela-
pids that can attain a relatively large size (3 m), making their 
ability to inject large quantities of venom a concern to humans. 
Their highly toxic venom is frequently used to paralyze other 
venomous snakes they seek for food.439 In addition to the tra-
ditional envenoming with fangs by biting, some species of 
cobras (N. mossambica, N. nigricollis, and others in Africa, 
and N. sputatrix and others in Asia) can spit or spray venom 
from an orifice in the front of their luminal fangs over a dis-
tance of 2–3 m.472 All cobras can dorsoventrally compress their 
necks to form a hood. The king cobra (Ophiophagus hannah) 
shares similar anatomy and venom properties to other cobras, 
but it is the world’s longest venomous snake, attaining a length 
up to 5.85 m.472,481
Cobra venoms contain cytotoxins and numerous PLA2 iso-
forms. The cytotoxins are basic proteins that alter membrane 
permeability and account for up to 40% of total venom pro-
tein.468 The cobra venom PLA2s are myotoxic and belong to 
the Group I PLA2s.482. Many of these toxins have been referred 
to as “cardiotoxins”, but this is misleading as primary car-
diotoxicity is not a feature of human envenoming; the term 
“three-fingered toxin”, which refers to the 3D structure of 
these toxins, is arguably more appropriate.
The clinical effect of cobra envenomation varies between 
species. Those causing predominantly neurotoxic paralytic 
effects may cause only minor effects at the bite site, while 
those causing predominantly local cytotoxic effects, including 
many spitting species, may cause minimal or minor neurotoxic 
effects.470,472 The cell-membrane-disrupting toxins in some 
cobra venoms frequently cause necrosis of the skin and sub-
cutaneous tissues. In particular, the venom of African spitting 
cobras, such as N. mossambica and N. nigricollis, can cause 
significant tissue damage, with neurotoxicity often minimal 
or minor.470,483 However, bites by some other cobra species 
such as Naja philippinensis in the Philippines and the African 
forest cobra, N. melanoleuca, cause minimal tissue damage 
and exhibit severe and rapidly developing descending flaccid 
paralytic neurotoxicity.470,484 Spitting cobras defensively eject 
their venom into the eyes of their predators, including humans, 
causing profound conjunctivitis, pain, photophobia, blepha-
ritis, and corneal opacification. These oculotoxic effects are 
believed to be related to the free/unbound “cardiotoxins” in 
the venom.485 Death following cobra envenomation can occur 
rapidly as a result of respiratory paralysis, which can start to 
develop in as little as 15 minutes. However, neurotoxic symp-
toms may also be delayed for several hours, causing a delay in 
seeking medical attention.
Antivenoms are available for treating envenomations by 
African and Asian species, and adequate dosing of the appro-
priate antivenom usually neutralizes the life-threatening 
symptoms.470,472,486 Additionally, the anticholinesterase drugs, 
edrophonium and neostigmine, have been successfully used 
in treating Philippine cobra (N. philippinensis)484 and mono-
cellate cobra (Naja kaouthia)487 envenomations, respectively. 
These drugs decrease the normal destruction of acetylcholine 
in the synaptic cleft of the neuromuscular junction, thereby 
making more neurotransmitter available to partially overcome 
the postsynaptic block caused by cobra neurotoxins binding to 
acetylcholine receptors. Such treatment should be considered

805
Plant and Animal Toxins
as adjunctive rather than replacing antivenom in treating cobra 
snakebite paralysis.476,488,489 The treatment of ocular exposure 
from the venom of spitting cobras has involved irrigation, and 
topical application of tetracycline solution and possibly adren-
aline for analgesia.488–491
Coral snakes, genera Micrurus and Micruroides, are native 
to the southern U.S., Mexico, and Central and South America, 
reaching their greatest diversity near the equatorial belt, and 
are relatively abundant compared with their Asian relatives 
(Calliophis spp.). The genus Micrurus is represented by nearly 
70 species, with a diversity of red, black, white, or yellow, 
ringed color patterns, and many mimic nonvenomous spe-
cies.435 These elapids are considerably smaller than cobras 
(<50 cm to 1.5 m), and their fangs are correspondingly smaller.
Coral snake venoms are capable of inducing cardiotoxicity, 
hemorrhagic toxicity, myotoxicity, and neurotoxicity, with the 
majority of Micrurus species possessing PLA2s with anticho-
linesterase and anticoagulant actions.492 Neurophysiological 
effects can result from irreversible neuromuscular blockade, 
reducing evoked acetylcholine release, but increasing sponta-
neous acetylcholine release.493
Coral snake envenomation gives rise to a bite with minimal 
pain in some cases, or significant local pain and sometimes 
local swelling, and symptoms of nausea and vomiting, dizzi-
ness, generalized weakness, drowsiness, euphoria, followed 
by muscle fasciculations, diaphoresis, and classic descend-
ing flaccid paralysis, with ptosis, partial or complete oph-
thalmoplegia, bulbar paralysis, diplopia, and slurred speech. 
Complete paralysis with the loss of respiratory function can 
last for several days up to 2 months.469 The neurological symp-
toms may not be immediately evident and are often delayed in 
their presentation for up to 12 hours.469,494
Treatment of coral snake envenomation is focused on the 
maintenance of respiratory function, and the use of antiven-
oms. In the absence of appropriate antivenom, it is possible to 
maintain life by providing artificial respiration for sustained 
periods until spontaneous breathing returns,495 but as paralysis 
can last up to 2 months, complications associated with artifi-
cial respiration may compromise survivability. Antivenoms 
for coral snake species in Central and South America are 
available and are effective, but many victims in these coun-
tries suffer symptoms without relief as they do not have timely 
access to a medical facility.486 An effective antivenom for 
North American coral snake bite is again available, after a 
period of constrained supply. Antivenom is most likely to be 
effective if given early, before major neurotoxic envenoming 
has developed. In the U.S., the recommended approach is to 
give antivenom to all patients with confirmed coral snake bite, 
as soon as possible, without waiting for evidence of neurotox-
icity, because once the latter develops antivenom is less likely 
to be effective.469
Mambas, genus Dendroaspis, are large (up to 4 m), agile 
African snakes with extremely toxic venoms. They come 
in grayish and green color morphologies, the black mamba 
(Dendroaspis polylepis) and the arboreal-inhabiting green 
mambas (D. angusticeps, D. jamesoni, D. viridis). The black 
mamba is named not for its body color, but for the charcoal 
colored interior of its mouth.496 They are proteroglyphic with 
enlarged maxillary front fangs that are moderately moveable, 
with no teeth posterior to the fangs, and these are associated 
with a well-developed venom gland.
Mamba venoms contain peptide toxins known as dendrotox-
ins that contain 57–60 amino acid residues and are stabilized 
by disulfide bridges. Dendrotoxins are presynaptic neurotox-
ins that stimulate acetylcholine release from axons in neuro-
muscular junctions. They selectively block certain subtypes of 
neuronal KV channels, with green (α-dendrotoxin) and black 
(toxin K) mamba venoms differing in the subtype of KV chan-
nels affected.497 Thus, via their action on KV channels, they 
facilitate neurotransmitter release at peripheral synapses, 
thereby repetitively inducing action potentials. The mamba 
toxin, fasciculin, is a potent inhibitor of acetylcholinesterase. 
Dendrotoxins and fasciculins work synergistically to produce 
rapid and severe paralytic neurotoxicity associated with mus-
cle fasciculation, a diagnostic feature of mamba envenoming. 
Mamba venoms also contain cardiotoxins498 and toxins that 
selectively act on different muscarinic acetylcholine receptor 
subtypes.499 Their venom is also high in hyaluronidase activity 
that is presumed to enhance venom spread.
Mambas can store from 4 to 8 mL per venom gland/fang, and 
they will bite repeatedly, thereby injecting a significant quan-
tity of venom. There may be only a tingling sensation at the site 
of envenomation, though bites can be painful, sometimes with 
swelling and, in the case of green mambas, local blistering and 
necrosis can occur. Systemic symptoms can appear rapidly 
(within 10 minutes) or be delayed. Symptoms are progressive 
and commonly consist of abdominal pain, unconsciousness, 
diaphoresis, flaccid paralysis, muscle fasciculation, respira-
tory paralysis, sustained hypotension, and terminal secondary 
cardiac collapse.470,500 The treatment of mamba envenomation 
victims with polyvalent antivenom (South African Vaccine 
Producers, Sandringham, South Africa) is effective, and the 
treatment of choice. Symptomatic and supportive treatment is 
important and should complement, but not be used in lieu of, 
antivenom therapy.470,500
Taipans are the quintessential venomous snakes of the ela-
pid family and are native to Australia and Papua New Guinea. 
These are sizable snakes (2–3.5 m) and similar to mambas in 
their alert, nervous behavior, being very aware of their envi-
ronment and any intruder. There are three species, the coastal 
taipan (Oxyuranus scutellatus), inland taipan (O. microlepi-
dotus), sometimes referred to as the fierce snake, believed to 
be the most venomous terrestrial snake in the world, and the 
western ranges taipan (O. temporalis). The average bite of the 
inland taipan delivers enough venom to kill >60 humans, while 
that of the coastal taipan is sufficient to kill 27 humans.496
Taipan venom contains neurotoxins and a prothrombin 
activator.501 The neurotoxin, taipoxin, is a trimeric PLA2 that 
inhibits the release of acetylcholine from the presynaptic cho-
linergic nerve terminal, causing severe descending flaccid 
paralysis. Postsynaptic PLA2 neurotoxins are also present in 
taipan venom.502 The procoagulants in the venom can cause 
severe defibrination coagulopathy that can lead to major sec-
ondary bleeding.476 Taipan venom can also cause myolysis, 
thought to be mediated by PLA2 toxins.
Envenomation by taipans results in the rapid development of 
severe coagulopathy and progressive neurotoxicity, presenting 
classically as ptosis, ophthalmoplegia, trismus, and paralysis

806
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
of bulbar, peripheral, and respiratory muscles.476,503 Antivenom 
is available and effective if given early after the bite; however, 
it is less efficacious in ameliorating neurotoxic symptoms after 
they are present because of the predominant presynaptic neu-
rotoxicity that causes intracellular damage to terminal axons 
at the neuromuscular junction, and respiratory support may be 
required for prolonged periods.475,504 The single most impor-
tant measure to assure survival following taipan envenomation 
is the rapid presentation to a medical facility with life support 
equipment.
Taipans are the dominant cause of life-threatening snake-
bite in parts of New Guinea, but they are a less frequent cause 
on mainland Australia. In Australia, the brown snake genus 
Pseudonaja accounts for most cases of snakebite and snake-
bite fatalities, the latter associated commonly with pre-hospital 
cardiac arrest. Brown snakes have a similar venom composi-
tion to taipans, but in humans it is the potent procoagulants 
that are most important, causing rapid defibrination and sec-
ondary effects, which can include AKI.475 Paralysis can occur 
but is rare. A specific (brown snake) and a polyvalent antive-
nom are available, the former being the treatment of choice.
The venom of the tiger snake, N. scutatus, and that of the 
closely related rough-scaled snake, Tropidechis carinatus, 
also have a potent mix of pre- and postsynaptic neurotoxins, 
myolysins, and procoagulants. Envenoming can cause signifi-
cant local pain, bruising, systemic defibrination coagulopathy, 
paralysis, and myolysis; the latter can be severe and life-
threatening with secondary kidney failure and hyperkalemic 
neurotoxicity.475 A specific/para-specific (tiger snake) and a 
polyvalent antivenom are available, the former being the treat-
ment of choice.
Also, in the tiger snake venom immunotype group are 
copperheads, Austrelaps spp., and broad-headed snakes, 
Hoplocephalus spp. The former cause predominantly neuro-
toxicity, while the latter cause procoagulant coagulopathy.475 
A para-specific (tiger snake) and a polyvalent antivenom are 
available, the former being the treatment of choice.
The genus Pseudechis contains a variety of species, includ-
ing Australia’s largest venomous snake, the mulga snake P. 
australis. Their venoms contain potent PLA2 toxins that are 
principally myotoxic, but the venom also contains antico-
agulants and neurotoxins, though the latter do not cause sig-
nificant neurotoxicity in humans. Bites cause significant local 
pain, sometimes extensive swelling, and, in some cases, anti-
coagulant coagulopathy and potentially severe-to-extreme 
systemic myolysis, with concomitant secondary effects.475 A 
specific (black snake) and a polyvalent antivenom are avail-
able, the former being the treatment of choice.
Death adders, Acanthophis spp., are an increasingly rare 
cause of snakebite in Australia, but still important in high-
land areas of New Guinea. Their venom contains both pre- 
and postsynaptic neurotoxins, myolysins, and anticoagulants, 
but in humans, the most common effect of envenoming is a 
progressive descending flaccid paralysis mediated, in most 
cases, by postsynaptic neurotoxins. Coagulopathy and myol-
ysis are rarely seen and usually minor. Bites cause local pain, 
but limited swelling.475 A specific (death adder) and a polyva-
lent antivenom are available, the former being the treatment 
of choice.
The New Guinea small-eyed snake, Micropechis ikaheka, is 
less well studied but can cause severe and lethal envenoming 
characterized by postsynaptic descending flaccid paralysis, 
myotoxicity, and anticoagulant coagulopathy.475 No specific 
antivenom is available, but Australian polyvalent antivenom 
is effective.
Kraits, genus Bungarus, are an important cause of snakebite 
across Asia, from Sri Lanka through to South East Asia. While 
their venom varies between species, there are many interspe-
cific commonalities. In particular, all species possess potent 
pre- and postsynaptic neurotoxins (β- and α-bungarotoxins, 
respectively) that cause descending flaccid paralysis, a hall-
mark feature of krait envenoming. Some species can also 
cause myolysis and hyponatremia, but they do not cause 
coagulopathy or bleeding and most bites are comparatively 
painless, though as envenoming develops, abdominal pain can 
occur. Kraits are principally nocturnal, and therefore most 
bites occur at night while the victim is asleep, an important 
diagnostic clue.472 Antivenom is available for several species.
15.5.9.4.2.4  Viperidae  Vipers and pit vipers, like the ela-
pids, are indigenous to four continents (absent from Australia), 
and represent two subfamilies, comprising about 30 genera 
that radiate to approximately 230 species (Table 15.7). The 
Viperidae have a prominent and well-developed venom-
delivery system, possessing advanced solenoglyphic dentition 
interfaced with a highly flexible maxillary bone, allowing 
the development of comparatively long fangs that are associ-
ated with sizable venom glands. As such, regardless of venom 
toxicity, they are capable of delivering a large quantity of 
venom deep into the tissues of their victims. The Viperidae 
are subdivided into two subfamilies based on the presence or 
absence of infra-red sensory facial pits that are used in sens-
ing prey. Those without heat-sensing pits (Viperinae) are Afro-
Asian (i.e., horned vipers, Cerastes spp.) and Eurasian genera 
(i.e., European adders, Vipera spp.) that are not found in the 
Americas or Australia. Viperids with the specialized heat-
sensing capability (Crotalinae, pit vipers) are found in both 
the Americas (i.e., rattlesnakes, Crotalus spp.) and Eurasia 
(i.e., Malayan pit viper, Calloselasma rhodostoma) but are 
absent in Africa and Australia (Table 15.7). Given their multi-
continental and latitudinal distribution (69°N to 47°S), they 
inhabit arboreal, semi-aquatic, and terrestrial ecosystems from 
tropical to temperate climates.439 The venoms of Crotalinae 
and Viperinae subfamilies, and their toxicological effects, are 
probably the most extensively studied of all snakes.
Viperidae venoms, although they possess some toxins simi-
lar to elapid toxins such as PLA2s, differ from those of ela-
pids in their clinical effects. In contrast to the neurological 
complications observed in most cases of elapid envenomation, 
viperid venoms primarily lead to venom-induced hemor-
rhagic effects. Exceptions to this basic concept are the neu-
rological effects caused by the venom of the South American 
rattlesnake (Crotalus durissus) and the Mojave rattlesnake 
(Crotalus scutulatus) in certain regions of the southwestern 
U.S. Asian viperid venoms, such as that from the Malayan pit 
viper, contain a serine protease that cleaves fibrinogen-A from 
fibrinogen, while the venom of the saw-scaled viper (Echis 
carinatus) contains a procoagulant prothrombin-activating

807
Plant and Animal Toxins
zinc metalloprotein, and the venom of the Russell’s viper 
(Daboia russelii) contains proteases that activate factor X in 
the blood clotting cascade.505 Viperidae, both Viperinae and 
Crotalinae, venoms alter blood coagulation, cause myonecro-
sis, and possess numerous enzymatic proteins that can impact 
nearly every organ system.506 Prothrombotic toxins in ven-
oms of the viper Bitis arietans (bitiscetin) and the pit vipers 
Bothrops jararaca (botrocetin) and Trimeresurus albolabris 
(alboaggregin) promote platelet aggregation. In contrast, 
similar toxins within the same C-type lectin and disintegrin 
families from Trimeresurus flavoviridis (flavocetin), Bitis ari-
etans (bitistatin), Gloydius halys blomhoffi (mamushigin), and 
Crotalus atrox (catrocollistatin) inhibit platelet function and 
demonstrate the common structure but the different function 
of some venom components across genera and different con-
tinents.507 The timber rattlesnake of the U.S., one of the most 
widely distributed species of Crotalinae, has a venom com-
ponent, crotalocytin, that causes rapid platelet aggregation.508
Viperidae venoms also contain myotoxins, which are 
expressed as PLA2 isoforms, with and without enzymatic 
activities. These are quantitatively important venom constitu-
ents that contribute to muscle necrosis.509 Within the pit vipers, 
zinc metalloproteinases have been isolated from the western 
diamondback rattlesnake, C. atrox (atrolysins), and from the 
eastern diamondback rattlesnake, Crotalus adamanteus (ada-
malysins); these metalloproteinases differ from most venom 
proteases in that they have hemorrhagic activity, and they 
are known as the snake venom metalloproteinases. L-amino 
acid oxidases are also common to most Viperidae venoms and 
serve as an aid in prey digestion.505
Envenomation by pit vipers or vipers usually results in 
complications with blood coagulation, tissue damage, and a 
redistribution of body fluids due to alterations in vascular per-
meability. The severity of envenomation varies considerably 
depending on how much venom was injected, and where the 
bite was sustained. Large quantities delivered intravenously 
represent a worst case scenario. Fortunately, most pit viper/
viper bites are fairly superficial and large quantities of venom 
are not injected, and sometimes no venom is injected at all.510 
Pronounced pain is almost always present following bites, and 
edema and ecchymosis are common. Envenomations by pit 
vipers and vipers can result in profound hypotension, incoagu-
lable blood due to venom anticoagulant effects, or as a result of 
consumption of blood coagulation components. Red cell mem-
branes may rupture, and delayed kidney damage may occur. In 
general, victims may exhibit a DIC-like syndrome.
Treatment of Viperidae envenomation usually involves 
the use of monovalent or polyvalent antivenoms, which are 
effective if administered within a reasonable time frame.448 
Antivenoms are available for the most prominent species 
of pit vipers and vipers.486 Supportive care and wound care 
are essential in the treatment of Viperidae envenomation, 
and necrotic lesions may be slow to heal. Morbidity from 
Viperidae envenomation frequently occurs when antivenom 
therapy is unavailable or an inadequate dose of antivenom has 
been administered.510
It is important to note that not all vipers cause the classical 
symptoms of local tissue damage and systemic coagulopathy 
and hemorrhagic syndrome, even within a single genus. The 
puff adder genus, Bitis, contains a number of species of great 
importance in Africa, particularly the classic puff adder, Bitis 
arietans. Most of these snakes cause local pain, extensive 
swelling, blistering, and bleeding around the bite site, with 
major fluid shifts into the bitten limb possible and consequent 
shock. However, the Berg adder, Bitis atropos, in addition 
to the local effects, but usually without coagulopathic/hem-
orrhagic effects, can cause progressive paralytic neurotoxic-
ity. In South America, the endemic rattlesnakes, Crotalus 
spp., unlike their North American relatives, often cause only 
minimal local effects, with envenoming being predominantly 
systemic, including coagulopathy, neurotoxic paralysis, and 
myolysis. This distinguishes their bites from those of other 
endemic pit vipers in South America such as Bothrops spp. and 
Lachesis spp., which usually cause marked local tissue injury, 
blistering and hemorrhagic problems, and systemic coagulopa-
thy, but without neurotoxicity. Such differences in envenoming 
profile have allowed the development of a syndromic approach 
to diagnosis and treatment of snakebite in several regions of 
Asia, Africa, and South America.470,472,474,488,489
15.5.9.4.3  Lizards
Heloderma, the only genus of lizards known to be dangerously 
venomous, comprises only two species, the beaded lizard 
(Heloderma horridum) and the Gila monster (Heloderma sus-
pectum). These two species are further divided into five sub-
species, H. suspectum suspectum, H. s. cinctum, H. horridum 
horridum, H. h. exasperum, and H. h. alvarezi.511 The Gila 
monster is native to the extreme southwestern U.S., and north-
western Mexico, while the range of the beaded lizard interfaces 
with that of H. suspectum at its southern extreme in Mexico 
but continues down along the Pacific coast to Guatemala.435 
These lizards are stout and have a solid body, legs, and tail, 
with a beady covering that is irregularly blotched with a pink/
yellow pattern on a black/brown background. The forked 
tongue of H. suspectum is black, in contrast to the pink forked 
tongue of H. horridum. The Gila monster prefers arid, rocky 
terrain and will even dig burrows or use burrows of another 
animal, while the beaded lizard inhabits forested areas and is 
frequently observed in trees during the rainy season.512 These 
lizards range in size from 35 to 70 cm, with the beaded lizard 
being the lengthier of the two.
Heloderma have short, slightly recurved, sharp, peg-like 
teeth that are grooved on both the anterior and posterior 
surfaces, and these grooved teeth are anchored to the maxil-
lary and mandible jaw bones. Although these lizards appear 
sluggish and somewhat uncoordinated when walking, as they 
appear to be waddling, they can rapidly flex their body and 
clamp their jaws around a victim. The delivery of venom by 
Heloderma stems from multilobed glands, lacking in muscu-
lature, that are located in the anterior portion of the lower 
jaw, on both sides, with each gland lobe independently having 
a duct that opens at the base of a tooth. When biting a vic-
tim, the muscle contraction of the jaw forces the venom from 
the ductal openings and channels it along tooth grooves, both 
upward and downward, with venom conduction via capillary 
action.
Biochemical studies of Heloderma venom have not been 
comprehensive but show that the two species have similar

808
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
venoms.513,514 The venoms contain a variety of enzymes, includ-
ing hyaluronidase, arginine hydrolase, PLA2,
515 and kalli-
krein-like proteases.513,514 One of the kallikrein-like enzymes 
capable of inducing hypotensive effects has been dubbed 
Helodermatine. Additionally, a toxin (Helothermine) that can 
lower body temperature in envenomated animals has been 
identified in beaded lizard venom.516,517 H. suspectum venom 
appears to be devoid of hemolytic and hemorrhagic proper-
ties,518 but vasoactive peptides and a vasodilating venom com-
ponent have been isolated.516
Although claims of death from Heloderma envenomation 
exist, their legitimacy has been questioned.511 Envenomation 
from Heloderma is quite painful, and much of the pain is a 
result of pure mechanical trauma. These lizards will hang on 
when they bite for extended periods of time without lessening 
their grip. Teeth may even be broken off when human victims 
try to remove the tightly attached lizard. Prominent symp-
toms, aside from intense pain, are edema, erythema, nausea 
and vomiting, dizziness, hypotension, leukocytosis, weakness, 
tachycardia, diaphoresis, and lymphangitis.519 Arteriospasm, 
with severe pain, of an envenomated digit may also occur.520 
Hypotension is a major concern and, in some cases, may be 
related to venom-induced anaphylaxis involving swelling 
of the tongue.291 A recent review and a severe case indicate 
that major risks from envenoming include angioedema, fluid 
loss associated with hypokalemia and metabolic acidosis, and 
cardiac disorders simulating ischemia.521,522 Another review 
reported patients with significant airway problems, some 
requiring direct intervention, but no fatalities in 105 cases.523 
Symptoms may persist for longer than 72 hours, and wound 
care is critical, as broken teeth may need to be removed, and 
secondary infection may ensue. Treatment of envenomation 
consists of supportive care and may involve fluids, antihista-
mines, and corticosteroids, with careful cleaning of the wound 
and antibiotics.519
15.5.9.5  Amphibia
In addition to the variety of freshwater and toxic marine ani-
mal life forms such as sedentary anemones, swimming cat-
fish, or drifting jellyfish, and the numerous semi-aquatic and 
terrestrial toxic animal life forms such serpentine-swimming 
sea snakes, walking lizards, and crawling land snakes, nature 
has filled another ecological niche with hopping amphibians. 
Amphibians may live in water or on land, or utilize both as 
adults, but usually have some stage of their life cycle that over-
laps between these two habitats. As a group, amphibians are 
prey for a variety of predators such as birds, fish, and snakes. 
Their limited modes of locomotion make it difficult for them 
to escape from predators, and consequently, they have evolved 
mechanisms for making themselves distasteful, or even poi-
sonous. Amphibians that have toxic bodies or produce toxic 
skin secretions (Table 15.8) mostly inhabit subtropical and 
tropical regions of the Earth, but a few are found in temperate 
regions.
Human interest in amphibian skin secretions began over 2 
millennia ago in China when powdered frog skins were used 
as heart stimulants and diuretics.243 Today, the quest for knowl-
edge concerning amphibian toxins and related biologically 
active compounds continues as evidenced by studies of TTX, 
one of the most widely distributed natural toxins. Present in a 
spectrum of unrelated taxa, such as bacteria, dinoflagellates, 
goby fish, hammerhead slugs, horseshoe crabs, octopus, and 
puffer fish, TTX is also found in several amphibian genera.524
Amphibian toxins are passively delivered, transdermally, 
when they are harassed or engulfed by predators. The skin 
of amphibians is a complex organ of interrelated biochemi-
cal, morphological, and physiological functions utilized for 
hydration, reproduction, respiration, thermoregulation, anti-
fungal and antimicrobial protection, and antipredator defense 
and is essential to amphibian survival.525 Toxins are generally 
released from specialized mucous-secreting, or granular, der-
mal glands distributed throughout the dorsal skin. However, 
in some cases, they are also present in specialized anatomical 
structures, like the bulbous parotoid gland located in the post-
maxillary region of some salamanders and toads.243 These 
are sites of synthesis for alkaloids, biogenic amines, peptides, 
proteins, and steroids.525 The primary consequence of toxin 
release is a distasteful adverse reaction in predators, but in 
some instances, ingestion of the toxin-containing amphibian 
may be lethal.
Frog, newt, and toad skin secretions, from several spe-
cies, representing several major amphibian genera, contain 
TTX.526 The frog genera Brachycephalus, Colostethus, and 
Polypedates,527 the toad genus Atelopus,526 and the newt gen-
era Cynops and Taricha528 all contain at least one species that 
contains TTX in its body. The origin of TTX in amphibians, 
and other animals, has been a topic of great interest, and there 
is considerable evidence that it is bioaccumulated from the 
diet or via a symbiotic relationship with a TTX-producing 
bacterium.529 However, recent studies suggest that the rough-
skinned newt Taricha granulosa produces TTX endogenously 
in granular skin glands, in high concentration, independent 
of bacterial symbiosis.530 A range of TTX analogs, includ-
ing 11-oxoTTX (which is four to five-fold more toxic than 
TTX), 
4-epiTTX, 
4,9-anhydroTTX, 
11-norTTX-6(S)-ol, 
5-deoxyTTX, 11-deoxyTTX, 11-oxoTTX, and 6-epiTTX

have been isolated from skin extracts of the Brazilian frogs

The toxicity of TTX results from its ability to specifically, 
and irreversibly block, presynaptic NaV channels, resulting in 
the abolition of action potentials at very low toxin concentra-
tions (μg/kg).532 This causes varying degrees of paralysis in 
animals, although some predators such as the common garter 
snake (T. sirtalis) appear to be resistant to TTX.533 TTX toxic-
ity to humans results from ingestion of significant quantities 
of TTX-containing animals. Human fatality resulting from 
cardiopulmonary arrest has been reported to occur within 
24 hours of ingesting a rough-skin newt.534
15.5.9.5.1  Frogs and Toads
Frogs and toads of several genera (Table 15.8) from particular 
bufonid, dendrobatid, mantellid, and myobatrachid anuran lin-
eages have yielded over 500 lipophilic alkaloids, representing 
22 different structural classes.535 These alkaloids include sev-
eral unique toxins that appear to be present in dermal secre-
tions, not due to de novo synthesis, but rather bioaccumulation 
from the arthropod diet of certain frogs. Myrmicine and formi-
cine ants appear to be a primary source of alkaloid sequestra-
tion in several brilliantly colored Central and South American 
dendrobatid poison dart frogs.536,537 The poison dart frogs 
best known for producing potent alkaloid toxins are found 
in the genera Allobates, Dendrobates, Epipedobates, and 
Phyllobates. Toxic alkaloids present in these genera include 
the neurotoxic batrachotoxins (BTXs), which cause irrevers-
ible opening of NaV channels, histrionicotoxins and epibati-
dine, which are nAChR agonists, and cardiotonic/myotonic 
pumiliotoxins (PTXs) and allopumiliotoxins.538 Additional 
skin alkaloid toxins include coccinellines, cyclopentaquinoli-
zidines, decahydroquinolines, tricyclic gephrytoxins, indoli-
zidines, piperidines, pseudophrynamines, and pyrrolidines.539 
On the other side of the ocean from these dendrobatid frogs 
are several varieties of frogs from Thailand that have toxic 
skin secretions. Skin extracts of the ranid frogs, Rana rani-
ceps, R. signata, and R. hosei, along with the rhacophorid frog, 
Polypedates leucomystax, cause locomotor difficulties and 
prostration when injected into mice, while a skin extract from 
the fanged frog Limnonectes kuhlii contains PTXs. Virtually 
nothing is known about the potential toxic effects of these 
extracts to humans.540
Toads of the Americas and Asia are also noted for their nox-
ious secretions. Note that the term “toad” is a common name, 
with no taxonomic status, given to certain frogs, especially 
in the family Bufonidae, that are characterized by leathery 
skin, short legs, and large bumps covering the parotoid glands. 
Bufotenin 
(5-hydroxydimethyltryptamine, 
5-HO-DMT), 
which is an analog of serotonin (5-hydroxytryptamine, 5-HT), 
is found in all toads. 5-HO-DMT can be methylated to produce 
bufotenidine and 5-methoxydimethyltryptamine (5-MeO-
DMT), although amongst amphibians the latter is only found 
in the Sonoran Desert toad Bufo alvarius.541 Methylated trypt-
amine analogs are known to have psychedelic effects, and 
these compounds are believed to be responsible for the hal-
lucinations in humans following licking of toads or smoking 
toad extracts.542
The toad genus Bufo also produces bufadienolides, highly 
toxic cardiac glycosides that inhibit the sodium–potassium 
ATPase pump,543 and which can cause atrioventricular block, 
bradycardia, and potentially fatal ventricular tachycardia. 
Small companion animals have died as a result of toad inges-
tion,544 and the introduction of the cane toad, Bufo marinus, 
to Australia has resulted in the death of many native spe-
cies attempting predation on the toads, thereby causing sig-
nificant ecological damage.545 The cardiotoxic effects of toad 
ingestion have been effectively treated with digoxin-specific 
antibodies.546
Further demonstrating the diversity of toad toxins has been 
the isolation of morphine-like toxins from B. marinus skin 
extract,547 and a sleep-inducing factor from a skin extract of 
the Indian toad, Bufo melanostictus548; males of this species 
possess a Bidder’s organ, located near the kidney, that pro-
duces a cardiotoxin capable of inducing cardioneuromuscular 
blockade in animals.549
15.5.9.5.2  Newts and Salamanders
Newts are similar to salamanders in appearance except that 
their skin has a dryer, less clammy, or slimy feel. Toxic species 
are native to the east and west coasts of the U.S. and Japan. 
The primary toxin found in salamanders is TTX, as discussed 
earlier.
Salamanders of European terrestrial forested habitats 
may be bright orange and black bicolored, or solid black, 
and have a wet sheen appearance. The European fire sala-
mander (Salamandra salamandra terrestris) and the Alpine 
salamander (Salamandra atra atra), like their dendrobatid 
amphibian relatives, secrete toxic alkaloids from their skin. 
Samamandarine and samamanderone are the steroidal alka-
loids of prominence, but cycloneosamandione, samamandi-
nine, samanine, samamandaridine, and samandenone may 
also be present.550 These are possibly cholesterol-derived ste-
roidal alkaloids, and they are synthesized by the salamanders 
rather than taken up in the diet or acquired through bacterial 
symbiosis. Detailed pharmacologic actions of these salaman-
der alkaloids are lacking, but they have nerve-blocking activ-
ity in both the central and PNSs.551 Although these toxins may 
serve as deterrents to predators, their effects on humans are 
unknown.
15.5.10  Marine Animals: Poisonous, 
Toxin Bioaccumulated
Poisonous (as opposed to venomous) marine animals do not 
synthesize the toxins they carry in their bodies and organ sys-
tems but rather accumulate the toxins from their diet or the 
environment. Thus, the toxins are produced by other organ-
isms that live in the surrounding marine environment. A vari-
ety of marine animals that are commonly eaten by humans 
may become toxin-bearing and cause severe risk to human 
health following their consumption (Table 15.9). For exam-
ple, one of the most well-known types of seafood poisoning, 
which manifests as neuro- and cardiopulmonary toxicity, and 
potentially death, results from eating puffer fish. Prepared in

810
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
gourmet restaurants, the pufferfish delicacy known as fugu, if 
improperly prepared, may lead to severe TTX poisoning in the 
innocent diner.552
Poisonous marine animals most commonly acquire 
their toxins from dinoflagellates (toxic microalgae) such as 
Alexandrium spp., Gymnodinium catenatum, Pyrodinium 
bahamense (all associated with paralytic shellfish poisoning 
and red tides),553 Dinophysis spp. (diarrhetic shellfish poi-
soning),554 Nitzschia spp. (amnesic shellfish poisoning), and 
Gymnodinium brevis (neurotoxic shellfish poisoning).555 Not 
surprisingly, the presence of dinoflagellate toxins in marine 
animals can fluctuate with seasonal climate changes that trig-
ger algal blooms. Additionally, some toxins form, and accu-
mulate, in fish as a result of spoilage; for example, scombroid 
food poisoning, which results from eating spoiled fish, is due 
to the high levels of histamine released from damaged cells.556
The forms of marine poisoning with most impact on 
humans, either directly through being poisoned, or indirectly 
as economic impact when fisheries are closed, are TTX, cigua-
tera, and shellfish poisoning. The latter has multiple different 
types, each with its own group of toxins and distinct clini-
cal effects. Ciguatoxin poisoning has a huge impact in some 
Pacific Island communities where up to 20% of the population 
may be poisoned in any given year because at-risk larger fish 
are an essential staple in the diet.557
There are no simple rules for treating victims of marine 
food poisoning, and symptomatic and supportive care, includ-
ing life-support, may be essential for a successful outcome. 
Some types of marine food poisoning may cause long-term 
morbidity.
15.5.11  Animal Toxins and Poisons 
from Unusual Sources
The amazing thing about animal toxins is that in addition to 
being present in commonplace animals such as snakes, jel-
lyfish, scorpions, and spiders, toxins and poisons show up 
in the most unlikely of creatures (Table 15.10). The pitohui, 
a unique poisonous bird from Papua New Guinea, contains 
neurotoxic alkaloids in its feathers to deter ectoparasites and 
predators, and these are similar to the BTXs found in Central 
and South American poison dart frogs.558 Ingestion of polar 
bear liver can cause serious vitamin A toxicity.185 Sea turtles, 
like many poisonous fish, can accumulate algal toxins and 
cause death when their meat is consumed.243 A small num-
ber of mammals are venomous, including platypus, shrews, 
solenodons, slow lorises, and vampire bats.559 Unsurprisingly, 
vampire bats support their hematophagic lifestyle by secret-
ing an anticoagulant protein toxin in their saliva known as 
draculin, which is a noncompetitive inhibitor of Factor Xa, a 
component of the coagulation cascade.560 Shrew venom can 
induce dyspnea, hypotension, and paralysis in mammals561 and 
immobilize insect prey.559 Even the most unusual of mammals, 
the egg-laying platypus, has a toxin-secreting crural venom 
gland, connected by a duct to an erectile keratinous spur, just 
above the webbed foot on each hind leg. Only the males pos-
sess this venom apparatus, as the venom is thought to be used 
to deter conspecific males during the breeding season. If skin 
penetration occurs, the venom, which is a complex mixture of 
enzymes and small peptides,562 can cause severe pain, edema, 
and hyperalgesia.563

15.5.12  Beneficial Uses of Naturally 
Occurring Toxins
The last half-century has seen enormous advances in our 
knowledge about poisonous and venomous animals, and in 
many instances, this has led to the development of better treat-
ments for humans that have been envenomated or become the 
victim of animal poisoning. Perhaps more surprisingly, this 
knowledge has also led to the development of several human 
therapeutics that are either exact replicas or mimetics of natu-
ral toxins.
Animal toxins, whether endogenously produced or purpose-
fully acquired from the environment or a symbiont, usually 
serve a key role in predation or defense. Since humans are not 
the natural prey or predator of any venomous or poisonous ani-
mal, many animal toxins that have toxic effects on prey and/or 
predators are harmless to humans and may in fact produce a 
beneficial effect. A classic example is the peptide ω-conotoxin 
MVIIA found in the venom of the fish-hunting marine cone 
snail C. magus. In its natural context, ω-conotoxin MVIIA 
is used to paralyze fish prey by inhibiting presynaptic CaV2.2 
channels, and thus neurotransmitter release, at fish neuromus-
cular junctions. Humans also contain CaV2.2 channels but they 
are not found at neuromuscular junctions; instead, they are 
located primarily in specialized nerve fibers in the spinal cord 
that are used to propagate pain signals to processing centers in 
the brain.564 Thus, in humans, ω-conotoxin MVIIA is an anal-
gesic. Ziconotide, a synthetic version of ω-conotoxin MVIIA, 
was approved by the FDA in 2004 for treating patients with 
intractable chronic pain; it is administered by intrathecal infu-
sion in order to target CaV2.2 channels in the spinal cord.201
Even deadly venomous animals can provide a source of 
human drugs, as exemplified by the development of a very suc-
cessful class of human therapeutics from a toxin found in the 
venom of the lethal Brazilian viper Bothrops jararaca. The 
clinical observation that patients envenomated by Bothrops 
jararaca suffer from hypotension and severe intestinal con-
tractions led to the isolation of a nine-residue venom pep-
tide (teprotide) that potently inhibits angiotensin-converting 
enzyme (ACE). ACE converts angiotensin I to the vasocon-
strictive hormone angiotensin II and degrades the vasodilatory 
peptide bradykinin. Thus, ACE inhibitors lower blood pres-
sure. Detailed structure-activity relationship studies of tep-
rotide ultimately led to the development of a smaller, orally 
active peptidomimetic known as captopril (D-2-methyl-3-
mercaptopropanoyl-L-proline). Captopril was approved by the 
FDA for the treatment of hypertension in 1981, and it became 
a blockbuster drug, with peak annual sales of more than USD 
1.6 billion.565
To date, seven human therapeutics have been derived from 
animal venoms (Table 15.11), including an anti-diabetic drug 
(exenatide) from the venom of the Gila monster (Heloderma 
suspectum) and an anticoagulant (bivalirudin) from the 
medicinal leech (Hirudo medicinalis).201,566 Several other 
venom compounds are currently in clinical trials, including 
ShK (dalazatide), a 35-residue peptide toxin from the venom 
of the sea anemone Stichodactyla helianthus, which is being 
developed to treat autoimmune diseases,567 and chlorotoxin 
(tozuleristide), a 36-residue CSα/β toxin from the venom of 
the deathstalker scorpion (L. quinquestriatus), which is being 
developed for intraoperative imaging of brain tumors.568 Even 
the ubiquitous TTX, which is found in both venomous and poi-
sonous animals, is being examined as a potential analgesic.569
Thus, it is clear that although some venomous and poison-
ous animals can cause harm to humans, they have nevertheless 
developed impressive pharmacological arsenals that provide 
a wealth of opportunities for the development of beneficial 
human therapeutics.
15.6  Summary
Paracelsus is famously quoted as saying that all substances are 
poisonous, only the dose differentiates between a poison and 
a remedy. The same can be said about toxins found in human 
food. The consumption of too much of any food, together with 
its natural toxins, can be harmful. In this chapter, we have 
covered some of the more visible natural toxins. However, the 
natural toxins discussed in this chapter are only a few in com-
parison to what is found in Nature and has been reported in 
the literature.
